# Good Room

# Chapter 1

Doug Bridge woke at 11:40 on a Sunday morning in February still wearing his suit pants and one dress shoe. The left one. His right foot was bare and cold where it hung off the edge of the mattress, and the sheets were kicked into a tight knot at the bottom of the bed.

The apartment was cold. He'd left the bedroom window cracked — he could hear the latch ticking against the frame in the draft — and the baseboard heater was doing what it always did, which was run at full volume and accomplish almost nothing. The ceiling above him was the same off-white it had been for twelve years. A water stain near the light fixture that he'd been meaning to assess had spread another inch since the fall, creeping toward the smoke detector with the patience of something that knew it had time. He noted this the way he noted all damage — automatically, with an estimate forming before the observation was even finished. Moderate. Cosmetic. No structural concern unless the source was active.

His head was not a headache. It was a condition. A full-body disagreement with being awake that started behind his eyes and traveled down through his neck and into his stomach, where something was sitting that he did not intend to investigate. His mouth tasted of pennies and carpet. The suit jacket was on the bathroom floor — he could see it through the open door, crumpled against the base of the toilet, one sleeve folded under it, nowhere near the hamper.

He sat up. The room tilted, then caught itself.

His phone was not on the nightstand. It was not on the floor beside the bed. It was not in his pants pocket, which he checked with the same methodical pats he gave lost paperwork. He found it in the bathroom sink, face-down against the drain stopper, resting there with a degree of care the rest of the evening apparently hadn't earned. The screen was still on. He picked it up.

Forty-seven missed calls. Two hundred and fourteen text messages. Notifications from apps he hadn't opened in months — Instagram, which he'd downloaded for Kelsey's engagement photos and never deleted, and Twitter, which he didn't use but which was now informing him he'd been mentioned six hundred and eleven times. His email had thirty-one new messages. The most recent was from someone at a company called Meridian Talent Group. The subject line said: "RE: Representation Inquiry — URGENT."

Doug set the phone on the edge of the sink and looked at himself in the mirror. His tie was gone. His shirt was buttoned wrong — one off from the second buttonhole down, so the collar sat wrong and the whole shirt looked misassembled. His hair, which had been thinning at the crown since roughly 2019 in a way that didn't qualify as bald but had stopped qualifying as anything else either, was pressed flat on one side. His eyes were red and reduced, and the skin beneath them had a texture he associated with water-damaged drywall — puffy, discolored, unlikely to improve without intervention.

He turned on the faucet and held his hands under cold water until his fingers ached. Then he unbuttoned the shirt, pulled off the suit pants and the one shoe, and stood in his boxers in the bathroom while the water ran and the latch ticked at the window and the heater groaned in the wall.

Something had happened at the reception.

He had fragments. He remembered the ceremony — Kelsey in the dress, the officiant who kept saying "sacred bond" in a tone that suggested a woman reading a lease she hadn't reviewed, Tyler standing at the altar with the pleasant composure of a man following instructions exactly. He remembered the cocktail hour. He remembered the first whiskey, and the second, and the one after that. He remembered Brendan's toast. Brendan, standing at the head table in a suit that fit exactly right, holding his glass, talking about Kelsey in a tone of composed emotional authority that made everyone in the room feel something specific and made Doug feel specifically that he was being outperformed by a man who had known his daughter for eight years and was already better at speaking about her than he was. Brendan had cried. At exactly the right moment. One tear, left eye, wiped with his thumb, and the room had exhaled all at once, and Doug had signaled the bartender for his fifth whiskey. Or his sixth.

After that, the evening became a file with water damage — legible in patches, ruined in the middle, the critical section exactly where the stain was worst. He remembered standing up. He remembered a microphone, the weight of it in his hand, heavier than expected, metal-cold and official. He remembered faces. He did not remember what he'd said to them.

He picked up his phone and scrolled through the texts.

Roy Utley, 11:47 p.m.: *You alright?*

Roy, 11:48 p.m.: *Call me when you're up.*

Megan, 12:03 a.m.: *Doug, we should talk about last night. I'm not angry. Just want to discuss when you're ready.*

Megan, 12:04 a.m.: *Brendan wanted me to tell you he thought it was extraordinary. His word, not mine.*

Janet Morse, his supervisor, 8:15 a.m.: *Hi Doug, hope you're doing well. Quick question re: Hoffmeyer claim when you get a chance. Also saw the video. We should chat.*

Doug stopped. He read Janet's text again. *Saw the video.*

He found Kelsey's thread. Three messages, sent at intervals that suggested each one had been composed, deleted, recomposed.

Kelsey, 11:52 p.m.: *Dad.*

Kelsey, 11:54 p.m.: *Call me tomorrow.*

Kelsey, 12:30 a.m.: *I'm not mad. I just need you to call me.*

Doug sat on the edge of the bathtub. The porcelain was cold through his boxers and he could feel the grout line pressing into the back of his thigh. He typed a message to Kelsey. Two words, because he did not yet know what the correct number was.

*How bad.*

No question mark. It wasn't a question. The three dots appeared immediately — she'd been waiting, or she was already holding her phone, or she'd been holding it since midnight.

*Don't start digging around online. Leave it alone for now.*

Doug looked at his phone. He looked at the bathroom — the suit jacket on the tile, the one shoe beside the door, the water stain on the ceiling now directly above him and impossible to stop noticing. He looked at the 214 unread texts and the 47 missed calls and the notification from an app he'd never used telling him that 611 people had opinions about him.

He went on the internet.

---

The video was the third result when he typed his own name into Google, which was not a sentence that had been true about Doug Bridge at any prior point in his fifty-two years. The first result was a Reddit thread titled "This man just eulogized his own marriage at his daughter's wedding and I can't stop watching," with 18,000 upvotes. The second was a tweet with the words "THIS MAN" in capitals and a nine-second clip he was not yet ready to watch. The third was the full video, seven minutes and fourteen seconds, uploaded to YouTube by an account called KelseyAndTylerWedding, which told him nothing except that somebody had made an email address and decided this belonged to everyone.

Two million views. The video had been online for fourteen hours and two million people had watched it. Doug tried to conceptualize two million. It was more than the population of Columbus. It was roughly the combined enrollment of every insurance adjusting certification program in North America, though he wasn't sure why that was the comparison his brain offered, except that his brain was stocked with facts that had no social use, and this was one of them.

He pressed play.

The video opened on the Marriott ballroom in Worthington — the warm lighting, the white tablecloths, the centerpieces Kelsey and Megan had spent four months negotiating. The phone was tilted slightly to the left, and every time the person holding it shifted their grip the whole room lurched half an inch.

Doug watched himself stand up.

The man in the video was wearing the same suit that was now crumpled on the bathroom floor, except in the video the buttons were still aligned and the tie was still on. His face was the red of someone who has had eight whiskeys on a stomach that contained two crab cakes and a bread roll. He was not staggering — he was moving carefully, feet set down one by one, because some part of him knew he was drunk and did not trust the floor. His eyes were bright and loose and he was smiling in a way that Doug had never seen on his own face, a smile that could only happen once the mechanism connecting intention to expression had been disconnected.

He took the microphone from the stand. "So," he said.

His voice was louder than it should have been, and a woman off-camera laughed — not at anything yet, just at the single syllable landing in the room with more weight than one syllable was supposed to carry.

"Brendan gave a hell of a toast," video-Doug said. "He really did. I had something too. Not written down. That would've been smart. I had thoughts." He looked toward Brendan and nodded as though they were both involved in a smaller, more manageable conversation. "The sunset part. And the crying. That was a lot. Left eye first, by the way. Then the other one. You staggered it. Professional." He took a breath into the microphone. "I have not cried since, I think, 2012. Maybe 2011. It was on I-70. No, 71, because I was coming back from Dayton. It doesn't matter. I was driving and crying hard enough that I missed an exit and the man in the next lane kept looking over like he was deciding whether to call somebody. He didn't. Fair enough." The room laughed, uncertainly. Video-Doug frowned at the sound, as if he had not intended to produce it. "You should not be operating a vehicle in that condition," he said.

Doug, sitting on the edge of his bathtub in February, watched himself and did not recognize the man holding the microphone. That Doug was something the current Doug had never been — unguarded in a way that, on camera, came close to ease.

"So I'm Kelsey's father," the video continued. "For Tyler's side of the room, if we haven't met, I'm her dad. I work in claims. Not the doctor." A harder laugh this time, from more of the room. He pointed the microphone vaguely toward Brendan. "Brendan is the doctor. Orthopedic surgeon. Knees, shoulders, whatever else on a person quits doing what it was designed to do. I adjust insurance claims. Residential, mostly. If your water heater explodes, I come look at the wall and tell you how bad it is. If a tree falls on your garage, I take pictures and write numbers down. That's what I do." He spread his free hand in a gesture that seemed to take in himself, the room, the entirety of what he'd done with the years since Kelsey was born. "And Brendan is, if we're using insurance language, which I am because that's what I've got, not like-kind replacement. This is not the same quality. This is an upgrade." The laugh that met this arrived in pieces and died before it settled. The phone jerked toward the head table just long enough for Doug to see Megan set her glass down, Brendan stop smiling, and Tyler turn toward Kelsey instead of Doug. "That sounds hostile. I hear it. I mean it as math. But we're not here for that. We're here for Kelsey."

He didn't talk about Kelsey. Not yet. He reached across the table and picked up someone's wine glass and drank from it. The person whose wine it was — an older woman, one of Megan's aunts maybe — made a reflexive move to take it back, saw that it was already at his mouth, and let her hand hover there in the air for a second before she withdrew it to her lap.

"Megan and I were married for eighteen years," video-Doug said. "Eighteen exactly. I checked that during the divorce because once lawyers are involved you start measuring your life in exact units, which is not healthy, but there it is." He looked down into the wine glass as if it might contain the next sentence. "In insurance, after eighteen years, you're talking about actual cash value. Depreciation. Not replacement cost. That's not — marriage isn't a refrigerator. I know that. What I mean is the numbers get ugly fast and then people start fighting over the furniture because the furniture at least can be priced." The phone drifted again. Megan was sitting very straight now, one hand flat on the linen. At Tyler's parents' table, Tyler's mother had set down her fork and did not pick it back up. "Megan came out ahead on that deal. She should have. She's smart. She made the correct decision." Off-camera, Megan said his name once. The microphone caught it anyway. Doug took another drink. "If I had inspected me in year eighteen, I would've said stop sinking money into it." The pause this time stretched longer, and the room went quiet in a way that felt different from before — not amused-quiet but held-breath-quiet. "That sounds bitter. It is a little bitter. It is also true."

He took another drink of the wine that wasn't his. "And Brendan's a good man. He is. I tried, for a while, to dislike him on principle. Eighteen months maybe. That might be too precise. But I gave it a real effort." His voice rose and then flattened again. "And I couldn't do it because he's decent. He is decent to Kelsey and decent to Megan and he cooks and he gave that toast, and if he were an asshole this would be simpler. I would at least know where to stand." He looked down at the microphone. "At work — at work every situation has a form. Water damage, fire, theft, acts of God, which is a real category. But this, there is not a — I don't know what you file for this."

Doug, on the bathtub, heard himself say "asshole" in a room that contained Megan's parents, Tyler's mother, Kelsey's coworkers from the dental practice, and a woman named Diane from Megan's book club who had once told Doug he seemed "very capable" in a tone that clearly meant something else entirely. The sound that came out of Doug's mouth in the bathroom was quiet and involuntary and was the word "shit," spoken to no one.

The video kept going.

"But the thing I was trying to say is not about Brendan." He was pointing now — at nothing, at the room, at something only he could see. "It is about the house. No. The table in the house. Kelsey — are you there? Of course you're there." His eyes found a point off-camera and held there. His voice dropped, not to a whisper but to something closer to the register people use when they've forgotten they're being listened to.

"The kitchen table on Briarwood. You sat at one end doing your math homework and I sat at the other end filling out proof-of-loss forms. You had that TI-84 calculator. The expensive one. Or maybe they were all expensive. I do not know. You hit the buttons so hard I kept thinking you were going to crack the screen." He stopped and swallowed. The room had gone silent in a way that was different from every other silence in the video — not uncomfortable, not amused, but the silence of people who had been suddenly and without warning shown something private. "We'd sit there for two hours. Sometimes three. We didn't talk much. You'd look up and ask me how to spell 'necessary' and sometimes 'February,' and I got 'February' wrong almost every time, and you'd laugh at me for it." He was still holding the microphone but he'd lowered it slightly, and his voice was quieter on the recording now. "That was the best part. For me, I mean. The furnace running and your pencil on the paper and that calculator clicking and me not saying anything because I didn't need to. That was — that was it." The phone image shook once and drifted toward the head table. Kelsey had her napkin bunched in one fist. Tyler had turned toward her completely. Brendan, visible beyond them, had gone motionless.

"When your mother and I split up, I should've taken that table." He rubbed his forehead with the heel of his hand. "It was maybe eighty dollars at Sears. Seventy-nine ninety-nine, maybe. I fought about the 401k. I fought about the Civic. I fought about a lot of things I could put numbers next to, and I left the table there because it was just a table, except it wasn't just a table and I knew that even then." His voice stopped. Not a sob — a mechanical failure, the voice quitting because the thing behind it was larger than the aperture. He stood there for four seconds that, on the recording, felt like a weather event. Then he cleared his throat. "I can fight for things with paperwork. I know how to do that. The other things I — I notice those after. A long time after, usually."

Doug paused the video. He sat in the bathroom with the phone in his lap and the faucet dripping — he'd left it running — each drop hitting the basin with a sound he could feel in the bones of his face. His hands were resting on his thighs and they were shaking, not from anything he could name but from the specific physiological event of hearing yourself say the truest thing you've ever said and not remembering the decision to say it.

He pressed play.

The last ninety seconds were worse. Video-Doug had broken through something and couldn't get back to the shape of a toast. He tried Tyler first. "Tyler seems like a good guy," he said. "He laughed at the 401k thing. Or he did the polite version of laughing. Either one, thank you, Tyler." Tyler, already halfway out of his chair now, gave a tight nod Doug would not have noticed the first time. Then he looked at the ceiling as if the next topic might be up there. "I'm fifty-two. I live alone. I eat cereal for dinner a lot. At the counter. It feels better there. I don't know why." One laugh came from somewhere near the back and died alone. "And cereal costs too much now. I do resent that." He dragged the microphone wire with his shoe, noticed it, and gave up on correcting it. "And Brendan, the sunset part. I am still on that. Did you practice it? You can tell me later. No, tell me now if you want. I really would like to know."

From off-camera, Kelsey said, "Dad."

Not loud. Worse than loud. Flat and immediate, the tone people use when a child is about to touch a stove.

"Kels." His voice was almost normal now, which was worse than when it had broken. "You turned out so good. Better than — no, that's not what I mean. You are good. You're really good, and I know I don't say that enough. I don't think I say it. I don't think I have said it." The failure again, longer this time. He stood there with the microphone in one hand and someone's stolen wine in the other and the room full of people waiting for him to finish a sentence he couldn't finish. "I'm proud of you. I am. And I know this is not the — this is bad timing and probably the wrong room for it, but I am." A beat. "Tyler's mom, I'm sorry about the language. And the furniture part. And maybe the rest of it too."

A hand — Tyler's this time, unmistakable up close — reached in and took the microphone gently, patient enough that it never turned into a tug. Doug let it go. Brendan started the applause, two quick claps that no one could politely refuse forever. Kelsey did not join in. Megan's hands stayed in her lap. Tyler's mother was looking down at her untouched plate. Then the video cut to a wider angle that showed the room — the tables, the flowers, the wedding party — and in the far right of the frame, barely visible, Kelsey was looking at her father with an expression that Doug, watching from his bathroom, could not read, because the camera wasn't close enough and because even if it had been, he might not have had the vocabulary.

The video ended. 2.1 million views. Fourteen thousand comments. The runtime said 7:14.

Doug stared at the frozen final frame — a blurry wide shot of the ballroom with his own figure visible at the edge, still standing, one hand at his side. He sat there on the bathtub for what might have been a minute or five minutes. The faucet dripped. The heater ran. A car passed on the street outside, its tires on the wet pavement making a long tearing hiss.

He pressed play again.

The second time was different. The second time, the horror backed off enough for him to notice things. He noticed the beat after the highway story, the way his own face stayed solemn while three tables laughed a second late. He noticed that when he said upgrade, Megan set her glass down so carefully it looked rehearsed and Brendan stopped smiling without changing anything else in his face. He noticed Tyler had turned toward Kelsey long before he came to take the microphone, which meant the room's attention had split earlier than Doug had understood. He noticed Tyler's mother put down her fork at the furniture line and leave it there.

He noticed the table section, and this time it was not just his own left hand he watched — the thumb worrying the stem of the stolen wine glass, the microphone hanging lower and lower, Kelsey's napkin crushed in her fist while Tyler faced her instead of him. His stomach still dropped. He had not remembered his voice going that small.

And he noticed the part about Kelsey. The "you turned out good" part. He'd meant every word of it, and the problem wasn't that he'd said it in front of 180 people after eight whiskeys. The problem was that he'd needed the eight whiskeys and the 180 people. He'd had four thousand evenings — standing in his kitchen, driving her to the dentist, sitting across from her at restaurants where she ordered pad thai and he ordered whatever was too spicy — and he'd talked about the weather and the Reds and the condition of the roads and never once said the thing he'd said drunk to a room full of strangers.

He put the phone down and walked into the kitchen. The apartment arranged itself around him the way it always did — the Ikea bookshelf with its slight forward lean, the counter where he ate cereal standing up every evening, the single clean glass drying upside down on a dish towel, the chair by the window with a week of unopened mail on it.

He drank a glass of water. He drank another one. He put the glass in the sink and looked out the window at the street, where a neighbor was chipping ice off a windshield with a credit card, and the ordinary fact of this — a Sunday in February in Clintonville, someone dealing with ice — was so far from what was happening inside his phone that the distance felt structural, a gap between two conditions that no adjuster could reconcile.

---

The phone rang at 12:15. Doug was in the kitchen in sweatpants and a T-shirt from the laundry pile, eating a piece of bread because bread was the only thing that didn't require a decision. The caller ID showed a 614 number he didn't recognize. He let it ring through. It rang again — same number. He let that go too.

A text from Roy: *If you're up, call if you want. Don't if you don't.*

Doug looked at the text for a while. It was the right thing, constructed at the right scale — an offer that made no demand, carried no curiosity disguised as concern. Roy had sent two sentences and meant each of them at face value, which was a skill Doug had never seen anyone else manage.

He typed back: *I'm up. I'll call later.*

Roy: *Okay.*

The phone rang again. 216 — Cleveland. Kelsey. He let it go. He wasn't ready for Kelsey, for the conversation that would begin with him saying something insufficient about being sorry and end with her saying things she was too angry to organize, except Kelsey angry wasn't Kelsey loud — it was Kelsey quiet and specific, and Doug knew exactly what that was because she'd gotten it from him.

He ate the bread. It was the heel, the end piece that most people threw away, and Doug ate it because discarding a usable portion of a purchased product had always registered as a small, correctable failure. The bread was dry. He didn't toast it because the toaster had been making a burning smell since October and investigating it would mean pulling it away from the wall, and behind the toaster was an accumulation of crumbs from a period he preferred not to calculate, and the whole project exceeded what this particular Sunday could support.

His phone buzzed. Then again. Texts were arriving from numbers he didn't have saved — people who'd gotten his number from somewhere, a cousin in Akron he hadn't spoken to since his father's funeral, someone who identified themselves only as "Mike from college" though Doug could not place a Mike from college and had attended a commuter campus where he'd known perhaps eleven people. He put the phone face-down on the counter and watched the back of it vibrate against the laminate, a small persistent tremor that kept renewing itself.

He thought about going back to bed. He thought about getting in his Civic and driving to the Mutual Fidelity office and sitting at his desk and opening the Hoffmeyer claim file, which concerned a detached garage in Reynoldsburg and a disputed cause of structural failure, and doing the work of assessing damage that belonged to someone else. He thought about the video. It was still accumulating views at a rate he could calculate — the insurance adjuster in him wanted the number, wanted to quantify the scope of the event in square footage or replacement cost — but he stopped himself, because the number would be larger now, and larger was a direction he could not yet face.

He washed the glass. He wiped the counter. He stood at the window and watched the neighbor finish scraping the windshield and get in the car and drive away, and the street was empty again, and the quiet of it was the same quiet his apartment always had, except that now it carried the blank waiting space between incident description and loss estimate on a claims report.

The phone rang. He looked at the screen. 310. Los Angeles. Doug had never been to Los Angeles. He did not know anyone in Los Angeles. He had no professional or personal connection to the city, the state, or the time zone, and the number was not one that had any reason to appear on the phone of an insurance adjuster in Columbus, Ohio, on a Sunday afternoon in February.

He didn't answer.

The phone went still. The kitchen was quiet. The heater clicked off and the sudden absence of its noise was louder than the noise had been, and Doug stood there in his sweatpants with crumbs on his shirt and a hangover that had settled into something permanent and low-grade, the day's operating condition, and the apartment was exactly what it had been when he'd gone to sleep — the leaning bookshelf, the bread bag twisted shut with its plastic clip, the dish towel on the oven handle — except that somewhere between last night and now, the walls had become thinner, and two million people were on the other side of them.

The phone rang again. 310. He let it go to voicemail too. When the screen went dark, he carried the phone to the couch and opened the comments.


# Chapter 2

In the bathroom before work, Doug checked the number again. Fourteen million views. He was in boxers and a t-shirt that said MUTUAL FIDELITY CLAIMS DIVISION SOFTBALL 2019. They had never had a softball division. Someone in marketing had ordered the shirts anyway, and Doug had kept his because it was soft and because he had no one to be embarrassed in front of.

Fourteen million. He said the number to the mirror, to his own face, which looked approximately the way a face looks after four hours of sleep, three of which had been spent reading what strangers thought of it. The number had no meaning. It was the population of a moderately sized country. Doug had looked this up at 1 a.m., when the count was still at eleven million and he'd needed some frame of reference — Belgium, or close to it. More people had watched him say things he couldn't remember deciding to say than lived in a nation with its own parliament and a functioning rail system.

He drove to work in the Civic with the heat on high because February in Columbus was the color of wet concrete and roughly as warm. His phone sat in the cup holder, buzzing at intervals that had stopped corresponding to individual events and had become ambient, like sitting on a washing machine. He'd stopped looking at each notification around midnight. The 310 number — Los Angeles, he'd figured out — had called six more times since Saturday. He hadn't answered. He didn't know anyone in Los Angeles. He didn't want to know anyone in Los Angeles.

The internet had been busy overnight. Doug knew this because he'd watched it happen before giving up on sleep, lying on his couch in the dark with the phone held above his face, reading the terms of his own situation.

There were memes now. Someone had captured the moment — three seconds into the toast, roughly — where Doug's left hand was raised in a gesture that, in context, was him reaching for words and, out of context, looked like a man delivering a papal blessing over his daughter's open bar. This image existed now with white block text: YOUR MARRIAGE HAS BEEN ADJUSTED. There was another one, his face mid-sentence, mouth open, eyes half-closed in what was clearly the alcohol but read on screen as beatific certainty: WHEN THE WHISKEY KICKS IN AND THE TRUTH COMES OUT. There were dozens more. Doug's face with the caption PROOF OF LOSS, which he recognized as insurance terminology someone had pulled from his job title, and which was more accurate than whoever made it probably intended.

Someone had auto-tuned the toast into a song. Doug had listened to thirty seconds. His voice — his actual voice, the one that came out of his body — had been pitch-corrected and layered over a beat that sounded like it belonged in a car driven by someone thirty years younger than him. The words were still his words. The cadence was still roughly his cadence. But the musicality made every sentence sound like it was building toward a chorus that never arrived, which was, Doug thought, not a bad description of his marriage.

He'd closed the app.

A Twitter thread by someone whose profile identified them as a "body language analyst and certified life coach" had broken down the toast frame by frame. Doug's posture (defeated). His hand gestures (subconsciously reaching). His eye contact with Kelsey (avoidant but searching). His single glance toward Megan, which the analyst called "the micro-expression of a man who has rehearsed saying nothing for twelve years and just forgot the script." The thread had four thousand retweets. Doug had watched the original video twice and could not identify any of the things this person was identifying, which either meant the analyst was making it up or that Doug's body had been doing things he hadn't authorized, which was, given the toast itself, not surprising.

A Reddit post titled "This man just eulogized his own marriage and I can't stop watching" had, when he'd last checked, forty thousand upvotes and a comment section that scrolled for what appeared to be geological ages. Doug had started reading the comments. This had been a mistake he intended to repeat.

The Mutual Fidelity parking lot looked the same as it always did. Doug pulled into his spot — not assigned, but his through the accumulation of years that turns habit into tenure — and sat in the Civic for a minute with the engine running. The building was three stories of beige brick. It processed insurance claims. It was the most uninteresting structure in a city that had several strong contenders, and Doug had never been so grateful to see it.

He walked in. Greg was at the security desk with a Louis L'Amour paperback spread face-down on the counter. Every morning Greg gave Doug the same two-finger salute. This morning he gave it, then dropped his eyes to the bent corner of the paperback before Doug reached the desk.

"Morning, Greg."

"Morning, Doug." A pause. "Cold one today."

"Yeah."

By the time Doug got to his cubicle, enough people had looked up and then back down that the second floor seemed to be doing it in sequence. His work inbox had seventeen new messages. Two were from people in other departments who had somehow developed urgent questions about claim numbers they could have answered themselves.

Janet appeared at 9:40 with the Hoffmeyer file and her white mug that said WORLD'S OKAY-EST MOM. She kept a dish of butterscotch hard candies on her desk for difficult conversations. She hadn't brought one.

"Doug. Do you have a minute?"

"Sure."

She sat in Roy's overflow chair, opened the folder, and walked him through the contractor's estimate and the roof-pitch calculation. Doug said he'd double-check the measurements. Janet thanked him, closed the folder, and stayed where she was.

"So," Janet said.

"Yeah," Doug said.

Janet looked at the Blue Jackets schedule pinned to the partition beside his monitor, opened her mouth, and shut it again. "Let me know about the Hoffmeyer measurements."

"I will."

She went back to her office. Before she sat down, Doug's email dinged. Janet had forwarded something from HR titled "Social Media Guidelines: A Reminder for All Employees." It had gone to the full office. Doug understood, with the practiced clarity of a man trained to read subtext in policy language, that it was for him.

---

Roy arrived at 10:15, late because of a dentist appointment, the right side of his jaw swollen from a root canal. He sat down, opened his computer, and said "Morning" out of the left side of his mouth.

"Hey. You okay?"

"Root canal."

"Sorry."

"It's fine. It's a tooth."

"Roy."

"Yeah."

"Did you—" Doug stopped. He wasn't sure how to phrase it. "Have you been on the internet this weekend?"

Roy blinked once. The swollen side of his jaw made the pause look slower than it probably was. He kept his eyes on Doug long enough for the fluorescent lights to fill the silence. "No," he said. "I check the weather. That's about it."

"Okay."

"Why?"

Doug turned his phone face-down on the desk. "No reason."

Roy opened a file — paper, because Roy still preferred paper files even though the system had been digital for three years and Janet had stopped fighting this preference approximately eighteen months ago.

"I heard something," Roy said, without looking up.

"Yeah?"

"Something happened at the wedding. A couple people mentioned it."

"Yeah."

"I don't need to know what it was."

"Okay."

"I'm just saying that. In case you were going to tell me."

"I wasn't."

"Good." Roy turned a page. He read it. He turned another page. Doug watched him read until he felt his hands unclench from the chair arms.

The desk phone rang. The landline, which almost never rang because most business came through email and the only people who called the desk line were older policyholders who didn't trust computers and Kelsey, who sometimes tried the office when Doug's cell went to voicemail. He picked it up.

"Doug Bridge, Mutual Fidelity."

"Mr. Bridge, this is Tammy Hess from the *Today* show. I'm calling to see if—"

"I'm sorry, this is my work line."

"I understand, and I apologize. We got your office number from—"

"I'm sorry," Doug said, and hung up.

He looked at the phone. The *Today* show had his work number. Someone in New York had called the main line for Mutual Fidelity Insurance, navigated the directory, and selected Doug Bridge, Claims Division, as though he were the sort of person who ought to be reachable.

"Who was that?" Roy asked.

"Wrong number."

Roy accepted this without investigation. He turned another page.

Before lunch Doug's Gmail had added eleven more emails: a production company, a podcast called *Dads Being Real*, two literary agents, a brand partnership consultant, a morning radio show in Cincinnati. He put the phone in his desk drawer. It kept buzzing under the file folders like something trapped alive.

He opened the Hoffmeyer file. He measured the roof pitch calculation. He documented the square footage. The numbers were straightforward and the numbers asked nothing of him and he read them with a care that anyone watching might have mistaken for dedication but was actually something closer to need.

By noon the video was at twenty-two million. Doug knew this because he checked in the bathroom, standing in a stall with his pants on and the seat down, reading the screen with the focused attention of a man studying the results of a test he hadn't known he was taking. He refreshed the page. Twenty-two million and climbing. He put the phone away and went back to his desk and ate the turkey sandwich he'd brought from home and didn't taste any of it.

By two o'clock it was at twenty-six million. By the time he left the office at 5:30, walking across the parking lot in the dark — because February in Columbus meant darkness at 5:30, and the parking lot lights made the slush look orange, and the cold was the kind that found the gap between his collar and his neck and settled there — the count had reached thirty-one million.

---

He drove home. The apartment was the same as he'd left it — the couch cushion still dented from where he'd sat reading comments until two the night before, the suit from the wedding draped over the kitchen chair where he'd finally taken it off Sunday afternoon, the one dress shoe by the front door and the other one still unaccounted for. He'd been wearing one shoe when he woke up Sunday morning. The other was somewhere between the reception hall and his apartment, and the fact that he'd been home for two days and hadn't looked for it or thought about it until now was the kind of thing that, in a different person's life, might have been funny.

He stood at the kitchen counter and ate a bowl of cereal. Raisin Bran, the store brand, not because he preferred it but because it was $2.19 and the name brand was $4.49 and the taste difference wasn't enough to justify the markup. This was the kind of calculation Megan had watched him make for eighteen years — the per-unit comparison, the coupon logic, the small satisfactions of rational purchasing — and the look on her face during the last three years of their marriage, when he'd hold up two boxes of something in the cereal aisle and explain the math, had been the look of a woman who had realized that the man she'd married would comparison-shop his way through every experience, including the ones where the point was not the savings but the standing next to someone in a grocery store because you wanted to be there.

The cereal was fine. The apartment was quiet. Outside, a car drove past with its bass turned up so loud the glasses in the cabinet above the sink rattled against each other, a sound Doug heard every night at roughly this time and had stopped noticing and now noticed again because noticing was all he'd been doing for two days and he couldn't turn it off.

He checked the phone. Forty-four million.

The number had more than tripled since the morning. Doug tried to visualize forty-four million people. He could not. He tried to think of it in insurance terms — forty-four million policyholders, each one a file, each file a name and an address and a specific thing that had gone wrong with their house. He couldn't process that either. The number existed outside any frame he had for understanding the world. He assessed claims for houses in central Ohio. He understood square footage, depreciation schedules, the rate at which a water-damaged hardwood floor loses value. He did not understand forty-four million strangers forming opinions about seven minutes of his life that he himself could barely remember living.

He sat on the couch. He opened the comments.

He knew this was a mistake. He'd known it was a mistake the first time, Sunday morning, and had done it anyway, the way he sometimes couldn't stop re-reading a claim file that didn't add up — the compulsion of looking for the one detail that would make the whole thing cohere.

The comments didn't cohere. They weren't supposed to. They were the sound of a fraction of forty-four million people processing Doug Bridge into something that fit their needs.

**This guy is my hero.** Doug as folk hero, Doug as truth-teller, Doug as the man who said what everyone thinks and nobody says. These came with exclamation marks and crying-laughing emoji and the phrase "I felt that" repeated so many times it stopped sounding like a feeling and started sounding like applause.

**What an asshole.** Doug had ruined his daughter's wedding. He had humiliated Megan in front of everyone she knew. He had taken a room that was not about him and made it entirely about him. Those comments came in short, certain sentences, and Doug read them with the numb attention of a man reviewing a denial he had expected to receive. They weren't wrong.

The ones he couldn't file were the comments that treated the toast like a tool. A woman wrote, "The part about the table made me leave my husband," and Doug stared at that until the screen dimmed. Another commenter named InsuranceBro614 wrote, "I work in claims and this is the most accurate description of a marriage I've ever heard from an adjuster. 10/10 would recommend the whiskey." Doug didn't know which part bothered him more: that something he'd said about a table might have shoved a stranger out of her marriage, or that somebody who might be sitting on the second floor of Mutual Fidelity had recognized himself in it.

He read comments until 1:45 a.m. The apartment was dark except for the phone's glow. His cereal bowl was in the sink, unwashed. The suit was still on the chair. The missing shoe was still missing.

At 1:47 he put the phone down and picked up the Gahanna file.

The Gahanna claim was a water heater. A standard forty-gallon residential unit in a house on Agler Road — the homeowner had submitted it Friday, before the wedding, and it had been sitting in Doug's take-home stack since then, untouched, while Doug was getting drunk and saying true things and becoming a person forty-four million strangers had opinions about. The water heater had burst at the base seam. The basement had taken four inches of standing water before the homeowner found it. He'd documented everything: photographs of the water line on the basement walls, the receipt for the original unit purchased in 2016, a contractor's estimate for remediation and replacement. The documentation was clean. The coverage was clear. The depreciation on a 2016 unit was calculable — ten years of expected life, six years in service, the math was straightforward and the math didn't care what Doug had done at a wedding.

He read the file. He read it again. He read the contractor's estimate line by line — demolition of damaged drywall, removal of saturated carpet padding, installation of dehumidification equipment for a minimum of seventy-two hours, replacement of the forty-gallon unit with a comparable model, labor at $85 per hour for an estimated fourteen hours. The numbers were real. They referred to actual things — water that had actually damaged a floor, carpet that was actually ruined, a heater that had actually failed at a seam the way heaters do when they're six years old and the anode rod hasn't been replaced and nobody checks the anode rod because nobody thinks about the thing that keeps the tank from corroding until the tank corrodes. The numbers didn't have opinions about Doug. They didn't think he was brave or pathetic or a genius. They were a claim, and he was an adjuster, and the distance between the problem and the resolution was a thing he could measure and close.

He made notes on the file. He flagged a question about whether the homeowner's policy covered mold remediation or whether that would require a separate rider — the standing water had been there for several hours before discovery, and four inches for several hours in a basement with limited ventilation was enough to start a mold issue that the homeowner might not see for weeks. He paper-clipped the estimate to the photograph of the water line. He put the file on the kitchen table, aligned with the edge, and set his pen on top of it.

It was 2:20 a.m. The phone sat face-down on the couch where he'd left it. The screen was dark. Somewhere — on the internet, in Belgium, in all the places people look at their phones in the middle of the night — millions of people were watching Doug Bridge say the truest things he'd ever said, and none of them knew that the man in the video was sitting in a two-bedroom apartment in Clintonville, in his underwear, at a kitchen table, at 2:20 in the morning, processing a water heater claim from Gahanna because it was the only thing left that made sense.


# Chapter 3

Doug got to the office at ten to eight. He was always early. It wasn't ambition. It was the same instinct that made him check his mirrors twice before changing lanes: on time felt one step from late, and late meant something had gone wrong that he should have prevented. The parking lot was a third full. Roy's car, a 2011 Camry with a dent in the rear quarter panel that Roy had decided years ago was not worth fixing, was already in its usual spot near the loading dock, where nobody else parked because it was farthest from the entrance and because nobody else wanted to walk, which was exactly why Roy parked there.

Mutual Fidelity occupied the second floor of a building on Sawyer Road that also housed a tax preparation service and an orthodontist whose patients could sometimes be heard through the floor, a low hum of suction and distress that Doug had long ago stopped noticing. The elevator was slow enough that Doug took the stairs, which he did not consider exercise. The office was open-plan, thirty desks arranged in rows of five, separated by low partitions that provided the illusion of privacy without any of its function. The carpet was the color of weak tea. The ceiling tiles were acoustic and stained in two places from the building's intermittent plumbing issues, which Doug understood better than the building management did — he'd assessed the office's own water damage claim two years ago, and the repair had not addressed the root cause, which was a compression fitting in the third-floor bathroom that would fail again within eighteen months. He'd noted this in his report. No one had followed up.

His desk was fourth in the second row, next to the window. Roy's desk was across the partition. Both desks had the same equipment — a monitor, a keyboard, a phone, a wire inbox that held actual paper because claims documentation still arrived by mail more often than people assumed. Doug's desk had a coffee mug from the 2019 office Secret Santa that said WORLD'S MOST ADEQUATE on it, a gift from Roy that Doug had long since accepted as praise.

He hung his coat on the back of his chair and turned on his monitor. The inbox had eleven new emails, eight of them internal. Two were from claimants. One was from his supervisor Janet, forwarded without comment, subject line: "FW: Social Media Use and Company Representation — Revised Guidelines." Doug opened it. The email was from HR and used the phrase "professional conduct in digital spaces" three times in four paragraphs. It did not mention Doug by name. It didn't need to. Mutual Fidelity had existed for forty-one years and none of its employees had ever gone viral for anything, a record that Doug had broken by accident on a Saturday night with eight whiskeys in him and a microphone someone should have taken away.

He closed the email and opened the first claim file.

The morning had its own architecture. Doug liked mornings at the office — the specific quiet of thirty people doing the same kind of work, the heating system pushing warm air through the ceiling vents with a sound like slow breathing, the occasional phone conversation conducted in the moderate tone everyone used, as if the entire floor had agreed on a volume and stuck to it. He'd been here fifteen years. The work suited him, and he'd never had to justify that because no one had ever asked. You processed claims. You read the documentation, assessed the damage, compared it to the policy, determined what was covered and what wasn't, and issued a finding. The work was precise, specific, and consequential to the people whose houses had flooded or burned or collapsed, and Doug gave it the necessary attention without effort — the attention of someone who noticed what was actually there rather than what should be there.

His specialty since 2014 was residential water damage. He'd ended up in it the way people end up in most specialties — a staffing need, a reassignment, a gradual accumulation of expertise that became identity. He knew what happened when a pipe burst in a wall at 2 a.m. in January — the sound first, which homeowners always described as a pop or a crack but was really more of a give, something structural surrendering. He knew the smell of a house three days after a sump pump failure — the sweet, vegetal rot that got into the drywall and stayed there, behind the paint, in the fiberglass insulation, months after the standing water was gone. He knew the look on people's faces when they stood in their own ruined living rooms holding the documentation they'd been told to gather, usually a stack inches thick or almost nothing at all, and never exactly what the form asked for.

The first file on his desk was a garage roof in Reynoldsburg. A family named Pardee had woken up on a Thursday to find their garage ceiling bowed inward, the weight of ice accumulation having finally exceeded the structural capacity of a roof that was, according to the building permit, twenty-three years old. The claim was straightforward — ice damage, covered under the homeowner's policy, minus the deductible. Doug reviewed the adjuster's field photos on his monitor: the sagging drywall, the water stain spreading across it like a bruise, the ice dam visible on the exterior shot where the gutter had separated from the fascia board. He compared the roof age to the depreciation schedule and prepared his assessment. The Pardees were going to get less than they expected, because the roof's actual cash value was not what it would cost to replace it — it was what a twenty-three-year-old roof was worth with twenty-three years of weather on it. This was the part of the job that required a specific kind of honesty: telling people that the thing they'd lost was worth less than they thought, by the numbers, even though the numbers had nothing to do with what it felt like to wake up to a ceiling caving in over your car.

He finished the Pardee file at nine-fifteen and opened the next one. A grease fire in a duplex on East Main. The tenant had left a pan of oil on the stove and gone to check on a child in the other room, which was a sentence Doug had written in various forms hundreds of times — the particular American catastrophe of unattended cooking oil, which caused more residential fires than any other single source, and which almost always started with someone leaving the kitchen to do something that seemed like it would take ten seconds. The fire itself was contained to the kitchen, but the smoke had traveled through the shared wall into the other unit, and the question of how far smoke damage extended was always a negotiation between what the nose could smell and what the policy defined as "damaged." The neighbor in unit B claimed she could still smell it in her bedroom closet. The insurer's position was that odor without visible residue did not constitute damage. Doug was going to have to go out there with a thermal imaging camera and settle it. He made a note.

He was halfway through the grease fire documentation when Kevin Beech from IT stopped at his desk. Kevin was twenty-six, tall, with a beard that looked like a decision he'd stopped keeping up with. He carried a laptop under one arm and kept his phone ready in the other. He'd been wearing the same pair of New Balance sneakers for as long as Doug had known him, gray with an orange stripe, and their continued survival seemed to bother him, as if he were waiting for permission to replace them.

"Hey, Doug." Kevin shifted the laptop to his other arm. "So I know this is kind of random, but — could I get a picture? My girlfriend's been sharing your video all week and she's watched it like fifteen times."

Doug looked at Kevin. Kevin had fixed his email three months ago when the Outlook sync failed, and they had discussed nothing personal on that occasion or any other.

"A picture," Doug said.

"Yeah, like a selfie? If that's cool. She'd just — yeah."

It was not cool, though Doug couldn't have explained why. It was a small thing, a phone held up, a moment. But something about it felt like being reclassified — moved from one category to another without filling out the paperwork. He'd been a coworker Kevin nodded at in the hallway. Now he was something Kevin's girlfriend wanted proof of.

"Sure," Doug said, and Kevin leaned in and held up his phone and Doug made a face that he could feel, from the inside, was the face of a man being identified in a lineup.

"Awesome. Thanks, man." Kevin looked at the photo. "She's gonna lose it. You're, like, her favorite person on the internet right now."

Doug wanted to say that being someone's favorite person on the internet was a category he had not known existed until a week ago and would have preferred to go on not knowing about. He said, "Tell her I said thanks."

Kevin left. Roy, on the other side of the partition, had not looked up from his monitor.

"That'll happen," Roy said.

"What will?"

"The picture thing." Roy typed something, finished it, and leaned back. "My nephew is on YouTube. He reviews flashlights. People send him flashlights. He has over three hundred flashlights now."

"He reviews flashlights."

"He reviews flashlights. He's very thorough about it. Tests the lumens, the battery life, the water resistance. I don't understand it but he seems to enjoy himself. He's got a whole room that's just flashlights."

Doug let that sit. With Roy, information was often as close as comfort got.

He turned back to his monitor and opened the next file.

---

The pool claim had been sitting in his queue for two days, and Doug had been saving it the way some people save the good part of a meal — not consciously, just pushing it down the stack. The file was labeled BURRIS/COMMONWEALTH — two parties, two policies, a jurisdictional tangle that the intake notes described as "complex coverage determination required."

Carl Burris, of Westerville, owned a twenty-four-foot above-ground pool. Round, steel-walled, installed in 2019 by a company that had since gone out of business, a fact irrelevant to the claim and still somehow attached to the loss. On the night of February 8th, the wall of the pool had split along a manufacturing seam. The manufacturer's warranty had expired nineteen months prior. Approximately six thousand gallons of water — Doug did the math: a pool that size held roughly eight thousand gallons when full, and February in central Ohio meant it was at three-quarter capacity from snowmelt and winter runoff — had poured through the gap in the wall, crossed eight feet of frozen yard, gone under the chain-link fence that separated Burris's property from his neighbor's, and flooded the rear garden of one Margaret Commonwealth.

Margaret Commonwealth grew roses. Specifically, she grew hybrid tea roses, which Doug learned from the documentation were a particular category of rose bred for large blooms on long stems, the kind you saw in florist shops and at funerals. She had seventeen of them. She had receipts for all seventeen, dating back over six years of purchases from a nursery in Delaware, Ohio, that specialized in rose stock. The total documented value of the seventeen hybrid teas was $4,218.47, which included the plants themselves, the soil amendments, a drip irrigation system she'd installed herself, and a cedar trellis structure that the floodwater had knocked sideways and cracked at the base.

The roses were dead. Six thousand gallons of chlorinated pool water in February had killed them comprehensively — the chlorine burned the root systems and the cold prevented any chance of recovery. Margaret Commonwealth had photographs. She had taken them, Doug noted, in the specific way of someone who understood that documentation mattered: each plant labeled, each receipt matched to a purchase date, the trellis photographed from three angles. She had done this better than most professional claimants he worked with. Her file was immaculate. The roses were still dead.

She had filed the claim. And here was where it got interesting.

Carl Burris's homeowner's policy covered the structural failure of the pool — the replacement cost of the wall, the labor to repair it. But his liability coverage, Section II of the standard homeowner's form, covered bodily injury and property damage to third parties only under specific conditions, and the definition of "occurrence" in Burris's policy required that the damage result from either an "accident" or "negligence." The pool wall splitting was arguably an accident. But the insurer could also argue negligence — that Burris should have inspected the wall before winter, should have maintained the seam, should have known that a steel wall installed by a company that no longer existed might not last forever. The adjuster's field notes indicated that Burris had not inspected the wall. Burris had not, as far as Doug could determine from the documentation, thought about the wall at all between the day the pool was installed and the night it failed. Doug understood this completely. Most people did not think about the structural integrity of the things that held their lives in place until those things gave way.

Margaret Commonwealth's policy was a different problem entirely. Her homeowner's coverage included a rider for her garden — she'd had the foresight to schedule the rose collection individually, which meant the plants were listed and valued on the policy. This was unusual and, Doug thought, genuinely impressive. But the coverage was for named perils: wind, hail, fire, theft, vandalism, and a handful of other events that the actuarial tables considered worth insuring against. Not flood. Not "structural failure of adjacent property." Not "your neighbor's above-ground pool catastrophically discharging six thousand gallons of chlorinated water onto your prize hybrid teas in the middle of February," which was not a named peril in any homeowner's policy Doug had ever read, because the actuarial tables had not, apparently, accounted for this possibility.

Doug read the file twice. He pulled up the policy language for both Burris and Commonwealth on side-by-side windows. He cross-referenced the liability endorsement on Burris's policy with the Ohio Revised Code standards for negligence in property maintenance. He looked up the replacement cost of hybrid tea roses from the Delaware nursery's website, which listed prices by cultivar — the cultivar names alone were worth the search: Mister Lincoln, Double Delight, Peace, Chrysler Imperial. Margaret Commonwealth had a Pink Peace that the nursery listed at ninety-two dollars.

He was, he realized, enjoying himself. Not the loss itself — the loss was real, the roses were dead, Margaret Commonwealth's six years of cultivation were gone and no policy was going to bring back a mature Pink Peace in bloom. But the coverage question was genuine. Two policies, neither designed for this scenario, both containing language that could be read in multiple directions, and the resolution would determine who paid for seventeen dead roses and a cracked cedar trellis. A right answer was buried somewhere inside it, and finding that answer required exactly the attention Doug was built to give.

---

Sal's was a block and a half from the office, a narrow storefront with a counter, a deli case, and a row of booths along the front window. Doug and Roy had been eating there every workday for nine years. Roy ordered the same thing every time, down to the way he asked for it: turkey on wheat, mustard, no tomato. Doug varied his order within a narrow range — ham and swiss, or roast beef, or occasionally the Italian, which was good but more bread than he wanted by the end.

Today Doug got the roast beef. Roy got the turkey. They slid into the booth by the window. After nine years it had become theirs through repetition. They always sat there, and neither of them would have cared if someone else were sitting there first. No one ever was. The red vinyl was split at one corner and patched with a strip of silver tape that had been there for as long as Doug could remember, which was a different form of acceptance.

"So the pool wall splits," Doug said. He was holding his sandwich but hadn't bitten into it yet. "Steel wall, above-ground, installed in 2019. And — six thousand gallons. Well, the pool holds about eight thousand full, but February in central Ohio, snowmelt, three-quarter capacity, so call it six thousand. It goes under the fence, across eight feet of frozen yard, and into the neighbor's garden."

Roy ate. He ate with the efficiency of a man who considered lunch a task to be completed rather than an experience to be prolonged. He chewed, swallowed, and listened with the same expression he wore for everything, which was no particular expression at all.

"The neighbor has roses," Doug said. "Seventeen hybrid teas. She's got receipts going back six years. She scheduled them on her policy individually, which — I don't think I've seen a claimant do that."

"Chlorine," Roy said.

"Chlorine and cold. Either one probably would've done it. Both together, there's no question. They're all dead."

Roy took another bite. He was most of the way through the sandwich. "Whose policy covers it."

"That's the thing. Burris, the pool owner, he's got liability for third-party damage, but only if it qualifies as negligence or accident. And the wall splits at a manufacturing seam on a pool he never once inspected." Doug set his sandwich down. "Nobody inspects their pool wall."

"I don't have a pool," Roy said.

"Right, but if you did. Would you go out there every fall and check the seams?"

"No."

"Nobody would. But the insurer could argue he should've. And her policy — named perils only. Wind, hail, fire, theft. Not 'neighbor's pool blows out in February.' Both policies get close but neither one covers it. There's a gap and the roses are sitting right in the middle of it."

Roy folded the paper his sandwich had come in into a neat square and set it on the tray. He'd done this every day for nine years. The square was always the same size.

"How much," he said.

"The roses? Four thousand two hundred eighteen dollars and forty-seven cents. Plants, soil, irrigation, a cedar trellis. Some of those cultivars run sixty, seventy dollars a plant. She had a Pink Peace listed at ninety-two."

"That's a lot of roses."

"She did everything right," Doug said. "Documented, insured, scheduled. That's the part I keep coming back to."

Roy looked out the window. The street was gray and wet, a late-winter Columbus day with the sky and the pavement the same color and the temperature sitting right at thirty-four degrees, too warm for snow and too cold for anything to feel like it was going anywhere. A woman walked past with a stroller under a plastic rain cover that made it look like a small greenhouse.

"You'll figure it out," Roy said.

"I don't know that I will. There might not be a clean answer."

"Might not be. Still have to close the file."

Doug bit into his sandwich. The roast beef was fine. It had been fine for nine years. He had organized a surprising amount of his life around things that were reliably fine.

They ate. The lunch crowd there was small and consistent — two women from the physical therapy clinic downstairs who came in most days, a man in a reflective vest who ordered three subs to go and left without sitting down. The owners' daughter, who was maybe ten, sat behind the counter doing homework with headphones on, her pencil moving in the unhurried rhythm of a kid who'd been doing homework in this exact spot for years. She had a juice box balanced on a stack of napkins.

Roy finished and set his hands flat on the Formica, which was how Roy indicated he was done eating but not ready to leave. He sat like that, looking out the window.

Doug watched him. Roy's life fit him. It wasn't tailored or stylish. It was the right size and worn in. He owned a house in Gahanna that was, by Roy's own description, "a house." Three bedrooms, one of which he used, one of which was empty, one of which held a treadmill he'd bought in 2017 and used regularly until he decided that walking outside achieved the same result without the monthly cost of electricity, so the treadmill sat there now, unplugged and draped with a bath towel he'd never retrieved. He drove the dented Camry. He watched no streaming services and did not own a subscription to anything except the newspaper, which arrived in physical form and which he read at the kitchen table each morning. He read one book a month from the Columbus Metropolitan Library, choosing the next one by walking to the new arrivals shelf and picking up whatever was at eye level — a system he'd described once, without irony, and which Doug had found both unreasonable and difficult to argue with. He went to bed at nine forty-five every night.

"You hear from the phone people?" Roy said. He meant the calls Doug had been getting — the interview requests, the media inquiries, the 310 area code that kept ringing. Doug had mentioned them at the end of yesterday, briefly, with the detached air of a man reporting weather that had happened nearby.

"A few more," Doug said. "I haven't been answering."

"Probably wise."

"I don't know what I'd say."

"You'd say whatever you'd say. They'd run whatever part of it they wanted."

"Whatever part of it they wanted," Doug said. Not as agreement. He just hadn't thought about that part yet.

Roy looked at Doug's plate. "You going to eat that pickle?"

Doug looked at his plate. There was a pickle spear he hadn't touched. He didn't like pickles, which Roy knew, and Roy liked pickles, which Doug knew, and the transfer of the pickle had been happening for so long that asking about it was itself the point — not necessary, but repeated, and the repetition had made it into something.

"All yours," Doug said.

Roy ate the pickle. They walked back to the office. The air was cold and smelled like car exhaust and the particular mineral flatness that Columbus air carried in February — no flowers, no cut grass, nothing decomposing or growing, just the neutral smell of a city in winter waiting for a reason to change. Doug's phone was in his coat pocket. It had not buzzed during lunch, which was a relief and also, in some small way he didn't want to look at directly, a disappointment.

---

He spent the afternoon on the Commonwealth file. The Burris insurer held to its position that a wall splitting at the manufacturing seam was a defect, not negligence. Commonwealth's insurer held to named perils. Doug listened to both people tell him, in slightly different vocabularies, that the dead roses belonged to the gap between policies.

At four-twenty he called Margaret Commonwealth.

She answered on the first ring. "Hello."

"Mrs. Commonwealth? This is Doug Bridge with Mutual Fidelity. I'm handling the Burris claim."

"Good," she said. "Because right now all I have is seventeen dead rose bushes and a neighbor who keeps looking at them from his kitchen like that's going to help."

Doug turned slightly in his chair so Roy couldn't hear the call, though Roy was not a man who strained to hear anything. "Are the plants still in place?"

"Yes."

"The trellis too?"

"Tilted over and cracked."

"And the fence line hasn't been moved?"

"No."

He looked at the clock on his monitor. "Do you have somebody scheduled to clean it up?"

"My nephew's coming tomorrow morning with a truck," she said. "I'm done looking at it. The roots are burned. The canes went black yesterday. If you need to see them before they come out, tonight's the night."

Doug looked back at the file. "Will you be home around six?"

"I will."

"All right. I'll come by and document the loss myself."

There was a short pause, the kind that usually meant gratitude was trying not to sound like gratitude. "Thank you," she said. "And for the record, Carl says he's sorry. He said it three times. It didn't help the roses."

"No," Doug said. "I don't imagine it did."

At four-thirty Janet stopped by his desk. Janet was fifty-three, had managed the residential team for eleven years, and wore reading glasses on a beaded chain that she adjusted so often the motion had become unconscious — she'd reach up and touch the chain even when the glasses were already in place, the way some people touch a ring they've been wearing for years. She had a pen behind one ear that Doug was fairly sure she'd forgotten was there.

"Did you get a chance to look at the email I forwarded?"

"I did."

"HR wants to make sure everyone's aware of the updated guidelines." She said this with the expression of a person delivering a message she had not written and did not entirely endorse. "It's not about you specifically."

"I know."

She gave a brief nod, then looked at his screen. "How's Commonwealth?"

"Both carriers think the other one should be the problem. She's pulling the roses tomorrow morning. I want to go out there after work and see the yard before the evidence disappears."

Janet considered that. "For four thousand dollars in landscaping."

"For the liability question."

"Same difference to Accounting." She shifted the legal pad in her arms. "Go, then. But if you want Legal to touch it tomorrow, I need a full timeline in my inbox tonight. Not just notes. Timeline, photos, who said what."

"All right."

"And Doug."

He looked up.

"If somebody from television calls the main line, I'm sending them to HR."

"That seems reasonable."

Janet went back to her office. Doug printed the field notes, grabbed a company camera from the supply cabinet, and worked until five-fifteen with the particular concentration of a man who had converted an interesting problem into a site inspection.

Roy was already in his coat when Doug stood up.

"I'm heading to Westerville," Doug said.

"For the roses."

"Yeah."

Roy zipped his coat. "Take pictures of the fence."

"I will."

They walked out together. Nothing more needed to be said, and that distinction was one of the reasons the friendship worked.

Margaret Commonwealth's house sat on a short street of ranch homes with chain-link fences and winter-brown yards. The split pool wall was visible from the curb, folded outward along the seam like metal that had given up all at once. By the time Doug stepped through the gate the floodwater was gone, but its route remained: a broad stripe of flattened grass running under the fence and into the rose bed.

Margaret met him in a barn coat and gardening gloves. "I labeled them again," she said, as if he might think she'd relaxed since filing the claim.

"I saw that."

The rose bed ran along the back fence in a neat rectangle. Seventeen blackened plants rose from the mud like wiring stripped of its coating. Plastic tags marked the cultivars in tidy block letters. The trellis had gone over sideways and cracked where one leg had hit the frozen ground. Doug crouched, touched the soil, and felt cold water under the crusted top layer. The smell coming off it was dirt, chlorine, and the sweet wrong smell of something alive that had turned too quickly.

"Did Mr. Burris mention any problem with the wall before it failed?" Doug asked.

"Not to me." Margaret folded her arms inside the coat. "He said he heard a crack, came outside, and by then the whole thing was coming apart."

Carl Burris appeared at his back door as if his name had summoned him. He was a big man in a sweatshirt and jeans, holding a coil of hose he clearly had no use for. He came down the steps and stopped at his side of the fence.

"It happened fast," he said to Doug, not to Margaret. "I went out there with a flashlight and it was already split open."

"Had you noticed any bowing in the wall before February eighth?"

Carl looked at the collapsed ring of the pool. "Maybe a little by the ladder. I don't know. Pools always look a little wrong in winter."

Margaret made a sound through her nose and looked past Carl at the dead roses.

Doug took photos of the seam, the fence line, the dead plants, the trellis base, the stripe the water had cut through the yard. Halfway through the second set his phone buzzed in his coat pocket. He checked the screen without thinking. A 917 number. New York.

He put the phone back in his pocket and kept shooting.

When he was done, Margaret said, "So what happens now?"

Doug lowered the camera. "Now I write it up tonight and send it to my supervisor for legal review in the morning. Nobody gets to deny it off two phone calls."

Margaret nodded once. Carl shifted the hose from one hand to the other and said nothing.

The apartment was dark when Doug got home. He turned on the kitchen light, poured a bowl of Cheerios, and ate standing at the counter while the camera uploaded the photos. The milk was close to its sell-by date but still fine. Mud had dried in a thin line along the hem of his pants. The second bedroom door stayed closed.

He set the bowl in the sink, opened a blank email to Janet, and typed the subject line: BURRIS/COMMONWEALTH — timeline, site photos, request for legal review. Halfway through the first paragraph his phone lit on the counter. Same 917 number. He watched it ring until the screen went dark. Then he went back to the timeline, starting again with the crack Carl Burris said he had heard in the dark.


# Chapter 4

After the call he'd ignored over the Burris timeline, she called three more times before Doug answered. Twice more he'd watched the same 917 number glow on his phone and let it ring through to the voicemail he had never set up. The fourth time she left a message with the front desk at Mutual Fidelity, and Janet had walked the pink message slip to Doug's desk with a look that said she had questions she was choosing not to ask.

"A Layla Banks called," Janet said. "She says she's a talent manager. She says it's urgent." Janet placed the slip on Doug's keyboard with the care of someone delivering evidence. "She called on the main line, Doug."

"Thank you," Doug said.

"The main line," Janet said again, and went back to her office.

Doug called back that evening from his apartment, standing in the kitchen with a bowl of Grape-Nuts going soft on the counter. He didn't know why he chose that moment. Maybe because the cereal was already ruined and the evening had nowhere else to go.

She picked up before the first ring finished.

"Doug. Doug Bridge. Oh my god, finally. Okay, hi. Hi. I'm Layla Banks, I run Banks Management, and I — okay, first of all, I've watched your video probably thirty times, which I know sounds insane, but I'm going to be really honest with you because I think that's what you respond to based on, you know, the whole toast thing. I think you're sitting on something massive. I'm not talking about the video. I mean, yes, the video, but the video is the door, not the room. The room is — okay, can I just — are you free Thursday? Or Friday. I can fly out tomorrow."

"Fly out where?" Doug said.

"To you. Columbus. I want to sit down with you and talk about what's possible here because what's possible here is — I don't want to do this on the phone. Can I come to you?"

"You want to come to Columbus?"

"I'm looking at flights right now. I can get there Thursday afternoon. Is there somewhere we can meet? Your office, or — actually, I'd rather do it somewhere relaxed. Your place? Is that weird? That's probably weird. A coffee shop. Or your place, whatever's easier."

Doug looked at his apartment. The Ikea bookshelf with the shelf that sagged on the left side. The kitchen table with three chairs because the fourth had broken in the move and he'd never replaced it. The art print from the Columbus gallery that Megan had left behind, which he'd never taken down because the wall behind it was a slightly different shade of white.

"My place is fine," he said, because a coffee shop seemed worse.

"Amazing. Okay. Thursday. Text me the address. This is going to be so good, Doug. I'm — you have no idea. Okay. Thursday."

She hung up. Doug picked up his cereal. The Grape-Nuts had achieved the consistency of damp gravel. He ate them anyway, standing at the counter, because it was seven-thirty on a Tuesday in February and a woman in New York had just booked a flight to come see him, and the Grape-Nuts were the only thing in the apartment that hadn't changed.

---

Layla Banks arrived on Thursday at 4:20 p.m. She was smaller than her voice. Five-three, maybe five-four, in boots that added two inches but didn't change the fundamental impression, which was of someone who moved through space as if she were late for something better. She wore a black leather jacket that looked expensive over a white shirt that looked deliberate, and she carried a bag — not a purse, a bag, the kind with structure and intention — from which she produced, in the first four minutes of being in Doug's apartment, her phone, a laptop, a legal pad, two pens, and a folder with a printed logo that said BANKS MGMT in a font Doug associated with dentist offices.

She shook his hand. Her grip was firm and practiced. She smelled like something — not perfume, something sharper, something that had a name Doug would never know.

"Okay," she said, and she was already past him, already in the living room, scanning. Doug knew that look from damaged houses: first the inventory, then the assessment, then the quiet decision about what was worth what. The bookshelf. The table with three chairs. The print on the wall. The coat hooks by the door with one coat and one jacket, both brown. The kitchen visible through the pass-through, the counter with the cereal box he'd forgotten to put away.

"I love this," Layla said, meaning the apartment, or meaning she was ready to start.

She sat on his couch — the one he'd bought at a Cort furniture clearance eight years ago — and set her phone face-down on the coffee table. She picked it up within ten seconds, checked something, set it down again. This happened three more times before Doug sat in the armchair across from her.

"So," Layla said. "Let me tell you what I'm seeing."

What Layla was seeing, as it turned out, was everything. She talked for twenty-two minutes. Doug knew this because he'd glanced at the microwave clock when she started and glanced again when she paused for the first time, and the difference was twenty-two minutes. In that time, she had outlined what she called "the landscape of opportunity," and the landscape was vast and detailed and apparently ready for immediate construction.

There was the Netflix special. "I've already had preliminary conversations with a development exec there — Charlie Falk, he's great, he totally gets it. They want to meet you. They're thinking a one-hour special, shot somewhere real, not a studio, somewhere that feels like you. Your apartment, maybe. Or not your apartment. We'll figure out the venue."

There was the book deal. "Three publishers have already reached out to me — well, to you, technically, but they can't find you because you don't have representation, which is why you need representation, which is why I'm here. We're talking a humor book, or a memoir, or a humor-memoir hybrid. The category's flexible. The advance would be — I don't want to throw numbers around prematurely because nothing's real until it's real, but we're talking probably mid-six figures. Maybe more. The market for this right now is insane."
