There was the TED talk. "They love regular people with a moment. You're the definition of a moment. We'd position it around, like — I don't know — vulnerability? Honesty? The comedy of being real? We'd figure out the framing. But TED is a door that opens other doors."

There was the corporate speaking circuit. "This is where the money actually is. Pharmaceutical companies, insurance companies — oh my god, you literally work in insurance, that's perfect — tech companies, sales conferences, leadership retreats. They pay between fifteen and forty thousand dollars for a keynote, and you'd be doing, I don't know, two or three a month at first. You don't have to be slick. They don't want slick. They want the toast guy."

There was the CBD endorsement.

"Just hear me out," Layla said, raising both hands.

"I don't know what that is," Doug said. It was the first complete sentence he'd managed in twenty-two minutes.

"It's — okay, it's a wellness brand. They do gummies and tinctures and — the point is, they're looking for a spokesperson who's real, who's relatable, and they specifically asked about you. The money is — it's not huge, maybe twenty or thirty thousand, but it's easy money. You hold a bottle, you say something about relaxing, they put it on Instagram."

"I don't have Instagram."

"We'll get you Instagram. That's — honestly, that's a whole other conversation. Your social media is — I mean, you don't have social media, which is actually kind of amazing as a branding angle but it's a nightmare operationally, so we'll deal with that."

There was also, possibly, a podcast.

"Everyone has a podcast," Doug said.

"Exactly! And none of them are interesting! Yours would be interesting because you're not trying to be interesting, you're just —" She gestured at his apartment, at him, at the space between them, encompassing everything. "This."

Doug sat in the armchair. His hands were on the armrests. The upholstery had a texture like corduroy but wasn't corduroy — Megan had told him the name of the fabric once, years before the clearance sale, when they were still people who bought furniture together, and he'd forgotten the word but remembered the conversation, which was the kind of thing his memory did to him.

"Can I ask a question?" Doug said.

"Absolutely. Yes. Ask me anything."

"Who are your other clients?"

Layla's phone buzzed on the coffee table. She flipped it face-down without looking.

"So, Banks Management is a boutique firm. We're intentionally small because I believe in focused attention and — I have a roster of about twelve clients right now. A few podcast hosts, some branded content creators, and Derek Voss — do you know Derek? He was on The Bachelorette, season — I want to say fourteen? Fifteen? He's doing really well. He has a fitness brand and a supplement line."

"I don't watch The Bachelorette."

"That's fine. That's not the point. The point is, I know this space. I know how to take a moment — what you have right now is a moment — and turn it into a career. Not a flash-in-the-pan thing, not a meme lifecycle. An actual career."

She leaned forward. Her hands were on her knees. Her nails were short and painted a color that Doug's mother would have called "unserious." A silver ring on her right hand, not her left — thin, worn to a smooth shine, the kind of ring you didn't take off.

"Doug. People are going to come for you. They're already coming. Producers, publishers, brands, every talent manager in New York and LA who watched that video and did the math the same way I did. The question isn't whether someone's going to help you navigate this. The question is whether you have someone in your corner who understands how this works, or whether you're sitting here answering calls from people who don't have your best interest at heart."

She paused. Her voice had dropped half a register, and Doug couldn't tell if this was strategy or something closer to honest.

"I have your best interest at heart. I also have my own best interest at heart, and I'm going to be straight about that because I think you can tell when someone's bullshitting you."

"Usually," Doug said.

"I think you'd be great for my company and I think I'd be great for you. I think in six months we could be looking at a — I don't want to say a number, but it would be a number that would change what your year looks like. What your next few years look like." She stopped herself, recalculated. "What do you make now? If you don't mind."

"Sixty-one thousand, four hundred," Doug said. "Before taxes."

Layla absorbed this number. Something happened in her face that she recovered from quickly, a microexpression Doug recognized from claims work — the brief recalculation when a homeowner revealed the full extent of the damage and it was less than the adjuster expected. Not disappointment, exactly. Recalibration.

"Right," she said. "Okay. So."

"So," Doug said.

She picked up her phone, set it down, picked it up, set it down. The fourth time, she left it in her hand.

"Can I be really honest with you?"

"You said you were going to be."

"Yeah, I — okay. Here's what I think. I think you could make more money in the next six months than you've made in the last five years. I think the Netflix deal alone could be — I really don't want to say numbers until something's signed. But significant. And the corporates, the book, the — look, I know this is a lot. I know I've been sitting in your apartment for twenty minutes telling you everything's about to change, and you're probably thinking, who is this person and why should I trust her."

Doug wasn't thinking that, exactly. He was thinking about the number five years, which at his salary was roughly three hundred and seven thousand dollars, and about the microwave clock and whether she'd notice if he looked at it again. Then he wasn't thinking about either of those things. He was thinking about how Layla Banks had said his name on the phone — "Doug. Doug Bridge" — with a certainty that made him think she'd been repeating it to herself for days, practicing it because it mattered.

"Okay," Doug said.

"Okay what?"

"I'm interested."

Layla's whole body changed. She sat up straighter. She smiled with her whole face, not just the professional part, and Doug could see the strategy in it and something else he wanted to believe wasn't strategy. It didn't matter that he couldn't separate the two.

"The CBD thing," Doug said. "I'm not sure about the CBD thing."

"Then we don't do gummies. That's side money. I'm talking about the stuff that actually moves your life: Netflix, the book, corporates. That's what this is."

"And the TED talk."

"And the TED talk, eventually. TED moves slow. TED is a longer play."

"And the podcast."

"Okay, the podcast is — honestly, the podcast is just an idea I had on the flight. It might not — we'd figure out the podcast later. The podcast is phase two."

"How many phases are there?"

"There aren't official phases. I'm using 'phases' loosely. The point is, we'd start with the big-ticket items and build from there. You don't have to say yes to everything tonight. You just have to say yes to me."

Doug looked at her. She was leaning forward on his couch, phone in hand, the folder with BANKS MGMT on her lap, and for once she wasn't talking. The thought came to him gradually, factually, without drama: he might not be the only person in the room being offered something they wanted. Layla Banks ran a twelve-person management company out of New York. Her biggest client had been on a reality dating show three or four seasons ago. She was sitting in a two-bedroom apartment in Clintonville on a Thursday afternoon, on a couch that cost four hundred dollars and smelled faintly of its previous home.

She needed this too. Maybe more than he did.

"Okay," Doug said. "Yes."

"Yes?"

"Yes."

"Okay! Oh my god, okay." She was already opening the folder, unclipping something, sliding pages across the coffee table. "I have the paperwork. It's standard representation. I brought it because I was hopeful, but I also didn't want to fly home and have to FedEx it, so —"

She placed the contract in front of him. It was eleven pages, held together with a black binder clip.

---

Doug picked up the contract and read it.

This was not what Layla expected. He could tell because she shifted on the couch three times in the first minute, crossed and uncrossed her legs, and picked up her phone twice without looking at it — a new behavior. Previously she'd at least checked the screen. By page three, the pen in her hand was clicking open and shut against her thumb. He'd seen homeowners get to the same place while he read policies in their living rooms: respect cut with irritation, the irritation winning.

"It's pretty standard," she said, when he was on page four.

"Mmhm," Doug said.

He read in full: every word, every clause, every defined term tracked back to its first appearance. This was the fundamental skill of his profession, the competence on which his career rested: reading a document past the point where most people stopped. Doug had processed over four thousand claims in his time at Mutual Fidelity. He had read policies, endorsements, riders, amendments, proofs of loss, contractor estimates, and enough subrogation correspondence to fill the second bedroom twice over, which was saying something because the second bedroom was already full. He did not skim. He did not flip to the signature page. He read.

The contract was with Banks Management LLC, a New York limited liability company. It established a management relationship granting Layla Banks exclusive representation rights for all entertainment, media, publishing, and endorsement opportunities. The commission structure was twenty percent of gross revenue on entertainment and media, fifteen percent on publishing, and twenty-five percent on endorsements. The term was two years with automatic renewal unless terminated by either party with sixty days' written notice. The exclusivity clause was broad. The termination provisions were narrow.

"Page six," Doug said. "The indemnification clause."

"What about it?"

"It says I indemnify you against any claims arising from my public appearances. That's one-directional. If you book me somewhere and the venue has a liability issue, or if there's a contractual dispute with a third party you introduced, I'm the one carrying the exposure."

Layla blinked. "That's — I mean, that's standard language. Every management contract —"

"It's standard because most people don't read it. Can we make it mutual?"

"Mutual?"

"If I'm indemnifying you, you indemnify me. Same language, reversed. My exposure shouldn't be asymmetric."

Layla looked at him for a long moment. Then she pulled out one of her pens and wrote something on her legal pad. "Okay. Sure. We can do mutual indemnification. What else?"

"Page eight. The revenue definition. It says 'gross revenue from all professional activities entered into during the term of this agreement.' All professional activities."

"Right."

"Does that include my job?"

"Your — at the insurance company?"

"At Mutual Fidelity. If I keep working there — and I'm going to keep working there — this language could be read to include my salary as a professional activity entered into during the term. You'd be entitled to twenty percent of sixty-one thousand, four hundred dollars."

Layla opened her mouth, closed it, and tilted her head at him. For the first time since she'd arrived, she didn't have the next sentence ready. Then she pulled the legal pad closer.

"I would never — that's not the intent, Doug."

"The intent isn't in the contract. The language is in the contract. Can we add an exclusion for pre-existing employment income?"

She wrote on the legal pad again. Faster this time. "Absolutely. We'll carve that out. What else?"

Doug turned to page nine. The apartment was quiet except for the refrigerator, which had a hum that Doug had stopped hearing three years ago but which reasserted itself in silences like this one. Layla sat on the couch. She was not checking her phone. This was, Doug would later realize, the longest he ever saw Layla Banks sit still.

On page nine he found the termination clause. Either party could terminate with sixty days' written notice, but termination did not end the commission obligation — Layla would continue to receive her percentage on all deals initiated during the term, regardless of when revenue was received. Doug recognized this structure. He'd seen it in commercial insurance brokerage agreements: a trailing commission designed to protect the broker after the relationship ended. It was reasonable in principle. In application, it could go on forever.

"The trailing commission," Doug said. "After termination, you receive your percentage on deals negotiated during the term. For how long?"

"For the life of the deal."

"So if you negotiate a book contract in month one and I terminate in month three, you receive fifteen percent of that book's earnings for as long as the book earns?"

"That's how these work."

"I believe you. I'd like to cap it at twenty-four months post-termination."

"Doug."

"It's a trailing commission on a management contract, not a life estate."

Layla stared at him long enough for the refrigerator to take over the room. She bit the inside of her cheek, jaw working. When she answered, she did it slower than she'd said anything all afternoon.

"Thirty-six months," she said.

"Twenty-four."

"Thirty. Final."

Doug considered this. Thirty months was reasonable. It gave Layla protection on deals she'd invested time in while giving him a defined exit. He'd seen worse terms in commercial brokerage. He'd also seen better, but those were negotiated by attorneys billing four hundred dollars an hour, not by insurance adjusters sitting in armchairs on a Thursday evening with cold cereal on the counter.

"Thirty months," Doug said. "With the mutual indemnification and the employment income carve-out."

"Done."

She wrote it down. Her handwriting was fast and angular, the letters connected by strokes that were more intention than ink. She looked up.

"Anything on ten and eleven?"

Doug read pages ten and eleven. Page ten was a standard confidentiality provision — mutual, limited to proprietary business information, with a two-year survival period. Reasonable. Page eleven was signature blocks and a choice-of-law clause specifying New York.

"The governing law is New York," Doug said.

"That's where the company's organized."

"I'm in Ohio."

"Right, but it's — the contract is with a New York LLC. Every one of my agreements uses New York law."

Doug looked at the clause. In insurance, choice-of-law provisions could determine the outcome of a coverage dispute. Which state's regulations applied, which courts had jurisdiction, what standards governed interpretation — these were real decisions with real consequences. But this was a management contract, not a commercial liability policy, and the practical difference between Ohio and New York contract law for a document this size was marginal. He was not going to fight for this one.

"Fine," he said. "The rest is fine."

Layla let out a breath she'd been holding since page six. She put her pen down, sat back, and for a second didn't reach for her phone or her folder or the next point in the pitch. It was the same pause Roy took when a claim file turned out to be unusually complete: respect edged with the discomfort of having underestimated somebody.

"You know," she said, "most of my clients sign these in about thirty seconds."

"Most of your clients are podcast hosts."

She laughed, short and rough enough that she put a hand to her mouth and looked at the floor. When she looked back up, the laugh was gone and the contract was back in her hands.

"Okay," she said. "Let's make it ugly."

She flipped to page six, drew a line through the indemnification language, wrote MUTUAL in the margin, and initialed it. On page eight she wrote EXCLUDES PRE-EXISTING EMPLOYMENT INCOME beside the revenue definition and initialed that too. On page nine she crossed out the open-ended trailing commission language, wrote 30 MONTHS above it, and initialed the change with a fast, angular L.B.

"My attorney will clean this up on Monday," she said. "But if we both sign this version, this is the deal."

Doug read each handwritten change. He checked the page numbers. He checked the signature block.

"You'll sign it too," he said.

"Yes," she said. "Doug. Yes."

He initialed page six, page eight, and page nine. Then he signed page eleven above his printed name, slowly enough that the black ink pooled at the downstrokes. Layla signed beneath him without rereading anything. She clipped the pages back together and slid the contract into her folder.

"Okay," she said, and the speed was back in her voice. "We're signed. I'll have the clean copy in your inbox Monday, and I'll set up the Netflix meeting first thing. Charlie's been waiting. The book publishers can wait another week but not much longer because the window on this kind of opportunity is — it's not forever. The interest is real but interest decays. We need to move."

"How fast?"

"Fast. Not reckless fast. Strategic fast."

She pulled on her jacket. Doug stood up. They were in his living room, which had never felt particularly small until someone with Layla's velocity was in it. She took up more space than her body accounted for. The room had to reorganize itself around her.

"I'm staying at the Hilton downtown," she said. "I fly back tomorrow afternoon. If you think of anything between now and Monday, call me. I answer my phone."

"I know," Doug said. "You picked up before the first ring."

"Yeah, I — I was waiting for you to call back. I'm going to be honest about that. I've been waiting for you to call for two weeks."

She said it without the pitch rhythm, without the forward lean. Just a sentence, flat and unadorned, delivered at the door of his apartment while she adjusted the strap of her bag. For once there was no next idea arriving behind it. Doug believed her. Or wanted to. He pictured the small company, the twelve clients, the former Bachelorette contestant with the fitness brand, and her phone lighting up over and over with numbers that weren't his. He had no way to know if the last two weeks had looked like that. It was what he saw anyway.

"Thank you for coming out here," Doug said.

"Thank you for picking up the phone, babe. I'll talk to you Monday."

And she was through the door, and the folder with his signature in it was under her arm and then in the hallway and then gone. The apartment returned to its normal dimensions. The refrigerator hummed. The bookshelf sagged on the left. The cereal box was still open on the counter.

He went to the kitchen. There was a black streak of ink along the side of his hand where the pen had dragged when he signed. The terms were still in his head: twenty percent on entertainment and media, fifteen on publishing, twenty-five on endorsements, exclusivity, automatic renewal, mutual indemnification, the salary carve-out, thirty months on the tail, New York law.

He poured a bowl of Grape-Nuts, left a thumbprint on the side of the bowl, and ate standing at the counter.


# Chapter 5

Doug got to Worthington twenty minutes early because he always got anywhere twenty minutes early. It was a claims habit, left over from years of showing up first to assess the site, note the conditions before anyone else's presence altered them. Kelsey had picked the diner on High Street — neutral ground, she'd said, which implied that Doug's apartment and her apartment and every restaurant they'd ever been to together were charged territory now, places where the toast might follow them in. He'd agreed because he agreed with most things Kelsey suggested, and because the last time he'd disagreed with her about a restaurant she'd been fourteen and the argument had been about Applebee's and he'd lost.

The conditions here were a half-full diner with vinyl booths, a specials board in faded chalk that listed a turkey melt and a soup that was either cream of mushroom or cream of chicken depending on how you read the handwriting, and a draft coming under the front door that carried the smell of road salt and wet asphalt. February in Columbus. Not brutal, just relentless — a cold that didn't argue with you, just waited.

He chose a booth near the back, away from the window, which turned out poorly because the kitchen door swung open every thirty seconds and he could hear the fryer pop and plates being stacked and a cook saying something muffled and laughing at it alone. He ordered coffee. The waitress was a woman around his age, heavyset, with a brace on her left wrist and a pen tucked behind each ear — both blue, like she'd been burned once by running out of ink and had decided it wasn't going to happen again. She brought the coffee in a mug that said OHIO IS FOR LOVERS, which was wrong about Ohio in at least two ways Doug could think of.

He wrapped his hands around the mug. The heat was good. He sat there for eleven minutes watching the front door and thinking about what he was going to say and not arriving at anything that worked, which was how most of his important conversations began.

Kelsey came in at seven past noon. She was wearing a coat he didn't recognize — dark green, longer than what she usually wore, the collar up high on her neck — and her hair was pulled back tight, the no-nonsense configuration she wore at work, a woman who spent her days leaning over open mouths. She spotted him and didn't wave. She walked to the booth, sat down across from him, put her phone face-down on the table, and said, "I left at nine. The drive was shit."

"Road work?"

"No, the road was fine. The drive was shit because I spent two hours thinking about what I'm supposed to say to you and I still don't have it."

Doug nodded. He'd spent the morning doing roughly the same thing, except his commute from Clintonville was twelve minutes, and he'd filled the remaining time rearranging the salt and pepper shakers on his kitchen counter as if their alignment might produce a script for this conversation.

The waitress came back. Kelsey ordered a club sandwich and a Diet Coke without looking at the menu. Doug ordered a BLT because he'd been staring at the specials for eleven minutes and the BLT was the only item that didn't require a decision about sides or bread type. The waitress wrote it down left-handed, the brace adjusting her grip slightly off-angle, and Doug noticed that the brace looked new and clean, which meant the injury was recent, which meant she was still figuring out the small recalibrations — the grip and reach and angle — that came with working around a part of your body that had stopped doing what it used to do.

"I'm sorry," Doug said, when they were alone.

"Okay."

"I mean it."

"I know you mean it, Dad. You've been sorry since the day after. Sorry isn't really the question."

She picked up a sugar packet from the caddy and turned it over between her fingers — front, back, front — not opening it, just giving her hands something to do. He recognized this. She'd done it with erasers as a kid, her phone case as a teenager. Kelsey's anger always went to her hands first.

"What's the question?" he said.

"I don't know yet. That's what I drove two hours to figure out." She dropped the sugar packet. "Can I just tell you what it's been like? The last two weeks. Can I just do that?"

"Yeah."

"Twenty-three hundred," she said. "Tags. Not mentions — tags. People tagging me in posts about your video. People I went to high school with. People I've never met. My coworker Jen — you've met Jen, short, has the bangs — she screenshot a TikTok reaction video, some guy doing commentary over the toast, and she put it in the work group chat. With a heart emoji. She thought she was being supportive."

"That's —"

"I'm not done." Her voice didn't rise. It dropped, and the drop was worse. "A patient. I had a patient in the chair, bib clipped on, I'm in there with the scaler doing a subgingival, and she goes, 'Oh my God, are you Wedding Toast Girl?' My hand is in this woman's mouth. She's trying to talk around a scaler and she's telling me she cried, she thought it was so moving, she's actually tearing up while I've got calculus on my instrument and I'm just standing there with my hand in a stranger's mouth listening to her cry about my dad."

Doug looked at the table. There was a ring of dried coffee near his placemat, a faint circle, residue you'd document in a claims photograph if the surface were insured.

"Tyler's mother," Kelsey said. She'd gone quiet, and with Kelsey the quiet was always where the real pressure lived. Not the volume — the precision. "She sent me a text. Three sentences. 'We saw your father's speech. We understand.' She italicized 'understand.'"

"You can italicize in a —"

"Yes, Dad, you can italicize in a text message. That's not the point." She looked at him across the table with the particular steadiness she deployed when he did this — when he retreated into the small technical question because the larger one was sitting right there and he didn't know how to pick it up. "Tyler's mother. Who I sat next to at the rehearsal dinner for forty minutes while she told me about her bridge club and her hip replacement and her opinions on the asparagus. Who I've met four times. And she understands now. She understands our family. Because you got drunk and talked into a microphone about things you've never said to any of us sober."

The food arrived. The waitress set down the plates with her braced left hand, compensating slightly, the BLT landing a half-inch off the placemat. Doug thanked her. Kelsey didn't look up.

They ate. Or they went through the motions. Doug picked up a triangle of the BLT. The bacon was cold and rigid, one strip hanging past the bread like it was trying to leave the sandwich. He ate it anyway. The lettuce was brown at the edges. The tomato was the pale pink of February tomatoes, shipped from somewhere warmer and emptied of whatever they'd been by the trip.

Kelsey dismantled the club sandwich. She ate the turkey layer first. She'd been doing this since she was twelve, when she'd decided that sandwiches made more sense as components — disassembled and consumed in order of preference. Turkey first, then bacon, then bread and lettuce last, which she liked least. It was a detail Doug knew about his daughter, part of the private archive he'd accumulated over twenty-eight years: turkey first, her too-tight grip on a pen, the scar on her left knee from a bike pedal in fourth grade, the fact that she always parked facing out because she'd read somewhere it was safer. He catalogued these things thoroughly and precisely and never quite understood what they added up to.

"How's work otherwise?" he said, because the silence between bites was getting heavy and he didn't know how to hold it yet.

"It's fine. It's teeth."

"Right."

She chewed. He chewed. The kitchen door swung. Somewhere behind it a plate broke and someone said "shit" and someone else laughed and the normal operations of a diner on a Wednesday in February continued without regard for what was happening in the back booth.

"Can I ask you something?" Kelsey said.

"Yeah."

"The thing you said about the table — was that real?"

Doug's hands went still on the sandwich. The table. She was asking about the table — the table in the kitchen of the house on Briarwood, the maple table they'd bought when Kelsey was four from a furniture store that no longer existed, a table with a gouge near one leg where the delivery men had caught the doorframe and a long pale scratch across the top from Kelsey's protractor in seventh grade. She'd been horrified about the scratch. He'd told her it was fine, it gave the table character. And in the toast, eight whiskeys deep and still naming things exactly, he'd said something about that scratch — he couldn't remember the exact words, the eight whiskeys had turned the evening into a landscape seen through rain — but he remembered the word "protractor," because even then he was the kind of man who named the instrument, and he remembered something about the scratch being the only lasting mark he'd made alongside anybody. Or close to that. Close enough that it was on the internet and his daughter was sitting across from him asking if it was real.

"I was pretty far gone," he said.

"That's not what I asked."

"I know."

"Was it real? What you said about the scratch. The thing about — you know what you said."

Doug looked at the salt shaker. It was glass with a chrome lid, and the salt inside had clumped at the bottom from humidity, a solid mass where the granules had fused into something denser than they were meant to be. He focused on the salt and not on the table and not on the scratch and not on the house on Briarwood that Megan had sold fourteen months after she left, to a couple from Westerville who didn't know about the protractor, who would sand the mark smooth or cover it with a placemat or not notice it at all.

"I don't remember exactly what I said," Doug told her. This was a lie. It was the specific lie of a man who remembered precisely what he'd said and could not say it again sober because saying it sober would mean he'd chosen to, and choosing was different from the words walking out of you at a wedding reception while the room was still recovering from your ex-wife's husband's perfect toast. Choosing meant it was yours. Drunk, it was an accident. He could live with an accident.

Kelsey watched him. Her eyes were her mother's — dark, level, capable of holding a gaze longer than was comfortable as a form of pressure all its own. She knew he was deflecting. She had spent a lifetime watching him deflect. His retreats into literalness had a pattern. The technical answer arrived first, and the real answer sat beside it untouched.

She set down the piece of bread she was holding.

She folded her hands on the table.

And then neither of them spoke.

The diner went on around them. The kitchen door swung and the fryer crackled. Two booths over, a couple in their sixties argued about a nephew — the word "irresponsible" recurring at intervals like a metronome. The waitress passed with a pot of coffee and refilled Doug's mug without asking, which he registered as both a kindness and an intrusion but couldn't manage to say anything about either way. At the counter, a man was eating soup and reading his phone, and the sound of his spoon against the bowl was steady and small and regular, measuring time in cream of mushroom. The front door opened and cold air rushed in and someone said "Jesus, it's cold" and someone else answered "February" as if that were an explanation, which in Columbus it was.

A song played on the diner's radio — something country, a truck and a dirt road, a woman who left. Then Fleetwood Mac, "Landslide," which was so on-the-nose it had to be coincidence. Then a commercial for a dealership in Delaware, Ohio, zero percent financing, which Doug happened to know meant the interest was folded into the sticker price — useful information for no one in this booth at this moment.

Five minutes. Maybe six. He didn't look at his phone and neither did she.

The refill sat untouched long enough to skin over. Kelsey kept her hands folded until the ice in her Diet Coke gave up and settled. When she picked her sandwich back up, she started with the bread, the part she liked least.

"Tyler says hi," she said.

"Tell him hi."

"He liked the toast. He thought it was funny."

"Tyler did?"

"He laughed. I don't know at which part. I didn't take a survey." She shrugged. She was coming back to the surface, reassembling the normal shapes of conversation around herself. "Maybe it's funny if you're not in it."

"Maybe."

"Anyway." She wadded her napkin and dropped it on her plate. "I should go. I told Ty I'd be home by five."

"Drive safe."

"I always drive safe."

"I know you do."

She stood. She pulled on the green coat, zipped it to her chin. She was shorter than he expected — she was always shorter than he expected, because he carried in his mind a Kelsey who had kept growing past five-four, which she hadn't, same height as Megan, same height as Megan's mother, a genetic line that had nothing to do with anything and that he thought about now because thinking about his daughter's height was easier than thinking about the silence that was still sitting in the booth between them.

"The BLT was pretty good," he said.

Kelsey looked at him. And then she laughed — short, barely a sound, escaping before she'd decided whether to allow it. "God, Dad."

"What?"

"Nothing. The BLT was pretty good." She shook her head. She put a twenty on the table and when he reached for his wallet she said "Don't" in a voice that closed the discussion. Then she walked out of the diner without looking back.

He watched her through the window, crossing the parking lot in the dark green coat. She got into a silver Civic — she drove a Civic too, a newer model than his, because she'd replaced the one he'd given her at graduation with the same make and he didn't know whether that was loyalty or habit or the simple fact that Civics held their resale value, and he preferred to think about resale values rather than about the silence and the table and the five minutes during which his daughter had sat across from him knowing everything he couldn't say and letting the not-saying be enough.

The waitress brought the check. $23.40. He left the twenty Kelsey had put down and added twelve of his own, because the waitress had the brace and the two pens and had refilled his coffee without asking and he didn't know what else he could do for anyone today, so he could at least do this.

---

He called Megan at 4:30. He was sitting on the couch in the dark. He hadn't turned on the lights when he got home, and the February afternoon had already given out — the transition from gray to black so gradual it didn't register as a change, more like the gray just thickening until it was opaque. His phone was at twenty-two percent. He should have charged it first, but the charger was in the kitchen and the cord was too short to reach anywhere comfortable, and he'd spent the two hours since Worthington sitting on this couch building up to this call, and if he moved he might lose whatever fragile momentum he'd managed.

On the drive back from Worthington he'd passed the turn for Briarwood and kept going. The house had belonged to other people for twelve years, but he could still place the table in the old kitchen exactly: maple top, one leg gouged by the delivery men, the pale line Kelsey had cut into it in seventh grade by pressing too hard with a protractor and dragging. He'd covered the scratch with a placemat for a week because she'd been so upset, then stopped because the mark was there whether you acknowledged it or not. The toast had taken that line in the wood and put it in front of people who had no business knowing it existed.

She picked up on the second ring. "Doug."

"Hey."

"Hi." Behind her, a cabinet closed, then something touched the counter with a careful ceramic click. She was in her kitchen. Brendan's kitchen. The one with the island and the six-burner range and the herb pots on the windowsill in labeled ceramic containers, because in Brendan's house even the basil had a system. "I had a feeling you'd call."

"Yeah. I wanted to apologize. For the toast. For talking about the table. About Briarwood."

"I appreciate you saying that." Her voice was measured, each word given room. "But it wasn't vague, Doug. You said the maple table in the Briarwood kitchen still had the scratch Kelsey made with a protractor. You said you spent the last year of the marriage looking at that line because at least it was a visible thing. A mark you could point to. Then you said the scratch lasted longer than we did. People heard that."

Doug pressed his thumbnail into the arm of the couch. The upholstery had pilled in the places where his hands rested — eight years of the same position on the same cushion producing small hard knots of polyester fiber. He focused on one, about the size of a lentil.

"I don't remember it that clean."

"I do. Or close enough that the difference doesn't help either of us." Another soft cabinet close. "This isn't about me being furious. I've had two weeks to sit with it, and I do not think that speech invented anything. You'd been drinking. Brendan had just spoken. The room was already emotional. Then you said the thing neither of us had ever said in public."

"You're saying his toast set me off."

"I'm saying the night was already open, and then you stopped containing yourself."

"I wasn't trying to make you look bad."

"I know." Something small met the counter on her end of the line. "If you'd stood up and blamed me, this would actually be simpler. What was hard about it was that you didn't. You stood there and told a room full of people you could keep track of damage in a piece of furniture better than you could admit your marriage was going bad."

"How's Brendan?" he heard himself ask, which was not what he'd intended to say and not a question he wanted answered.

"He's good. He's at the hospital tonight." He heard a drawer slide shut. "He watched the video, Doug. More than once. He had a reaction to it, and I'd rather say it than let it come in sideways."

"I know."

Then she said, "He kept calling it brave."

The word arrived in Doug's dark apartment and just sat there.

Brave.

Brendan Coyle — orthopedic surgeon, weekend triathlete, a man whose own toast at the same wedding had been polished and warm and had brought the room to tears at precisely the right moment because Brendan understood pacing and Doug understood depreciation schedules — thought that Doug's drunken seven-minute catastrophe was brave.

Doug pressed the fabric pill flat with his thumbnail. It sprang back.

"He said most people never let their own family hear that much," Megan continued. "That was the part he respected."

Doug tried to locate what he felt. It wasn't one thing — it was a stack, multiple items on the same claim, each requiring separate assessment. Gratitude, because Brendan was being kind and the kindness was real. Humiliation, because the kindness came from the man who had his wife and his house and his composure and his herb pots. And something else underneath those, harder to file — the specific weight of being admired by the person whose life was the clearest evidence of everything you'd lost. Brave implied he'd chosen to do it. Brave implied he'd stood up at that podium and decided, sober and deliberate, to tell the truth. He hadn't chosen anything. He'd been eight whiskeys past the last checkpoint and the truth had walked out on its own, and calling that brave was like complimenting a burst pipe for the water pressure.

"It wasn't brave," Doug said.

"No?"

"It was bad timing. Whiskey. A microphone."

"All right," Megan said. "Those things are also true."

He could have let that be the end of it. Instead he said, "I signed with a manager."

There was a beat on the line before Megan said, "Layla Banks?"

"Yeah."

"For the toast."

"For whatever this is now. There are meetings. A Netflix development thing. Some speaking offers."

Another cabinet, this one opened and shut. The small domestic sounds in that kitchen irritated him on principle. "All right," Megan said again. "Then I need to say something plainly."

Doug waited.

"If you decide to do this publicly, our marriage cannot become the part you use when the room gets quiet. And Kelsey cannot keep being the person who pays for it after."

"I wasn't planning to use Kelsey."

"I know that." The line sat between them for a second. "Intent is not really the relevant issue anymore."

He looked across the dark apartment toward the kitchen counter, the charger coiled there like a short white snake. "You think I'd do that."

"I think you do not always know what you'll say once people are looking at you."

The sentence landed with the same dull force as Kelsey's patient in the dental chair, Tyler's mother understanding the family now, the waitress's brace, the twenty on the table, every small piece of the day that had turned into evidence against him. He pressed the fabric pill flat again. It sprang back again.

"Was it true?" he said.

The line stayed quiet long enough that he looked at the screen before Megan said, "What part?"

"That it lasted longer than we did. That we were already done and I was the only one still sitting there."

"No," she said. "Not the only one. I was sitting there too."

He closed his eyes. The couch cushion shifted under his weight, old foam compressing where it had compressed a thousand times before.

"That was the worst part of hearing it," Megan said. "Not that it was false. That it was public."

"If Kelsey asks you again what that line meant," Megan said, "I think you probably owe her a sober answer."

"Take care of yourself," she said.

"Yeah. You too."

He hung up. The apartment was fully dark. His phone said 4:53, the screen glowing in the room like something left on by mistake. He got up and plugged it into the kitchen charger and stood there at the counter while the little lightning bolt appeared on the screen, and the apartment had its usual quiet — not peaceful, not hostile, just the sound of a space with one person in it and room for more.

He stood there another minute with his hand still on the phone, trying to picture the wedding from Kelsey's table instead of the podium. His jacket was off. His tie was uneven. Tyler's mother and Brendan's colleagues and whatever cousins had filmed him all sitting there while he told the story of the scratch as if a piece of damaged wood could explain eighteen years of marriage. The shame in it was not that the story was false. It was that he'd made the two people most trapped inside it hear it with an audience, and maybe laugh too.

He opened the refrigerator. Milk. Condiments. Half a sleeve of saltines from a stomach bug in January. One egg that had rolled to the far back corner of the shelf and sat there under the light, small and solitary.

He closed the refrigerator and picked up the phone again. He opened his text thread with Kelsey. Her last message was from that morning, saying she'd be there a little after noon. He typed, "It was real." The three words sat in the box under her name. He looked at them until the screen dimmed, then deleted them and set the phone back by the charger.

Doug walked to the bedroom. He took off his shoes and placed them by the door — left, right, aligned with the baseboard, because he was a man who put things in order even when nothing else was — and lay down on the bed in his clothes. The ceiling had a water stain directly above him, a ring of discoloration from the tenant upstairs, something he'd noticed when he moved in twelve years ago and had never reported. He knew what caused it — a slow leak, probably a supply line fitting, a failure that cost maybe eighty dollars to fix and three hundred in drywall if you waited. He'd waited twelve years. The stain was roughly the shape of Ohio, which was either fitting or coincidence, and he went with coincidence, because the alternative — that his ceiling had developed opinions about his life — was a thing that happened in movies, not in two-bedroom apartments in Clintonville.

From the kitchen came the small buzz of the phone against the counter, then another a minute later. He let it buzz. He lay there looking at the stain until he could no longer make out Ohio in it, only the darker ring at the edge where the water kept finding the same path.


# Chapter 6

The phone buzzing on his kitchen counter had led him, somehow, to Los Angeles. He had never been there. He had never had a reason to go. The farthest west he'd traveled was Denver, for a claims seminar in 2017, and Denver had been fine in the way that most places were fine — the hotel had a lobby, the conference room had a projector and a bowl of individually wrapped mints, and the airport was a building he left through. Los Angeles was not Denver.

The first thing he noticed was the light. Not that it was sunny — Columbus had sun, sometimes, and Doug was not opposed to sun — but that light in Los Angeles held a harder edge. Stepping out of the terminal at LAX, he could see the exact outline of his shadow on the concrete. Light stopped. Shade started. In Columbus, in March, shadows bled at the edges. Gray smears that could have been a cloud, a building, a guess. Here his shadow was precise enough to trace.

Layla had arranged a car, which turned out to be a man named Dmitri in a black Escalade who held a sign with Doug's name spelled "Doug Brigge" and drove with one hand on the wheel and the other on his phone, texting in what looked like Russian at a speed that suggested either the conversation was urgent or Dmitri simply drove and texted at the same rate, which was fast. He had a small laminated photo of a girl, maybe five or six, clipped to his sun visor. At red lights his eyes flicked up to it and back to traffic, and the second a gap opened he punched the Escalade into it. He asked Doug where he was visiting from. Doug said Columbus. Dmitri said, "Ohio?" Doug said yes. Dmitri nodded once and did not ask a follow-up question, which Doug appreciated.

The freeway had seven lanes. Doug counted them. Seven lanes of traffic moving at what appeared to be both very fast and completely stationary, depending on which lane you looked at, and Dmitri threaded the Escalade through them without hesitation, cutting into openings that looked theoretical until the SUV was already in them. Outside the windows, the city slid by in a blur of low buildings and strip malls until Doug started noticing the plants. Palm trees lined the boulevard, their trunks bare until the burst of leaves at the top. Along the concrete walls bordering the freeway, bougainvillea poured over fences and climbed walls, bright pink and purple in March. In Columbus, in March, nothing bloomed. Things were still thinking about it. Here the flowers had already started without waiting for permission.

The hotel was in Silver Lake, which Layla had described as "totally your vibe" in a text, a phrase Doug had looked at for a long time without understanding. The building was two stories, painted a gray that someone had probably called "concrete" or "ash" on a paint chip, and had a sign that was just the hotel's name in lowercase letters without punctuation: the wilding. Not The Wilding. Just the wilding, all lowercase. Doug assumed this was deliberate because everything else in the neighborhood seemed deliberate.

The woman at the front desk had short hair and earrings made from what appeared to be tiny pieces of driftwood. She was maybe thirty, and she wore a linen shirt that was either wrinkled by design or had been ironed with visible reluctance. She called Doug "love" when she handed him his key card, which was not a plastic card but a matte black rectangle with no visible markings. "You're in room seven, love. Up the stairs and to the left. There's cold brew in the lobby fridge, and we've got a sound bath in the garden at six if you want to decompress from your travel."

Doug did not know what a sound bath was. He did not ask. He took the matte black rectangle and went up the stairs.

The room had exposed brick on one wall, which Doug understood as a design choice rather than a construction deficiency, though his instinct was to assess the mortar joints, which looked original and were, in his professional opinion, due for repointing within five years. The bed was low to the ground and made with white sheets that had the crisp authority of sheets that had never been slept in, or had been washed at a temperature that removed all evidence of prior use. There were no closet doors. There was a closet — a rectangular alcove built into the wall with a rod and four wooden hangers and a folded white robe — but where doors would normally be there was nothing. Just the opening. Doug's bag had his suit in it, the same suit he'd worn to the wedding, the one Layla had told him to bring because "you might need it, babe, you never know," and he hung it on one of the wooden hangers and watched it hang there, visible from the bed, from the desk, from the bathroom doorway. You could not not see the closet. The suit looked like a person standing in the alcove, waiting to be worn by someone who didn't live here. This was apparently the point.

The bathroom had a shower made entirely of glass — floor, walls, door, all of it, the kind of arrangement that meant the person showering had no barrier between their body and the rest of the room. In Doug's apartment in Clintonville, the shower was glass too, but it was old glass in an aluminum frame, cloudy at the bottom from hard water and set in the corner behind a sliding door that closed all the way. Nobody had ever been in a position to observe him showering there, which was exactly how he preferred it.

On the nightstand, next to a small succulent in a clay pot: a book called *The Surrender Experiment*. Doug picked it up. The cover had a picture of a calm-faced author, with the kind of expression that suggested either genuine inner peace or a significant financial investment in appearing calm. The subtitle was "My Journey into Life's Perfection." Doug read the first sentence, which was about the author sitting in meditation and realizing that his personal preferences were the source of all his problems. He put the book down. He had not considered that his personal preferences were a problem. He had considered them preferences.

He sat on the edge of the low bed and looked at the room. The exposed brick. The doorless closet with his suit hanging in it. The glass shower he could see from where he sat. The book about surrendering. The succulent, which was alive and seemed to be doing fine without anyone attending to it, which was maybe the point of succulents.

His phone buzzed. Layla: "How's the hotel?? Isn't it incredible??"

Doug typed: "There are no doors on the closet."

Layla: "OMG I know right?? It's so Silver Lake. Get some rest babe, big day tomorrow!! Meeting's at 2. I'll pick you up at 1."

Doug looked at the time. It was 4:30 in the afternoon, Pacific. 7:30 in Columbus. He usually ate dinner at 7:30, and dinner was usually cereal. He was hungry. He went downstairs. The woman at the desk — her name was either Marin or Maren; the tag was in cursive that blurred the difference — directed him to a restaurant three blocks away. "They do amazing small plates," she said.

He walked three blocks through a neighborhood where every storefront had a single word for its name — Provision, Botanica, Yield — and nothing in the windows that indicated what was sold inside. A vine with orange flowers he'd never seen before grew straight out of a crack in a retaining wall and kept going. The restaurant was Provision, and the menu was written on a chalkboard in handwriting so deliberate it might have been a font.

He ordered a chicken dish with something called "charred broccolini" and a grain he couldn't identify. The waitress asked if he wanted "still or sparkling." He said "tap." She paused, wrote it down, and moved on. The bill was $34 for one plate and a water. In Columbus, $34 bought him and Roy lunch for three days, pickles included. He paid it and walked back to the hotel in sixty-eight-degree air at seven o'clock in March. In Columbus that temperature belonged to July. His body kept waiting for the evening to correct itself.

He went to bed at nine o'clock Pacific, which was midnight his time. He brushed his teeth in a sink made of concrete — actual concrete, the kind used for foundations, formed into a basin — and looked at his face in a mirror that was larger than the mirror in his apartment and somehow showed more of him than he wanted to see. He got into bed. The sheets were smooth and cool. Through the doorless closet opening, his suit hung in the dark, a familiar shape in an unfamiliar room, shoulders slightly off because the wooden hangers were wider than his actual shoulders. He closed his eyes. Somewhere outside, a dog barked twice and then stopped, and the silence that followed was warmer and softer than any silence in Columbus, where March silence had a metallic quality, the sound of cold air holding still.

---

The Netflix office was not what Doug expected, though he wasn't sure what he'd expected, since his exposure to the entertainment industry was limited to a documentary about the Fyre Festival that Roy had described to him in such detail that Doug felt he'd seen it, even though Roy didn't watch documentaries, which meant Roy had heard about it from someone who had, and the secondhand summary had arrived at Doug's desk at Mutual Fidelity fully intact.

The building was glass and pale wood and had a lobby that felt more like a hotel lobby than the hotel's lobby had. A woman at the front desk gave Doug a visitor badge on a lanyard and told him to wait, and while he waited he sat on a couch that was either very expensive or an extremely convincing reproduction of something very expensive, and next to the couch there was a small table with a bowl of tangerines on it, and Doug ate one because he hadn't had lunch. He hadn't had lunch because the meeting was at two — which was apparently when people in Los Angeles ate lunch, not met about television — and Layla had picked him up at twelve-thirty instead of one, talking on her phone in the back seat of another black car about someone named Marcus who was "literally on fire right now, not in a bad way, in a brand way." Doug had watched Silver Lake turn into a freeway turn into another neighborhood turn into the Netflix parking lot with his stomach empty and a tangerine peel in his hand and nowhere to put it. He folded it into his jacket pocket and the jacket smelled like citrus for the rest of the day.

The conference room was on the third floor. Glass walls, which Doug was beginning to think was a condition in Los Angeles — an allergy to opacity, a commitment to making everything visible, including himself, which was the part that tightened his grip on the tangerine peel in his pocket. There were succulents on the windowsill. The table was long and pale and had the grain of real wood, which Doug noticed because the table in the Mutual Fidelity break room was laminate with a wood-grain print, and the difference was the difference between a claim with documentation and a claim without.

Charlie Falk came in like weather. He was mid-forties but moved like he hadn't been informed. White sneakers that were geometric and angular and looked like they'd been designed by someone who also designed buildings. A gray T-shirt that fit in the way of T-shirts that cost more than Doug's dress shirts. He had a notebook — not a phone, an actual spiral-bound notebook, covered in handwriting — and he was flipping through it as he walked, talking before he sat down, which gave Doug the impression that the conversation had started in another room and Charlie had carried it in here like a tray he hadn't put down.

"Doug. Doug, Doug, Doug." He said the name like he was testing the weight of it. He sat down across from Doug and leaned forward on his elbows. "Okay. I'm just going to say it. I've watched the toast — Maya, how many times have I watched the toast?"

One of the two people who had followed Charlie into the room looked up from her laptop. She was younger than Kelsey, maybe twenty-five, and had her hair pulled back tight and a pen wedged behind her ear that she kept reaching up to touch, as if checking it was still there. She wore sneakers too, but hers looked like they'd actually been used for walking, and there was a thin leather cord around her wrist with a single bead on it that she turned between her fingers when she wasn't typing. "You said fourteen," she said.

"It's more than fourteen. It's probably — that doesn't matter. The number doesn't matter. What matters is, every time I watch it, I see something new. And that's the mark of — that's, like — that's the thing."

The other person was a man in his early thirties with a beard trimmed so precisely it looked like it had been done with a level and a tattoo on his forearm of what appeared to be a line graph, though of what data Doug could not determine. He'd been nodding since he entered the room. He had not yet stopped. He had a notebook too, but his was leather-bound and expensive-looking, and he wrote in it with a pen that had a cap, which he removed and replaced between notes with a soft clicking sound.

"So." Charlie put both hands flat on the table. "Here's what I'm seeing. And tell me if I'm off base, because I want this to be a conversation, not a pitch. I want this to be us, in a room, just — figuring out what this thing is."

He paused. Doug waited. Charlie appeared to be waiting too, as if "tell me if I'm off base" had been a genuine request for permission to continue.

"You can go ahead," Doug said.

"Right. So what I love about the toast — and I know what I'm about to say is, like, a word that gets thrown around in this building, I know that. But I mean it specifically. What you did in that toast was — you were *authentic*. You were *vulnerable*. And the comedy wasn't — it wasn't material. It wasn't jokes. It was a man standing in, like, total emotional — it was — okay." He restarted. "You were in this space of honesty, and the comedy was a byproduct. Not the point. The byproduct. Does that make sense?"

Doug considered this. He'd been standing at a microphone. He'd been drunk. "I think so," he said, which was not true.

"And what excites me," Charlie said, building on his own sentence before it had finished settling, "is what that means for the special. Because we're not looking for a comedy special. We're looking for a Doug Bridge special. We're looking for — the masculinity conversation. That's the frame."

Maya looked up from her laptop. "We had two masculinity-conversation projects in development last year. Neither one got past testing."

"Those were constructed. This is — we're not constructing anything here." Charlie waved it off and turned back to Doug. "You are, whether you know it or not, at the center of the masculinity conversation right now. The internet didn't just make you viral, Doug. The internet looked at you and said, here's a different way to be a man. Not the polished guy, not the — no offense to, what's his name, your daughter's —"

"Brendan," Doug said.

"Brendan. Not the Brendan model. Not the guy who has the right thing to say at the right time. You're the guy who says the wrong thing at the wrong time and *means it*. That's where the charge is. That's what everybody's responding to."

Charlie sat back and held Doug's eyes, waiting the way gamblers waited once the ball dropped out of view. The nodding man — his name, Doug would learn later, was also Brendan, which was a coincidence that Doug did not have time to process — nodded harder. Maya touched her pen.

Layla, who had been on her phone in a chair against the wall since they'd sat down, looked up. "And the energy around this is insane, babe. Just — Charlie, tell him about the numbers."

"The numbers." Charlie waved this away with his hand. "Eighty million views, that's not a video, that's a cultural — but I don't even want to talk about numbers. I want to talk about Doug. I want to talk about your life."

"Okay," Doug said.

"So walk me through it. A day. What does your day look like?"

Doug thought about his actual days. He woke up at 6:40. He showered. He ate toast or a banana. He drove to Mutual Fidelity. He processed claims. He ate lunch with Roy at the sandwich place they'd been going to for nine years. He processed more claims. He drove home. He ate dinner. He watched something on television or he didn't. He went to bed.

"It's pretty normal," he said.

"What's normal? Give me specifics."

"I go to work. I process insurance claims. Residential, mostly. Water damage. I eat lunch. I come home."

"And dinner? What's dinner?"

"Cereal, most nights."

Charlie went still. Both hands stayed flat on the table. He looked at Doug as if the word had changed the room's dimensions. The nodding man stopped nodding. Maya's hand froze on the way to her pen.

"Cereal," Charlie said.

"Yeah."

"For dinner."

"It's faster than cooking. And I'm the only one eating, so it doesn't —" Doug didn't finish the sentence because he wasn't sure where it was going and because the sentence, wherever it was going, was going somewhere he didn't want to be in this conference room.

"What kind of cereal?"

"It depends. Raisin Bran, usually. Sometimes Cheerios if they're on sale."

"Raisin Bran." Charlie said the words the way a jeweler might say the name of a stone. "And you eat this — where? At a table? On the couch?"

"Standing at the kitchen counter."

Charlie put both hands on top of his head, elbows wide, like the ball had already cleared the fence. "Standing at the counter," he repeated, mostly to himself. He looked at Maya. She was already typing.

"Okay, so — here's where I'm going with this." Charlie leaned forward again. "And this is just an idea, I'm just riffing, but — what if part of the special is your actual life? Not recreated, not dramatized. Your *actual* apartment, your *actual* routine. We put a camera in your kitchen and we film you eating Raisin Bran standing at the counter. We film you at your desk at — where do you work?"

"Mutual Fidelity."

"At Mutual Fidelity, processing claims. We film you driving to work. We film the *real stuff*. And then the live portion, the performance, that's you being — you. Talking about being you. The way you did at the toast."

Doug thought about his apartment. He thought about the kitchen counter with its stain near the sink from a coffee mug he'd left too long, which he cleaned every Sunday with a sponge he replaced monthly. The Ikea bookshelf in the living room with the books Roy had lent him over the years, none returned, arranged not by author or title but by the order he'd received them, which was a filing system only he understood. The one art print on the wall above the couch — a lithograph from a gallery on High Street that Megan had bought during one of the phases of their marriage when she was trying to make the apartment feel like somewhere you'd choose to live, and which she'd left behind when she moved out, either because she didn't want it or because she'd forgotten it, and Doug had never asked which because both answers would have been bad in different ways. The second bedroom, the one he called the office even though there was no computer in it, just boxes from the move twelve years ago, still packed, stacked against the wall in a configuration that had settled into permanence, sealed with packing tape that had yellowed but not split.

"It's not very interesting," Doug said.

"See, okay, this is why I'm excited." Charlie said this with both hands open, palms up, as if receiving something. "The boring part isn't the obstacle. It's the entry point. It's like — it's the part people lean toward. You're not doing that version of interesting where everything's optimized and lit and branded. It's an actual apartment, an actual job, cereal because you're hungry and that's dinner. We don't come in and improve that. We just go there."

"You want to show my apartment on Netflix."

"I want to show your LIFE on Netflix."

"My life is pretty boring."

"Doug." Charlie put his hand on his chest. "This isn't taste. It's production. The second people feel that normal life has been cleaned up for them, they're out."

Maya had a small scar on her chin, barely visible. "If we're shooting in Columbus, that budget's coming out of development. Different approval chain."

"We'll figure that out."

"Just — I need to know before Thursday if we're —"

"Thursday is Thursday." Charlie turned back to Doug. "If we do Columbus, don't touch anything on our behalf. If the counter has a stain, it keeps the stain. If the Civic's dirty, good. If there's a room in the apartment you mostly keep shut, I need to know that now."

Doug looked at him. "There's a second bedroom. It's just boxes."

"Great," Charlie said. "Leave it exactly like that."

"It's my apartment."

"Of course it is. I'm saying we don't improve it for us."

Doug looked at the succulents on the windowsill. One of them had a brown spot on one leaf, and he had the urge to tell someone.

"I could show you the apartment," he said. "If you want to see it. I've got pictures on my phone from when I filed my own renter's claim a couple of years ago. Pipe burst in the unit above me. I documented everything — the water damage, the affected square footage, the baseboards. I've got before and afters."

Charlie looked at Layla. Layla looked at Charlie. The other Brendan — not that Brendan, a different Brendan, one who worked in content development and had a line graph tattooed on his arm — said, "Is the building union?" to no one in particular, and wrote something in his leather notebook without waiting for an answer.

"He's incredible," Charlie said to Layla.

"I *told* you," Layla said, putting her phone down for the first time since the meeting started.

"No, that's it." Charlie shook his head. "I don't want the version you've already documented. I want the place before you start explaining it. The sink stain. The cereal box. The boxes in the second bedroom. We're going to come to your apartment. We're going to bring a small crew to Columbus, Ohio, and we're going to film you living your actual life. That's the project."

"What would I do?"

"What you always do."

"I process claims."

"Yeah."

"You want to film me processing claims."

"I want to film you *being you*, and if being you means processing insurance claims, then yeah. We film you processing claims."

Doug thought about what processing claims actually looked like — him sitting at his desk reading documentation, making phone calls to policyholders, typing notes into the system, occasionally driving to a property to take photographs of water stains and mold patterns and warped flooring. He thought about explaining to a homeowner that their twenty-year-old carpeting had a pre-loss actual cash value of approximately $1.40 per square foot, and the specific expression people made when they heard this number, which was the expression of someone learning that the thing they'd walked on every day was worth almost nothing.

Maya stopped typing. "We'd need releases. Employer if we're inside the office. Building release for the apartment. Maybe neighbor holds if sound bleeds into the hall. Nothing unusual."

"The claims are confidential," he said.

"Sure, yeah." Charlie nodded. "Then we don't shoot screens. We don't shoot names. If Mutual Fidelity won't let us on the claims floor, fine, we shoot the parking lot, the commute, you coming out the front door at five. A no is still useful. Then we know what we can and can't touch."

"The policies are proprietary. Mutual Fidelity probably wouldn't —"

"Doug." Charlie held up one hand. "We'll ask for what we can ask for. That's my side. Your side is being Doug."

"I'm always Doug."

"I know," Charlie said, and for a second his voice dropped out of the pitch. He looked down at his notebook, thumb holding a page open, then sat forward again. "Okay, so — content test. Immediate next step. Two days in Columbus, two weeks from now. Three people. Minimal footprint. Day one, apartment and dinner. Day two, commute, work, lunch, whatever part of the job legal clears. If legal clears less than we want, we pivot outside and around it. We cannot sit on this."

"Cereal."

"Cereal, yes, but routine." Charlie tapped the notebook with his pen. "The cereal only works if we get the day around it. Maya's sending a list tonight. Landlord contact, employer contact, your normal schedule, where you park. And don't buy anything for the apartment because a camera's coming."

Charlie had already swung back into motion. The pen tapping the notebook. Maya typing. Layla smiling with her phone facedown in her lap. He meant it. That was the part Doug couldn't get his head around. Mockery Doug could have understood. Mockery was the entertainment industry's version of depreciation — a reasonable adjustment for wear and use. This was different. Charlie heard dirty car, second bedroom, employer who might say no and treated them as assets. Doug did not know how to assess worth without a depreciation schedule.

The meeting went on for another thirty-five minutes. Maya asked for his landlord's name, whether the building had quiet hours, whether the apartment numbers were visible from the parking lot, whether Mutual Fidelity had a legal department, whether he ever did site visits alone. Doug answered all of it. He knew the quiet hours because they were in the lease. He knew the upstairs tenant's last name because of the pipe burst. The questions made his apartment and his job sound less like a life than like locations that could be lost if someone filled out the wrong form. The other Brendan asked once whether Doug's building had an elevator — it didn't — and made a note of it. Otherwise he said "love that" after nearly everything Charlie said, which was a phrase Doug had never heard used as a standalone response to a declarative sentence. He counted. Brendan said it nine times. He said "totally" eleven.

When the meeting ended, Layla and Charlie stood in the hallway talking while Doug waited near the elevator holding his visitor badge and looking at a framed poster of a show he'd never heard of. He could hear fragments — "momentum," "window," "the authentic thing is so big right now," "we've gotta move before the cycle turns" — and the language was the same as in the meeting but faster, stripped of the pauses and the looking-at-Doug moments. In the room Charlie had kept stopping after certain phrases, watching Doug the way people watched a homeowner after giving them bad numbers. Out here he just kept talking. Doug bent the laminated corner of the badge back and forth until the elevator opened.

---

The flight home left at 7:15 p.m. Doug was at the gate by 5:30 because he didn't know what else to do with the afternoon and because being early for a flight was the kind of behavior that made sense to him in a day where very little else had. Layla had offered to "grab a late lunch, or — oh, there's this incredible thing at LACMA, have you ever been?" and Doug had said no and then said he was fine and taken the black car back to the hotel and packed his bag and checked out, and Marin-or-Maren had said "Safe travels, love" and he'd left the matte black key card on the counter and walked out into the afternoon light that was still, even now, too sharp, too precise, too much like light that had been designed rather than had just happened.

He'd left *The Surrender Experiment* on the nightstand. He'd read two more pages while packing. The author had given up all personal preferences and let life guide his decisions, and this had apparently led him to build a successful software company, which seemed to Doug like a result that required at least some personal preferences about software, but he was not in a position to evaluate another man's surrender.

LAX was LAX. He ate a turkey sandwich at a place near the gate that charged $16 for turkey and Swiss on sourdough, which was roughly twice what the sandwich from his and Roy's place cost, and the sandwich from his and Roy's place came with a pickle. He sat at the gate and watched planes taxi on the tarmac and kept making the same list: apartment, cereal, Civic, desk at Mutual Fidelity, counter stain, second bedroom. The list arranged itself in his head the way contents did after a loss, each item ordinary and suddenly requiring description.

He didn't know what to do with this. He bought a water and a pack of peanut M&M's from a kiosk where nothing cost less than $4.50, and he boarded the plane, and this time he had a window seat because Layla had rebooked his return flight, and he pressed his forehead against the cold plastic and watched Los Angeles shrink below him — the grid of streets, the low buildings, the freeways like rivers running cars instead of water — and then the mountains, which he hadn't known were there, which had been behind the city the whole time and which he had never once seen from the ground.

The plane crossed the desert. Brown all the way out to the horizon. Then something that was probably Nevada or Utah, a different brown — drier, stripped down, scrub laid out in small stubborn dots. Then green came back in patches. Fields. Rectangles. Agriculture visible even from thirty-seven thousand feet. The patchwork tightened into the Midwest. The Midwest was flat. Doug had never had to think about flatness before. It had been the baseline, not a feature. From somewhere else, though, he could see it. The land stayed level. The roads made sense. Nothing pushed up for attention.

Columbus appeared below him as the plane descended. He could see the grid of it, the low sprawl, the highways he drove every day reduced to gray lines on a surface of green and brown. The Scioto River was a dark thread. The neighborhoods were indistinguishable from this height — Clintonville, where he lived, and Worthington, where the diner was, and the Short North and German Village and Bexley and all the small named places that made up the geography of his daily life, all of them flattened into the same even surface, none reaching very high, none insisting on anything.

From the window, it felt right. He couldn't have said exactly why. The city had enough room for what it was and didn't need more. Manageable was the word that came to him. Chosen came a second later, and he did not dismiss it.

He'd gone to Ohio State because it was affordable and close to Dayton. Mutual Fidelity had hired him. The apartment in Clintonville had been available, and $1,150 a month for two bedrooms was reasonable. From the window, the streets and roofs held those decisions in place. They did not look temporary.

The plane landed. The wheels hit the runway and the cabin lurched and the passengers clapped, which Doug had never understood — you didn't clap for your bus driver, you didn't clap when you pulled into a parking space — but tonight he let the clapping finish before he stood, looking out at the low airport lights beyond the wing.

He got his bag from the overhead bin. He walked through the jet bridge and through the terminal, which was smaller than LAX in a way that felt proportional rather than insufficient. He walked to the parking garage where his Civic had been sitting for two days, and the car was gray and streaked with the salt residue of a Columbus March, and it started on the first try the way it always did, and the heater came on, and the seat was cold and then it wasn't.

He drove home on 670 and then north on High Street and then into Clintonville, and the trees were still bare, the way they'd been when he left, because two days was not enough for spring to arrive in Ohio. The streetlights made the familiar shapes of his neighborhood — the houses, the parked cars, the sidewalks he walked sometimes at night when he couldn't sleep. He pulled into his spot behind the building and turned off the engine and sat in the dark for a moment with his hands still on the wheel.

His apartment was exactly as he'd left it. The Ikea bookshelf. The art print above the couch. The kitchen counter with the stain by the sink. The second bedroom, door closed. He didn't open it. The boxes were in there, arranged in their permanent configuration, same as always, same as twelve years.

He opened the cabinet above the stove and took out the Raisin Bran. He poured a bowl. He stood at the counter and ate it, and the cereal tasted the same as it always tasted, which was fine, which was what cereal tasted like. Halfway through the bowl he found himself looking around the kitchen the way Charlie had looked at him in the conference room. Bowl. Spoon. Counter. Stain by the sink. Clock reading 11:47 p.m. Eastern. Beyond the doorway, the Ikea bookshelf and the art print. An apartment in Clintonville with its doors where they belonged.

He rinsed the bowl and set it in the drying rack, which held one bowl, one spoon, one coffee mug from this morning. He went to bed. The shower door in his bathroom was glass, clouded white at the bottom where the hard water dried, set in an aluminum frame that rattled when he touched it. He slid it shut even though he wasn't showering, just because it was there and it did what it was supposed to do.


# Chapter 7

At 9:59 Tuesday morning, the buzzer rang. When Doug opened the door at 10:01, Simone Barr was standing in the hallway with a laptop bag over one shoulder and a paper cup of coffee that wasn't from anywhere he recognized. The fact that she'd arrived when they'd agreed already distinguished her from every person Doug had dealt with since the video. Layla was always twenty minutes late or forty minutes early. Charlie Falk had called twice at 6 a.m. Pacific, which was 9 a.m. in Columbus and therefore, in his mind, reasonable.

She was shorter than he'd expected, though he didn't know what he'd expected. He'd Googled her the night before and found a headshot on a management site — at least five years old, with different hair — and a few credits listed on IMDb without photos. Three late-night shows. He didn't know which ones. He didn't watch late-night.

"Doug," she said. Not a question.

"Yeah. Come in."

She stepped inside and surveyed the apartment with the same attention Doug gave a water-damaged house before opening the claim file: measuring, not judging, though the measuring carried its own judgment. She didn't comment on the Ikea bookshelf or the art print Megan had left behind or the cereal bowl he'd forgotten to wash that morning. She set her bag on the kitchen table, sat down, pulled the lid off her coffee, and blew on it once.

"You want some coffee?" Doug asked. "I've got a —"

"I'm good." She took a sip. Her nails were short and uneven, trimmed without a mirror, and her left thumb had a callus along the side from years of pressing a pen too hard. No rings. Her hair was pulled up with a clip that was handling maybe sixty percent of its job. She wore a gray sweater with a small hole near the cuff of the left sleeve that she hadn't fixed or hidden, just kept wearing, past the point of seeing the hole at all. Doug noticed it.

He sat across from her. The kitchen table was small enough that their knees nearly touched, and he moved his chair back an inch. Simone didn't register this.

"So," Doug said.

"So." She opened her bag and took out not a laptop but a yellow legal pad, the kind you could buy at any office supply store for three dollars. She also took out a pen — black, nothing special — and placed them on the table beside the coffee. She didn't write anything. She looked at Doug.

"How does this work?" he asked.

"We talk. I listen. Sometimes I write things down."

"What things?"

"Things that work."

"How will you know?"

"I'll know."

Doug didn't know what to do with his hands. He put them on the table, then in his lap, then back on the table. The refrigerator motor kicked on behind him, louder than it needed to be.
