Outside, a car passed with a bad muffler — the scrape of metal on asphalt — and he thought, automatically, that if the muffler detached and struck another vehicle the liability would fall to the city for road maintenance, not the driver, unless the driver had been previously cited for the exhaust condition. This was not a useful thought. He had it anyway.

"Be funny," Simone said.

Doug waited for her to smile. She didn't.

"Funny," he said.

"Yeah."

He thought about it. He was aware of himself thinking about it, which made it worse — the same problem as trying to fall asleep by concentrating on falling asleep. He thought about jokes he knew. He didn't know many. Roy had told him one last month about a horse walking into a bar, and Doug couldn't remember the punchline, only that it involved the horse ordering a drink Doug couldn't remember and the bartender either commenting or not commenting on the horse being a horse. He couldn't remember which version was the joke.

The silence went on.

Simone didn't fill it. She sat with her coffee and her legal pad and her pen and she waited. She had the patience of someone who had watched a lot of people sit in exactly this chair and produce exactly this silence. The quiet passed through uncomfortable into something worse — a zone where the silence itself had become the subject, where every second that passed without Doug saying something funny was another second of evidence that he could not say something funny, evidence that was accumulating in the record like unanswered interrogatories.

He looked at the kitchen window. The glass needed cleaning. There was a smudge at eye level that had been there since he'd leaned his forehead against it during a phone call with Layla three weeks ago, and he kept meaning to wipe it and kept not wiping it, and by now the smudge had become a feature of the window rather than a failure of maintenance.

He cleared his throat.

"I don't really — I don't have anything."

"I didn't ask you to have anything. I said be funny."

"That's the same thing."

"It's not."

Doug looked at the blank legal pad. The blankness felt personal, like a claim form with no entries — not absence of proof, but proof of absence.

"Okay," Simone said. She shifted in the chair. "Tell me about your day yesterday."

"My day."

"Yeah. What happened."

"I went to work."

"So tell me about work."

Doug had been telling people about work for twenty years and nobody had seemed interested, including, on many days, Doug himself. But Simone was sitting there with her pen and her pad and the clip in her hair slowly losing its grip, and she was waiting, and he didn't have anything else to offer.

"There's a claim I've been working on," he said. "Homeowner in Westerville. He had an above-ground pool in his backyard. Intex, twenty-four feet, the ones with the metal frame. They retail around seven hundred dollars. He'd had it three summers."

Simone didn't write anything. She held her coffee.

"The wall failed. Middle of the night, nobody outside. September, so the pool was still full — about fifteen thousand gallons. All of it went sideways into the neighbor's yard."

"Okay."

"The neighbor had roses. Hybrid teas. Seventeen plants. She'd been growing them for eleven years. She had receipts for the original plants, the fertilizer, the trellises, the pruning equipment, the soil amendments, the pH testing kits. She had photographs from every June going back to 2013. She had a log of every bloom cycle."

Doug paused. He'd said "log" and the word had a weight he didn't expect, sitting in his kitchen with a woman he'd known for twelve minutes. Everything he was describing — the documentation, the receipts, the careful recording of something that mattered to the person who kept the records — sounded like something else when he heard it out loud. He moved on.

"Total documented loss, forty-two hundred dollars. That's the plants, the infrastructure, and the lost growing season. She filed a claim."

"On whose policy?"

Doug sat back an inch. Nobody asked that. People always asked what happened to the roses.

"That's exactly the problem," he said. "She filed on her own homeowner's policy. But her policy covers weather events, fire, vandalism, accidental damage from covered perils. A neighbor's pool wall failing isn't weather. It's not vandalism. It's a third-party structural failure from a product with a known defect in the frame connectors."

"So she's not covered."

"Not under her own policy. The pool owner — his liability coverage has a sublimit on property damage that caps at two thousand, and even that's arguable because the policy language says 'sudden and accidental discharge.' You could make a case that a wall failing from a known manufacturing defect isn't accidental, it's negligence. And once you're in negligence territory, his insurer might deny the whole thing."

Simone's coffee had stopped moving. She was watching him with an expression he couldn't read — not boredom, not the polite attention people gave when Doug talked about insurance at parties, which was part of why he'd stopped going to parties.

"So you've got a woman with forty-two hundred dollars in dead roses and no coverage path. His insurance might deny, hers definitely denies, the manufacturer has a class action pending but it won't resolve for three years, and this woman is standing in her yard looking at seventeen dead hybrid teas and she calls me — she calls me personally, because I handled her kitchen fire claim in 2019 and she trusts me — and she says, 'Doug, someone owes me for my roses.'"

He stopped. He didn't know why he'd told this story. It wasn't a bit. It wasn't material. It was the kind of problem that made his job interesting — a jurisdictional tangle most people found deadly boring and Doug found genuinely absorbing. All the pieces were there. None of them fit the same picture.

Simone laughed.

Not a big laugh. It started as a breath through her nose and then her mouth opened slightly and her shoulders moved and there was a sound that was, unmistakably, a person being amused.

Doug stared at her.

"Why are you laughing? It's a real claim. She lost eleven years of roses."

"I know."

"There's no coverage path. I spent three days on the phone with two adjusters and a claims attorney trying to find one. It's a genuine jurisdictional problem."

"I know it is."

"So I don't understand what's funny."

Simone was still almost smiling — not broadly, just the residual expression of someone who had found something she'd been looking for in a place she hadn't expected. She picked up her pen for the first time and wrote something on the legal pad. Doug couldn't read it upside down. He thought he saw the word "roses" but it might have been something else.

"What are you writing?"

"Notes."

"About what?"

"About you."

Doug sat with that. A pipe knocked once in the bathroom wall. The smudge on the window caught the thin spring light and held it.

"I still wasn't trying to be funny," he said.

"I know," Simone said. "That's the whole thing."

---

They met four times that first week. Tuesday through Friday, same table, same time. Simone brought her own coffee every morning — always a paper cup, always black, always from somewhere different, as if she were conducting a private survey of every coffee shop in Clintonville. Doug offered to make some on Wednesday and she said, "I don't trust anyone else's coffee," and Doug understood this immediately because he didn't trust other people's claims documentation either, not until he'd verified the source.

Wednesday, after she sat down, she said, "Do the pool one again."

Doug had spent part of Tuesday night replaying the conversation in bed. Something had worked. He'd made a person laugh. The mistake, he learned at 10:03 Wednesday morning, was thinking he'd understood why.

"Okay," he said. He cleared his throat. "So this guy in Westerville buys one of those above-ground pools, the kind that already looks half broken when you take it out of the box, and in the middle of the night the wall blows and wipes out the neighbor's roses. Seventeen of them. Which is too many roses, if we're being honest, and then she's got receipts for everything because apparently the rose people are —"

Simone lifted a hand.

Doug stopped. "What."

"You don't say 'the rose people.'"

"I just did."

"In life, you don't."

Doug felt the back of his neck go warm. A bus went by on High Street hard enough that the spoon in his cereal bowl from breakfast rattled in the sink.

"I was trying to make it sound more like a bit," he said.

"I know."

"That's the job, isn't it?"

"No." She pointed at him with the pen. "The sentence before that one worked."

"Which sentence."

"'Seventeen of them.' Then you started translating."

"Translating what."

"You're telling me what attitude to have about it instead of telling me what happened." She put the pen down on the table between them. "Do it again. Start with the size of the pool."

Doug started again.

"Twenty-four feet," he said. "Intex. Metal frame. Three summers old. The wall failed in the middle of the night. About fifteen thousand gallons went into the next yard."

"Keep going."

"The neighbor had hybrid teas. Seventeen plants. Eleven years. She had receipts for the original plants, the fertilizer, the trellises, the pruning equipment, the soil amendments, the pH testing kits. She had photographs from every June going back to 2013."

"Keep going."

"Total documented loss, forty-two hundred dollars. She filed on her own policy first, which was the problem, because there was no coverage path there. Her carrier said no. His carrier had a sublimit. The manufacturer had a class action. She kept saying somebody owed her for the roses."

Simone laughed again, shorter this time but actual.

Doug stopped talking. "Same place you laughed yesterday."

"Yeah."

"But not when I tried to help it."

She didn't answer that. She didn't pick up the pen either. It sat on the table between them like a tool neither of them was going to use.

"Tuesday I didn't know the story was funny," he said. "Today I knew. And I tried to do what I thought you wanted and it died."

"Yeah."

"That's going to be a problem."

"That's going to be the whole problem," Simone said.

They sat with it. The legal pad was still blank.

Thursday she didn't ask about insurance.

"Describe the apartment," she said.

"What about it."

"Start anywhere."

Doug looked past her at the living room. "Two bedrooms. Kitchen. Bathroom. Ikea bookshelf. One art print Megan left when she moved out."

"What's on the shelf."

"A book about the history of the insurance industry I got at a library sale for two dollars. A novel Roy lent me in July that I haven't started. The user manual for the dishwasher."

"Why is the dishwasher manual on the bookshelf."

"The drawer where I keep manuals is full."

"Show me."

Doug opened the drawer beside the stove. Inside were manila folders labeled KITCHEN, MEDIA, and SMALL APPLIANCES. The labels came from a label maker he'd bought at OfficeMax in 2014 and kept refilling ever since. Simone looked down. Doug felt suddenly indecent, like she'd found a room in him that wasn't for visitors. She slid out the blender manual. Black-and-Decker. Cover bent.

"You donated this."

"Yes."

"And kept this."

"It was filed."

Simone slid the manual back and closed the drawer.

"What's the print."

Doug looked into the living room. "Museum poster. Woman in blue dress. I don't remember the painter."

"Why's it still up."

"It would leave pale rectangles on the wall."

"Is that the only reason."

"It was also already hanging."

"What'd you have for dinner last night," she said.

He looked at her. "Why."

"Because I asked."

"Cereal."

"What kind."

"Raisin Bran."

"Big night."

He looked at the table. "It's fast."

"How often."

He did the math before answering because accuracy had been requested. "Four nights a week, probably. Sometimes five if work's bad."

"At the table."

"At the counter."

"Why."

Doug felt the question in his shoulders before he felt it anywhere else. "Because if you sit down with cereal at the table it starts being dinner."

Simone held the pen but didn't write. She was quiet long enough that Doug could hear the water heater cycling in the closet behind the bathroom wall.

She glanced toward the hall. "What's in the second bedroom."

"Boxes."

"From when."

"The move. Twelve years ago."

"You haven't opened them."

"I know what I use."

"Do you."

He looked at the hallway. "Mostly."

Simone didn't say anything. The silence sat there between them until Doug stood up, which he had not planned to do, and walked down the hall with her following two steps behind. The carpet there had flattened into a darker strip from years of the same path to the bathroom and back. He stopped at the second bedroom door. His hand went to the knob. He did not turn it.

"What's in the boxes," Simone asked.

Doug kept his hand where it was. "Tax files. Lamps. A set of plates we got as a wedding gift that Megan said were too formal to use every day. A space heater that smells like dust for the first twenty minutes. Cords for electronics that no longer exist. Framed pictures I took off the wall and never put back up anywhere else."

"Can I see it."

"No."

She stood behind him. The hallway was narrow enough that he could feel her there without turning around.

"Okay," she said.

He let go of the knob. Something cold had collected in his palm from the metal. They walked back to the kitchen without speaking. Simone sat down, opened her pad, and wrote for a long time — not notes from a conversation but something else, something that took up most of the page. Doug sat across from her and watched the pen and didn't ask.

Friday he tried to get ahead of her.

He bought a yellow legal pad on his lunch break because it seemed possible that the pad itself was part of the method, the same way a clipboard made a stranger look official in a parking lot. He sat at the kitchen table for forty minutes Thursday night and wrote twelve things he thought might count as material. The first four were about insurance. Two were about airport bathrooms from the flight back from Los Angeles. One was about how nobody ever knew what "full coverage" meant. One was just the words ROY'S SANDWICH underlined twice.

When Simone arrived Friday, he slid the pad across the table before she'd even taken her coat off.

She looked at it. "What's this."

"Things I wrote down."

She sat. "Read one."

Doug flipped to the first page. His handwriting on paper always looked more officious than he'd intended, like he was documenting a witness statement.

"Okay. So an umbrella policy, despite the name, has nothing to do with weather. Which I think is confusing. For people."

Simone waited.

Doug looked up. "That's the first one."

"I know."

"It sounded better last night."

She tapped the pad. "Read another."

"When people say they have full coverage, what they mean is they have not read their policy."

Nothing.

Doug turned the page. "There's one about airport soap dispensers."

"No."

She pushed the pad back toward him. She still had not written anything on her own.

Doug turned it facedown.

"Can't you just write me ten minutes that work," he said. "Actual jokes. Something I can rehearse."

"I could write you ten minutes," Simone said. "They wouldn't sound like you."

"Maybe that's fine."

"It isn't."

"You don't know that."

She leaned back. "Tuesday you told me about roses and you didn't know you were being funny. Wednesday you tried to help it and it died. Today you wrote twelve things and they sound like a claims brochure."

"The pattern is that I can't do this on purpose."

"The pattern is you can't do it as somebody else."

Doug looked at his yellow pad lying facedown on the table. He could feel the twelve lines underneath it like embarrassing medical results in a sealed envelope.

"The special is supposed to be an hour," he said.

"I know."

"And people are buying tickets because of the toast."

"I know."

"The toast had actual stakes, though. My daughter was getting married. I was drunk. I said things I wasn't supposed to say in front of people I had to keep seeing. That's different from a blender manual."

"Yeah, it is." She sat forward. "That's the part you keep skipping."

"I'm not skipping anything. I'm saying you can't build sixty minutes out of cereal and dead roses."

"Then don't," Simone said.

He stared at her. "That's the opposite of a plan."

"You want a plan because a plan is something you can make by yourself."

"The problem is people bought tickets."

"Yeah."

"So what am I supposed to do."

Simone looked at him for a long time. The pen was on the table between them and neither of them touched it.

"I don't know yet," she said.

Doug hadn't expected that. He'd expected a method, a framework — the professional version of what he did when he built a claim file from insufficient documentation. Not knowing was supposed to be his problem. It was strange to hear it from the other side of the table.

"You don't know," he said.

"Not yet." She stood and pulled her bag over her shoulder. "But I know your twelve things aren't it."

When she was at the door he said, "Wait."

She turned.

"The woman with the roses. Did she ever get her money?"

"Did she?"

"Sixteen hundred. I found an umbrella policy on the pool owner that everybody'd missed. Not full recovery, but it was something."

"How'd she take it."

"She said I was the only person who took it seriously."

"The claim."

Doug put his hand on the doorknob. "The roses." He was quiet for a second. "She told me there was a difference. Between taking the claim seriously and taking the roses seriously. I didn't understand the distinction at the time."

Simone shifted her bag higher on her shoulder. "And now?"

"I still don't," he said. "But I think about it."

"Same time Monday?"

"Monday."

After she left, Doug went back to the kitchen and washed the cereal bowl he'd left in the sink. The faucet clicked and dripped against the basin. He looked out at the smudged window and pictured the June photographs from the claim file, one for each year, the same patch of yard getting fuller.

He dried his hands and went down the hall to the second bedroom. He stood with his hand on the knob for a moment, not opening it, just standing there with the boxes on the other side and the apartment quiet around him.


# Chapter 8

At 6:52 p.m. on a Tuesday in early April, Doug sat in the Civic in Brendan and Megan's driveway, eight minutes before he was expected, and watched light move behind the kitchen windows. He had been inside their house exactly four times in twelve years. He knew this the way he knew the deductible on a standard HO-3 homeowner's policy -- it was a number that had settled into his memory through repetition and mild dread. The first time was Kelsey's high school graduation party, where he'd stood in the backyard holding a paper plate of potato salad and talking to the father of one of Kelsey's friends about the traffic pattern on I-270, which had been a real conversation for approximately seven minutes before the man went to get another beer and didn't come back. The second was a logistics meeting about the wedding seating chart, during which he sat at the kitchen island and Megan produced a laminated diagram and they agreed on everything in twenty minutes because there was nothing left between them that was worth arguing about. The third was delivering a tax form Megan needed urgently, which required him to step inside for ninety seconds while she found an envelope, and during those ninety seconds he had noticed that the kitchen faucet was new. The fourth, three years ago, he'd waited in the living room for Kelsey to get ready for dinner and Brendan had given him a beer and asked about his work, and Doug had described the documentation process for residential water damage claims until Brendan's expression passed through interest, past politeness, and arrived at something closer to medical concern. This was the fifth.

The invitation had come from Megan by text, which was how they communicated -- texts about logistics, occasional emails about Kelsey, the rare phone call when tone of voice mattered more than efficiency. *Brendan and I would love to have you for dinner Tuesday. He thinks it would be good for all of us to sit down together. No agenda.* The phrase "no agenda" was the kind of thing Megan said when there was definitely an agenda, and the agenda was the reasonable management of a situation she had already assessed and organized into a plan that would be presented as spontaneous. Doug had typed *Tuesday is fine* and stared at the words for forty seconds before sending, making sure the message landed in the narrow band between agreeable and dead.

He could have said no. He had weighed the contingencies -- mapping the outcomes, estimating the exposure. Saying no would require explaining why. The honest explanation was that Brendan's house made Doug feel like a depreciated asset standing next to a full replacement. He couldn't say this to Megan, who would contextualize it into something manageable. He couldn't say it to Brendan, who would take it seriously and respond with such careful warmth that Doug would want to walk into the Olentangy River. So he was here, in clean khakis and a blue button-down he had ironed for the first time since November. The iron had spit rust-colored water on his first shirt, which meant switching to this one, which had a slightly too-tight collar he could feel against his throat every time he swallowed.

The evening air was cool. April in Columbus still had winter in it -- the trees on Brendan's street had that uncertain look, buds visible but not committed, as if they were waiting for a second opinion. In Upper Arlington, people had opinions about their landscaping and the money to act on them. The lawns were already green. Doug's building in Clintonville had a shared strip of grass that someone mowed when it became embarrassing.

At 6:58 he got out of the car. The driveway was concrete, recently sealed, that dark even sheen that meant someone had paid to maintain something that would have been fine without maintenance. Two cars: Megan's Subaru and Brendan's silver Lexus. Doug's Civic, parked behind the Lexus, had a coffee stain on the passenger seat and a Mutual Fidelity parking tag hanging from the rearview mirror. He left the tag. Removing it would have been a statement about being seen.

The front door opened before he reached it. Brendan must have seen the headlights. He stood in the doorway wearing a blue apron over a grey crewneck sweater, a wooden spoon in his left hand, and the expression of a man who was genuinely pleased to see someone he had every reason to pity. Brendan was fifty-four and looked like a person who had arrived at his own life without taking any wrong turns -- tall, graying at the temples in the way that men in movies were permitted to gray, his posture easy, his hands the steady hands of an orthopedic surgeon who spent his days putting broken things back together. Doug's hands typed descriptions of other people's damage.

"Doug. Hey. Come in, come in."

They shook hands. Brendan's grip was firm and unhurried and communicated nothing except competence, which was enough. Doug stepped inside and the house took him in with warmth and the smell of rosemary and something citrus -- lemon, maybe, and something else underneath that he couldn't name because Doug was not a man who identified herbs with confidence. He knew the smell of wet drywall after a pipe burst. He knew the chemical signature of smoke damage versus grease fire damage. Rosemary and lemon were outside his professional range.

The hallway was wide enough that two people could walk side by side without touching. Doug's hallway in Clintonville was not. There was a photograph on the wall he didn't remember from the last visit -- Megan and Brendan on a beach somewhere, both wearing sunglasses, Brendan's arm around her shoulder with the settled weight of a man who had been touching this woman for a decade and still had reasons. Doug looked at the floor. The hardwood was clean and uniform, no visible wear at the thresholds or turning points. In claims, you could date a floor by where it wore first -- the path between bedroom and kitchen, the spot by the front door where shoes came off, the traffic triangle from fridge to stove to sink. This floor gave nothing away. It was either new or someone had paid to make the age invisible.

"How was the drive?"

"Fine," Doug said. "No traffic." This was true but also the smallest possible answer to a question that deserved even less thought than he was giving it. The drive had been twelve minutes of negotiating with himself about whether to stop at the Shell station on Henderson for gum, as if gum would make this evening tolerable.

"Good, good." Brendan walked toward the kitchen and Doug followed because the alternative was standing in the hallway. "Can I get you something? I've got a red open -- a Cotes du Rhone, it's good -- or beer, sparkling water, whatever."

There it was. Doug had run this scenario during the drive until the thinking itself went dull. He would be offered alcohol. He would say yes or no. Saying no would be a statement -- about the toast, about the eight whiskeys at the wedding, about the video that eighty million people had watched him give because of what happened after those eight whiskeys. Saying no would put the evening on a footing where everyone was performing awareness of Doug's relationship with alcohol, which was not a relationship he wanted examined. Saying yes meant monitoring himself, counting sips, performing control.

"Wine sounds good," he said. "Just a glass."

Brendan poured without ceremony from a bottle already breathing on the counter. The glass was the oversized kind that wine people used because the shape was supposed to matter, and it was heavy in Doug's hand. He took a sip. It was good. This was the worst possible outcome, because bad wine would have been one small deficiency to file away, one tiny crack in Brendan's general competence. The wine was good and the glass was heavy and the kitchen was the kind of kitchen that appeared in the magazines his mother left on her coffee table at Maple Glen, prices circled in pen, her opinions written in the margins beside photographs of rooms she would never occupy.

The kitchen had a stone-topped island, a range with six burners -- Doug counted, because counting was what he did when he didn't know what else to do -- and copper pots hanging from a rack, arranged by size, their bottoms darkened from use. None of it was decorative. Brendan cooked in this kitchen, which was different from merely having an expensive kitchen, and the difference was the part that Doug found hardest to dismiss. A nice kitchen you didn't use was just money. A nice kitchen with wear patterns was a life being lived.

Four lamb chops sat on a wooden cutting board, thick-cut, resting. The juices had pooled along the grain of the wood, pinkish-brown. There was a small bowl of green sauce and a sheet pan of roasted potatoes, golden, their edges slightly charred. Doug calculated the cost reflexively, an adjuster's reflex -- the lamb alone was probably forty dollars, maybe forty-five depending on the butcher. His own dinner the previous night had been a bowl of cereal he'd eaten standing at the counter, approximate cost sixty-three cents including the milk.

"Smells good," he said, because it did and because he had been standing in another man's kitchen in silence long enough to need to say something.

"Thanks. New marinade -- harissa and lemon. Megan found the recipe." Brendan prodded one of the chops with a fork, then stopped himself and set the fork down. It was a small gesture of self-correction, and Doug found it unreasonably humanizing. Even Brendan had doubts about his lamb. This was new information. Doug didn't know where to file it. "You're not supposed to poke them," Brendan said, mostly to himself. "It lets the juices out. I always forget." He had a tea towel thrown over one shoulder, not draped but bunched, the way someone did it when they'd been cooking for an hour and had forgotten it was there. His left thumb had a small cut near the nail, recently healed, and his watch -- expensive, Doug could see that much -- had a scuff on the face that said it was worn for function, not display.

Megan came down the stairs. She was wearing a dark blouse and jeans, small gold earrings, her hair down. Doug noticed -- with the detached precision he wished were genuinely detached -- that she looked good. She looked like a person who had built enough order into her days that appearance was one more thing she handled without visible effort. She had a particular way of entering a room, unhurried, as though everything on the other side of the threshold already knew she was coming. She'd always had it. When they were married he'd found it attractive. Now it was just a fact about her, filed under general information, cross-referenced with nothing.

"Doug. Hi." She kissed his cheek, a contact so brief it could have been imagined. Her perfume was different from what she'd worn when they were married -- lighter, less sweet. Twelve years was enough time to change your perfume several times over. He wondered, with the part of his brain that catalogued things uselessly, whether the change had happened all at once or gradually, and whether Brendan had bought it or whether she'd chosen it herself, and then he stopped wondering because the answer was none of his business in a way that had been formalized by the Franklin County Domestic Relations Court.

"Thanks for coming," she said. "I know it's --" She let the sentence find its own ending, which was a Megan move, giving the other person room to fill the silence with whatever version was most comfortable.

Doug didn't fill it.

"Sure," he said. He held up his wine glass, a gesture that communicated nothing except that he had hands and they were occupied. "Brendan got me the wine."

"I see that." She smiled. It was not the smile he'd heard on the phone two weeks ago when he'd called to apologize, the one that was organized and fair and slightly instructional. This one was smaller. Tired at the corners, maybe. Or maybe he was reading things that weren't present because reading absent details was, professionally speaking, what he did.

She poured herself a glass from the same bottle. They stood around the island with the lamb between them, each of them taking a side of the stone. Nobody said anything for a second. Doug had the sense that the room had already decided where he belonged and was waiting for him to accept it.

They sat down in the dining room. Megan had set the table with cloth napkins and actual plates. The room had the deliberate feel of a real dinner, careful enough that even the extra chairs looked assigned. The table was dark wood, rectangular, and seated six. There were three of them. Doug sat at one end, Brendan at the other, Megan in the middle on one side, which put her equidistant from both men in a way that was probably how she always sat and felt architectural. The three empty chairs made the room larger than it needed to be.

Brendan served. He placed a lamb chop on each plate deliberately -- the chop angled, the potatoes arranged in a crescent, the green sauce drizzled rather than spooned. Doug watched this and thought about his own three paper plates, the ones he used when he was too tired to wash a real dish, which was most nights.

"This looks incredible," Megan said. She meant it, and it did.

Doug cut into the lamb. It was pink in the center, tender, the harissa giving it a slow heat that arrived after the first chew and stayed. He swallowed. It was the best thing he'd eaten in recent memory, and that made the room harder to sit in.

"How's it going?" Brendan asked. The question was broad enough to mean anything -- the job, the video, the fame, the specific experience of sitting in this house -- and Doug chose the narrowest reading available.

"Busy at work. We've had a run of burst pipe claims from the thaw."

"Right, spring." Brendan nodded. "We see the same thing -- slip-and-falls, this time of year. The ice-melt transition." He was trying to meet Doug on professional territory, which Doug recognized as generous and which made the income gap between an orthopedic surgeon's slip-and-fall cases and an insurance adjuster's burst pipes feel like a distance measured in different units entirely.

"Doug's been working with a comedy writer," Megan said. The topic shift hit like a draft from an opened window. He hadn't told Megan about Simone -- Layla must have, or Kelsey, or the internet. There had been a line in one of the smaller articles.

"Simone Barr," Megan added. "I looked her up. She's written for some pretty impressive shows."

"She's good," Doug said. "We're working on material."

"What's that like?" Brendan asked. He had his fork halfway to his mouth and paused there, genuinely considering. "Working with someone on material, what do you actually do?"

"We sit at my kitchen table. She asks me questions. I talk. Sometimes she writes things down."

"Huh." Brendan ate his bite. "Is it helping?"

"I don't know yet."

They ate. The sound of silverware on plates, of three people chewing. Megan's knife made a small scraping noise against the plate and she adjusted her grip, a reflex so automatic she probably didn't notice it. Doug noticed. He noticed the scrape and the adjustment and the fact that Megan still held her knife the European way, blade facing in, the way she had held it through eighteen years of their marriage and twelve years of not being married, and the fact that this had not changed was both meaningless and exactly the sort of detail his brain stored without permission.

"So," Brendan said, and the word carried the weight of someone about to bring up the subject everyone had spent forty minutes navigating around. "The video. I just want to -- can I say something about it?"

"Brendan," Megan said. It wasn't sharp. It had the quality of a line that had been discussed beforehand, a planned intervention that was supposed to sound spontaneous.

"No, I just --" He set his fork down. "I watched it again. Last week. And I know we've talked about this, and I know it's been discussed to death, but I want to say, to you directly, that I think it was raw. It was incredibly raw and real, and I think there's something in it that most people can't access."

Doug moved a potato from one side of his plate to the other.

"I don't mean that in a --" Brendan glanced at Megan, then back at Doug. "I'm not analyzing it. I'm just saying, there was a willingness in it to just... say things."

"I was drunk," Doug said.

"I know."

"Eight whiskeys."

"I know. But the whiskey didn't put those things there. It just --"

"Removed the filter," Doug said, because everyone said this, and saying it first was faster than hearing someone else arrive at it.

"Yeah."

Megan touched her left earring, rotating it between thumb and forefinger. Doug didn't know if this was a new habit or one she'd always had that he'd lost track of in twelve years of not sitting across from her at dinner.

"I think what Brendan's trying to say," Megan said, "is that we've both sat with it. What you said about the custody, about the marriage --" She turned her wine glass by the stem. "We both heard those parts."

"It was our daughter's wedding," Doug said.

"It was," Megan said. "And you were drunk at our daughter's wedding and you said things about the marriage in front of everyone. Brendan's mother asked me afterward if you were all right." She set her glass down. "But you said things I'd never heard you say sober. Not to me. Not in eighteen years." She delivered this in sequence, each clause arriving in order: organized, fair, structurally sound. It was the voice she used when she was being reasonable, which was different from being kind and different from being honest. Being reasonable was Megan's craft. She was excellent at it. Doug had lived with it for eighteen years and it still made him feel like a water stain someone was trying to manage.

"Can we talk about something else," Doug said. It was not a question.

"Of course," Megan said. "Absolutely."

They talked about something else. Brendan mentioned a medical conference he was attending in May, something about shoulder reconstruction techniques. Megan talked about a fundraising gala for the nonprofit where she was coordinating donor tables, seating logistics, a silent auction that required insurance, and Doug found himself briefly engaged by the insurance question before realizing she had not actually asked for his professional opinion and was describing the event socially, not seeking a coverage assessment. He listened to the details and found them mildly soothing. Other people's operational problems were soothing when they were bounded and specific and not yours.

Brendan asked about Doug's current caseload, and Doug, grateful for the redirect, described the burst pool from a few months back -- the above-ground wall that had split in February, the flood that destroyed the neighbor's seventeen hybrid tea roses, the question of whose policy covered what when the damage crossed a property line through third-party structural failure. He explained it with the thoroughness the subject deserved. The pool owner's liability didn't extend to the neighbor's landscaping under the standard policy. The neighbor's policy covered weather events but not structural failure from an adjacent property. Seventeen hybrid teas, four thousand two hundred dollars, documented with receipts and photographs.

"So nobody's covered," Brendan said.

"There's a gap. Neither policy was designed for this scenario."

"And the roses are just... gone."

"The roses are destroyed. They were hybrid teas. You can't just replant hybrid teas, you have to buy mature stock, and the soil's been contaminated by the pool chemicals, so there's a remediation question on top of the replacement question." Doug said this with the care the information warranted, and Brendan was listening with what appeared to be genuine interest, which was either a credit to Brendan's social skills or a sign that the dynamics of insurance coverage gaps were more compelling than Doug had been led to believe.

"That's kind of tragic," Brendan said.

"It's a claims issue," Doug said. "But yeah."

Megan stood and began clearing plates. She had a system -- her plate, then Brendan's, then reaching for Doug's with a questioning look. Doug had eaten everything. The lamb, the potatoes, the sauce, gone. His plate was clean the way hungry people's plates were clean, which was not the same as polite people's plates, and he realized with a small flush of something he couldn't name that he should have left a bite behind. Leaving food signaled ease, and ease was the one thing he had not managed to perform all evening.

His wine glass sat where he'd set it down during the main course, still more than half full, the wine at body temperature now. He had been holding it and setting it down and holding it again for almost an hour, and the heat from his hand had changed it into something warm and flat. He left it on the table.

Brendan made coffee with a machine Doug couldn't identify, some chrome thing with a lever and a pressurized element and a sound like a small, expensive argument with physics. The coffee came in small cups. It was strong and slightly bitter and Doug held his with both hands because the cup was warm and the living room, where they had moved, was cooler than the kitchen.

The living room connected to the dining room through an archway wide enough that the two rooms were almost one. The furniture was the kind that did not call attention to itself while costing more than Doug's rent -- a deep sofa in a dark fabric, two armchairs, a coffee table with a single book and a ceramic bowl that held nothing. Doug sat in one of the armchairs because the sofa invited proximity and the armchairs were their own territory. Brendan took the sofa. Megan sat at the other end of it, legs curled under her, holding her coffee cup with both hands and keeping her attention moving between the two men. The archway left the room too open for privacy, and the chairs kept it from feeling easy anyway.

There was a bookshelf against the far wall with actual books, arranged by some system that suggested they'd been read and returned to where they belonged. Two framed prints, warm-toned, abstract. The lamp beside the sofa gave a yellow light that made the room feel smaller and warmer than it was. Doug's apartment had one art print, the one Megan had left behind when she moved out. He had never asked whether she'd left it on purpose or simply hadn't wanted it. Asking would produce an answer, and the answer would tell him something about how thoroughly she'd thought about leaving, and he didn't need to know.

"I'm glad you came," Brendan said. He was sitting with one ankle crossed over the opposite knee, coffee balanced on the armrest with a steadiness that probably came from years of holding small instruments in high-pressure environments. The posture of a man who was home. "I know this isn't --" He paused. "I know the three of us in a room together isn't exactly natural."

"It's fine," Doug said.

"I just think with everything going on, the video and the attention, it seemed like the right time to just... sit down. Clear the air a little."

Doug held his coffee. The cup was small and warm and probably cost more than his lunch. He was careful with it.

"I appreciate that," he said. He did. Brendan was trying to be decent. Doug kept both hands on the cup until the heat went from pleasant to uncomfortable, because it gave him something else to pay attention to.

"Can I ask you something?" Brendan said.

Doug didn't say yes. He waited.

"What are you going to do with all of it? The fame, the Netflix thing, the whole... trajectory. What's the plan?"

"There's a special. They're taping it at the Lincoln Theatre. Simone and I are putting material together."

"Are you enjoying it?"

Nobody had asked Doug this. Layla asked if he was ready. Simone asked if he was working. Kelsey asked if he was okay. Not one of them had asked whether he was enjoying it, probably because the answer was so obviously tangled that the question itself seemed reckless.

"Parts of it," Doug said. "The working with Simone. She gets what's happening better than I do."

"And the performing part?"

"I haven't performed yet. Not really. There's a corporate event coming up. Indianapolis."

"Well." Brendan drank his coffee. Set the cup down on the armrest. Picked it up again. Doug had never seen Brendan fidget before, and the motion was small enough to have been nothing, but Doug's entire professional life was built on noticing small things that might have been nothing and establishing whether they were. "I've been thinking about what you did. At the wedding."

Doug's body prepared to hear, for the thousandth time, someone's interpretation of the toast. He had a rotation of responses organized by function: deflection (he was drunk), minimization (it wasn't planned), redirect (the lamb was excellent). He queued the deflection.

"And I don't mean the speech itself," Brendan said. He uncrossed his legs and leaned forward, elbows on knees, and the shift in posture changed the geometry of the room. He was closer now, and his voice was quieter. Doug had noticed this about Brendan before -- the man got quieter when something mattered more, which was the opposite of most people Doug knew, including himself. Brendan rubbed the back of his neck with one hand. "I gave my toast first. You remember that."

"I remember."

"I worked on that toast for two weeks. I showed it to Megan. I showed it to a friend of mine. I timed it. I planned where I'd pause, where I'd let myself get emotional." He stopped rubbing his neck and folded his hands together between his knees. "I cried at the reception. You saw that. Everybody saw that. And I'd also cried in rehearsal, at the exact same spot. Both times. The same line, the same pause, the same --" He made a small gesture with his hand. "Production."

Doug looked at the coffee table. He looked at the book lying face-down on it. He could see part of the cover, something abstract in blue and white. He couldn't read the title from this angle. This bothered him in a way that was completely out of proportion, a small unknowable thing in a room full of larger ones.

"After my toast," Brendan said, "you stood up. And I remember thinking, sitting back down, that you'd keep it short. Congratulate them. Raise your glass. Sit down. Because that's what I would've done. If I'd been in your place, with all of that in the room, I would've gone as small as I could."

The room was very quiet. Megan's hands tightened around her cup, a pressure Doug could see in the way her fingers shifted against the ceramic, though her face was unchanged.

"But you didn't," Brendan said. "You stood up with everybody looking at you, with me sitting right there, and you said it as it was. You left the whole room with nowhere comfortable to go. That's what I mean. The standing up. I don't think I'd have had that in me."

Doug stared at the book on the coffee table. The blue and white cover. The title he couldn't read. Behind him, from somewhere deeper in the house, he could hear the kitchen faucet dripping -- a slow, rhythmic sound, a loose washer or a worn valve seat, something a homeowner would notice and fix on a weekend, and Brendan probably already had a plan for it, and the plan would work, because Brendan's plans worked.

Doug waited for the correction, the softening clause, the part where Brendan turned this back into pity or management. It didn't come. Brendan wasn't excusing the toast or pretending it had been anything but a wreck. He was only saying that Doug had stood up and done the harder thing, and Doug had nowhere to put that.

"The lamb worked," Doug said.

Brendan looked at him. Megan looked at him. Doug looked at his empty coffee cup, which was small and warm and sat in his hands like something he had been given and hadn't earned.

"I mean it," he said. "The harissa. With the lemon. It worked."

Brendan held his gaze for a second, then nodded once.

"Thanks," Brendan said. He leaned back. "I'll send you the recipe."

"Okay."

Megan set her coffee down and stood. "Let me wrap up some leftovers for you," she said. The sentence was ordinary and also the most transparent rescue Doug had ever witnessed, and he was grateful for it with a gratitude he would never mention to anyone alive.

She went to the kitchen. Doug heard the refrigerator open, the tear of aluminum foil, the specific domestic sounds of someone packaging food in a kitchen they knew by heart, and these were sounds he had heard Megan make a thousand times during their marriage -- foil pulled from the roll, the drawer shut with a hip, the particular rhythm of wrapping something up -- and he was hearing them now from a room away, in another man's house, and the distance between the two versions of those sounds was twelve years and a court filing and the undeniable fact that the kitchen she moved through now was better than any kitchen they had shared.

Doug stood. Brendan stood. They were on opposite sides of the coffee table with its face-down book.

"Thanks for coming," Brendan said. "I mean it. I know this was..." He searched. "A lot."

"Thanks for doing it," Doug said, and he meant the dinner and the lamb and the wine and the thing Brendan had said and the not making any of it worse than it needed to be.

"Anytime," Brendan said, and he meant it, and Doug found even this generosity exhausting, because the man followed through on everything, including kindness.

Megan came back with a foil-wrapped package in a plastic bag. She handed it to Doug. The bag was warm. She walked him to the hallway, to the front door, to the threshold where his visit had started and now ended.

"Drive safe," she said. Her hand rested on the doorframe. The gold earring caught the hallway light. "And Doug?"

He turned.

"That thing you did. With the lamb." She adjusted the earring. "You remember when I told you about the separation? The first thing you said -- you asked who was going to claim Kelsey on the taxes that year."

Doug remembered. He'd needed to know.

"I'm not --" Her hand shifted on the doorframe. "That's not what tonight was about." She was quiet for a second. "Eat it tonight. It'll be worse tomorrow."

"Okay," he said.

He walked to the Civic. The driveway was dark, the sealed concrete smooth underfoot. He set the warm bag of leftovers on the passenger seat, on top of the coffee stain, and sat for a moment with his hands on the steering wheel. The house glowed behind him, its windows lit. Brendan and Megan were in there, probably clearing the coffee cups, speaking in the low practical voices of people who belonged to the same kitchen.

He drove through Upper Arlington, through its quiet streets with their maintained lawns and sealed driveways and houses where people ate lamb on a Tuesday. He turned onto the highway and then into Clintonville, where the houses were closer together and the porches had the particular cluttered look of people who used them. He parked in the lot behind his building, where the pavement was cracked and the lines were faded and the space was his because he always parked in the same one.

He carried the leftovers inside. He set them in the refrigerator, which contained a half-gallon of milk approaching its date, a block of cheddar with one corner going dark, and a jar of mustard with maybe one more serving before it was empty. The warm foil package sat on the middle shelf between the cheddar and the mustard, giving off a little heat in the cold air.

He stood in the kitchen. His kitchen. The light over the stove was on -- he left it burning at night because the apartment felt less empty with one light going, which was not something he had ever said to anyone or planned to. The kitchen was small. The counter was laminate, beige, a color that had been chosen by someone who didn't care what color the counter was and would never be asked. There was no island, no stone, no copper pots. There was a toaster with a broken lever that he had to hold down manually, and a coffee maker that took seven minutes for four cups, and a cereal bowl drying in the rack that was the same cereal bowl he used every night.

He set the foil package on the counter beside the cereal bowl and left it there for a minute. The heat from it spread into the laminate. Under the stove light the foil looked almost white.

He unwrapped the foil. The crackle of it filled the kitchen for a second. The lamb was still warm. He ate it standing at the counter, in his kitchen, under the stove light, with his hands.

It was still good.


# Chapter 9

Doug had practiced the set fourteen times. He knew because he'd kept a log — hash marks on the back of a Mutual Fidelity envelope that sat on his kitchen counter between the toaster and the jar of pens he never used. Fourteen trips between the bathroom and the kitchen, roughly eleven steps, about the amount of stage Simone said a small room would give him. The first five times he'd read from the pages she'd printed out and annotated in her small, exact handwriting — *pause here*, *this is where they laugh*, *don't rush the transition*. The next five from memory, checking the pages only twice. The last four he hadn't needed the pages at all. Twenty-five minutes of material, organized into three sections with callbacks and what Simone called a "button" at the end, a comedy term for a closing line that left the audience feeling finished.

By Thursday afternoon the foil package was gone from the counter, but the Mutual Fidelity envelope was still there.

Doug could deliver all twenty-five minutes to his refrigerator with confidence.

The refrigerator had never been to an afternoon session on pharmaceutical sales incentives. The refrigerator had never consumed four drinks at an open bar. The refrigerator had not come to the keynote expecting to see the guy from the wedding toast video, the one the internet had declared a comic genius, the one eighty million people had watched and decided was saying something important about fatherhood or marriage or American masculinity or whatever the latest thinkpiece had settled on. The refrigerator was a 2019 Kenmore with a dent on the lower left panel where Doug had hit it with a suitcase, and it had been an excellent audience.

Layla had called the night before. "Okay, so, quick rundown. It's Astelline Pharmaceuticals, they do like anti-inflammatories or something, I don't know, it doesn't matter. Regional sales conference, about four hundred people, they've been in sessions all day and they want to be entertained. You're the after-dinner keynote. They've seen the video, they love you, they're primed. Thirty minutes, in and out. Fifteen K. You're gonna be great."

"What if I'm not great," Doug had said.

"Then you're still getting paid fifteen thousand dollars, babe. Think about that."

Doug had thought about it. Fifteen thousand dollars for thirty minutes. He made sixty-one thousand four hundred dollars a year before taxes at Mutual Fidelity, which came to $29.52 an hour if you divided by the standard two-thousand-and-eighty-hour work year, which Doug had once done on a slow Tuesday. Fifteen thousand dollars for thirty minutes was five hundred dollars a minute. It was $8.33 per second. Every second he stood on that stage, whether he was funny or silent or dying in front of four hundred strangers, was worth what his turkey club at the sandwich place cost. This did not make him less nervous. It made him nervous and aware that his nervousness was expensive.

He drove to Indianapolis on a Thursday afternoon in late April, two and a half hours on I-70 through landscape that transitioned from Ohio's version of flat to Indiana's version of the same flat with different highway signs. He'd left at three, telling Janet he had a personal appointment. That was technically true by insurance standards: accurate statement, misleading picture. He listened to the radio for the first hour and turned it off because every song either had too much energy or reminded him of something and he couldn't afford either.

The Indianapolis Convention Center was a building that looked like it had been designed by a committee whose only instruction was "large." Doug parked in a garage that charged eighteen dollars. Not unreasonable for downtown parking. He noted it anyway because he noted the cost of things. It was how his mind organized the world. Some people noticed architecture or other human beings. Doug noticed that the parking garage charged by the half-hour for the first three hours and switched to a flat fee after. The flat fee was a worse deal if you stayed less than four and a half hours. Doug planned to.

Inside, the convention center smelled like carpet cleaner and the particular staleness of air that had been circulated through the same building for three days of corporate programming. Banners for Astelline Pharmaceuticals hung from the ceiling — a teal logo, a tagline he couldn't read from this distance, and a photo of a woman smiling while doing yoga, which he assumed had something to do with the anti-inflammatory product line. People in lanyards moved through the hallways with the purposeful drift of the professionally conferenced, carrying tote bags filled with branded items they would never use.

A woman met him at the backstage entrance. Her name was Denise. She was the events coordinator, and she had a clipboard, a headset looped around her neck, and a blister on her left heel that she was trying not to favor. Doug could tell because she shifted her weight to the right every few seconds and winced slightly when she forgot about it. She walked him down a corridor with exposed pipe runs along the ceiling and gray industrial carpet that was a different, worse gray than the carpet in the public areas.

"So we've got you on at eight," Denise said. "Dinner wraps around seven-thirty, then there's a little bit of remarks from our VP of sales — Brian, he'll be quick, he's been told to be quick — and then he introduces you. Sound good?"

"Sure," Doug said.

"The video screens will have your name up. You want any slides or anything?"

"No."

"Great. There's water on the podium and we've got a handheld mic or a lavalier, whatever you prefer."

"Handheld," Doug said, because Simone had told him to use a handheld. She'd said it gave you something to do with one of your hands.

"Perfect." Denise checked her clipboard, flipping to a page she'd already looked at. "Is there anything you need? Green room's got some snacks, water, sodas. I think there's a fruit plate that's been sitting there since this morning, so maybe skip the fruit plate."

"I'm fine," Doug said.

"Okay great. I'll come get you about five minutes before." She paused. Her expression shifted into something less professional and more person-shaped. "My daughter watches your video like once a week. She's fourteen. She thinks you're hilarious."

Doug didn't know what to say to this. "That's nice of her."

"She made her stepdad watch it and he didn't think it was that funny, which she took as a personal attack on her judgment." Denise gave him the tight smile people used when they were mentioning family without really getting into family. "Anyway. Five minutes before. I'll come grab you."

She took three steps, stopped, and looked back at her clipboard. "Actually, before I park you, Brian wants to say hi. Two minutes. He likes to meet the speaker."

"I am not speaking yet," Doug said.

"Tell him that," Denise said, and turned toward the ballroom.

Dinner was in Ballroom C, which meant a room big enough to be divided but not improved by the division. Black curtains cut off the far third of it. The useful section held round tables in white cloths, teal uplighting at the walls, and two projection screens with his name on them in a font that managed to look energetic and mandatory at the same time. There was a photo of him too, clearly pulled from the wedding video. He was holding a microphone, tie half loose, eyes bright with the false competence of bourbon. Someone had cropped the frame close enough that the drink in his other hand was gone. The drink had been part of the explanation.

Brian was standing near the stage before anyone else at his table had stood, as though sitting through his own conference required a kind of courage he did not possess. Up close he was a large man with a golf tan above the collar and a watch so heavy it seemed orthopedic. He took Doug's hand in both of his.

"Doug. Great to have you. Huge fan. My wife sent me your clip at one-thirty in the morning and I watched it in bed with the sound off because she was asleep, which tells you how committed I was."

"I don't think the sound-off version would help much," Doug said.

Brian bent at the waist laughing. Two people at the table laughed a moment later because he had. "No, that's good. That's good," Brian said. "This is gonna be great."

A woman with a silver Astelline pin was already holding up her phone. "Can I grab one? My husband loves you. He says you said what everybody's thinking."

"I doubt that," Doug said, but she was already beside him.

He stood while she angled the phone high. In the screen he looked older than he expected and more sober than he deserved. The woman pressed in close enough that the side of her blazer touched his sleeve.

"Please tell me you're gonna do the marriage stuff," she said while she framed the picture.

"It's mostly insurance," Doug said.

Her smile held for one extra beat while the sentence found somewhere to go. "Honestly," she said, "same difference."

Denise steered him to a place card two rows from the stage. DOUG BRIDGE, in all caps on heavy cream stock. He sat because the card made it seem like not sitting would create paperwork. On his right was a regional manager from Cincinnati named Carla. On his left was a district rep from St. Louis named Brent. Across from him sat two more people whose names he missed because they were already telling him how glad they were he was here.

"We've needed this all day," Carla said. She was in her forties, wearing a green sheath dress and conference flats and the exhausted brightness of someone nearing the end of a professional obligation. "The afternoon sessions were brutal."

"What were they about?" Doug asked.

"Compliance. Incentives. Resilient selling." She drank half her water. "One of them was somehow about mindset."

Brent said, "That one felt illegal."

The table laughed. Doug laughed half a second late, because it was an accurate statement and accuracy frequently arrived after timing.

Dinner came under silver domes: chicken breast with brown sauce, rice pilaf, green beans laid beside each other as if alignment improved flavor. Doug cut the chicken into small exact pieces. He ate two and one green bean. After that the food expanded in his mouth and would not go down until he pushed water after it.

"So are you gonna do the toast?" Brent asked.

"No," Doug said.

"Not the actual toast," Brent said. "I just mean that version of you."

Doug set down his fork. "There wasn't really a version. There was an event."

Brent looked at him for a second, then laughed because he thought maybe that was the joke. Carla joined him out of courtesy. Doug did not.

"I love that," Carla said. "You're already in it."

"No," Doug said again.

Across the table, a man with a loosened tie told a story about a doctor in Terre Haute who kept spelling ibuprofen with two Bs on every lunch-order form. He did the doctor's voice in a way that was not especially good, and the whole table laughed anyway, not because the story was better than the pool-and-roses claim or because the voice was better than the story deserved, but because they had already agreed to receive him. Doug listened with the attention of a man watching someone assemble a device he had been told was simple.

More people stopped by the table to say hello. A woman from Louisville touched his shoulder and said, "My sister made me watch your clip in the airport and I had mascara running down my face by boarding." A man from Toledo told him, "You don't even need material, man. Just be yourself." Another asked if he could get a selfie for his wife and then, once the photo was taken, said, "You're gonna kill," in the tone people used at funerals when telling a family the deceased looked peaceful.

By the time dessert arrived, Doug had become a small station in the room's pre-show circuitry. People came by, spoke at him, took what they wanted from the exchange, and moved on. He saw the screens every time he looked up. The still image from the wedding stayed above the stage while people scraped cheesecake with dessert forks and checked email under the table and nodded along to conversations about market share. He could not stop looking at the photograph. Whoever had chosen it had frozen him at the exact moment he seemed most certain of himself.

Then Brian went onstage to hand out sales awards. One winner thanked caffeine and fear. Another thanked her district for not quitting on her in Q1. The room laughed hard at both of them. They were bad remarks, Doug thought, but bad in a way the room already knew how to love. Every laugh came quickly, with no negotiation. Doug sat with his hands in his lap and watched what ease looked like at scale.

Denise appeared beside his chair while someone at the podium was still saying "circle back." "Five minutes," she said quietly.

Carla touched Doug's forearm. "Go kill."

Doug stood. "I don't think that's the metric," he said, but nobody heard him because the room was applauding another plaque.

He followed Denise back through the service corridor to the green room.

Doug sat in the green room. It was beige and barely a room, just a partitioned section of backstage with a folding table, four chairs, a mirror ringed with bulbs, and the fruit plate Denise had warned him about. Three of the bulbs were dead. The grapes had moved through ripe and past resigned into something approaching geological. He didn't touch them.

He could hear the ballroom through the wall. Four hundred people, their collective sound arriving as a generalized hum — the same frequency as any crowd in any large room, many conversations producing a single indistinct noise. It was like standing next to a machine he couldn't see. He tried to pick out individual voices and couldn't. They were all one thing. In a few minutes he would stand in front of that thing and try to be funny, and it would either laugh or it would produce the other sound, the one that was patience disguised as silence.

He took the pages out of his jacket pocket. The material. Twenty-five minutes, printed on three sheets in twelve-point type because Simone had told him he shouldn't need to squint. He looked at the first line. He knew the first line. He knew every line. The problem was not the lines.

His phone buzzed. A text from Simone: *Don't rush the opening. Let the room come to you.*

He typed: *OK.*

A second text: *You know this material. Trust it.*

He put the phone facedown on the table. Through the wall, the crowd hummed. Someone laughed — a big laugh that carried through the partition and arrived in the green room as a muffled bark. It was the sound of someone being funny in front of those four hundred people, and that someone was Brian the VP of sales getting one last laugh before the introduction, or someone at a table telling a story about their regional numbers, or a man doing an impression of his boss, or any of the thousands of small human interactions that produced laughter without effort or preparation or fourteen rehearsals between a bathroom and a kitchen.

The fruit plate sat under the fluorescent light, the melon edges glossed over. Doug picked up a grape and squeezed it between his thumb and forefinger. It did not give.

---

When Brian went back onstage for the introduction, he had the energy of someone who had been told to keep it brief and resented the instruction. He wore a suit jacket over a polo shirt, which Doug noticed because it was a choice that committed fully to neither formality nor casualness and achieved a third thing that was worse than both. Brian did six minutes of remarks about regional performance metrics and market penetration trends and then pivoted to the introduction with the practiced ease of a man reading from a teleprompter while pretending he wasn't.

"So, uh, we've got a really special treat tonight," Brian said, and Doug, standing behind the stage-left partition, could hear the speech through a monitor speaker mounted on the wall. "I think most of you have probably seen the video — I mean, who hasn't, right? Eighty million views, the whole thing. And I just wanna say, as someone who gave a best man speech at my brother's wedding that, uh, did NOT go viral — I've got nothing but respect." Laughter. Brian was getting more laughs in his introduction than Doug was going to get in his entire set, and Doug knew it before Brian finished the sentence. The evidence was already organized. He just hadn't written it up yet.

"Please welcome, the man, the legend, the guy your wife has been showing you on her phone — Doug Bridge!"

Doug walked out.

The applause was genuine and warm, the sound of four hundred people who had already decided they liked him. The lights were brighter than the green room or his apartment or anywhere he had ever practiced, and they turned the audience into a dark, applauding mass with individual faces visible only in the first three rows. The mic was heavier than the one Simone had borrowed for their sessions at his kitchen table. It was warm from Brian's hand. Doug held it and walked to the center of the stage and the applause faded and the room waited.

"Thank you," Doug said. "Thanks for, uh. Thanks."

Good start.

The first three rows came into focus. Front row: a table of eight, conference badges still on, water glasses and wine glasses in various states of empty. A man with a red tie who was already smiling, committed to the evening. A woman with reading glasses pushed up on her forehead who looked ready to laugh. These were friendly faces. Doug picked the man in the red tie and aimed the next line at him. Simone's instruction had been simple: find one person and talk to them.

"So Layla — that's my manager, which is still a weird sentence — told me this was a pharmaceutical sales conference." Pause. The room waited. "I work in insurance. So we're both in the business of meeting people right before or right after disappointment."

A laugh. Small. More relief than pleasure. It was enough to let Doug take one more step.

"You people get them at hope," he said. "I get them in a damp basement with a ruined carpet and a Shop-Vac going. You say improved outcomes. I say mildew. My ex-wife used to say I approached every conversation like I was taking a recorded statement."

He waited because there was supposed to be a little turn there, just enough time for them to find it.

"If you've ever had an adjuster tell you he'll circle back after reviewing the policy," Doug said, "that means he's going to disappoint you in a more organized way."

Nothing. A fork clicked against a plate somewhere off to Doug's right. The man in the red tie kept smiling. The woman with the reading glasses slid them down onto her nose.

Doug moved the mic to his left hand and reached for the water on the podium. The rim of the glass hit his front teeth. He set it down too hard, and the thunk came back through the speakers larger than the mistake deserved.

He moved into the claims section, the one Simone had liked best.

"I had a file in Westerville," he said. "Above-ground pool wall split at two in the morning. Six thousand gallons into the neighbor's yard. The neighbor came outside in boxers and dress shoes. That's how fast it was. First thing I had to do was assess the damage. Fence. Patio furniture. Seventeen rosebushes. These men had been friends for eleven years. The flood didn't end it. The rosebushes ended it. Because weather would've covered the roses. If rain kills them, everybody stays friends. If your buddy's pool does it, now you're hearing the phrase third-party structural failure."

A few people laughed at the phrase. A table in the middle gave him a little more than that. Then a woman in the third row — younger, blazer, conference lanyard — looked down at her phone. She didn't pick it up. She just checked it once, like she was verifying an exit. It lasted a second. Doug saw it and it was louder than anything else happening.

He was rushing now. He could hear the set narrowing. The pauses Simone had built into it collapsed first. Then the transitions. He skipped the section about depreciation, his bridge out of the claims material and into the personal stuff, and the whole set lurched with him.

"I live alone in a two-bedroom apartment," he said too fast. "That is an optimistic amount of apartment. The second bedroom has been full of unopened boxes for twelve years. I eat cereal for dinner because there's no cleanup. You pour it, you stand at the counter, you assess the damage, and then the night is over."

A little air moved through the room on "optimistic amount of apartment." Then silverware started again. A server in a black vest slipped between two tables with a tray of coffee cups, using Doug's silence like cover.

"I bought curtains for the second bedroom in 2018," Doug said. "They're still in the Target bag. Apparently the boxes didn't need privacy as bad as I thought."

No one laughed. His free hand had gone to the folded pages in his jacket pocket. He kept smoothing the same crease with his thumb as if the paper were something he could flatten back into usefulness.

"My friend Roy uses the word adequate the way other people use excellent," Doug said. "He bought a lawn mower ten years ago and still talks about it with mild respect."

Nothing there either.

"That's most of adulthood past fifty anyway," Doug said. "You wake up, you assess the damage, you see what's still standing."

A man near the back coughed. Real cough, throat-clearing, probably just convention-center air. In the silence after Doug's line it took over the room. The sound carried the same warning Doug heard behind wet drywall. Small signal. Bigger problem underneath.

At a table near the doors, two men bent toward each other and started talking in voices they must have believed were discreet. A woman by the center aisle gathered her tote bag and eased her chair back while Doug was still speaking. She ducked her head in apology to no one specific and kept going.

He heard himself repeat "assess the damage" and the second use came back dead, already used up. He had six minutes left, including the Roy section Simone liked and the closer built around a coverage gap. The man in the red tie had stopped smiling. The woman with the reading glasses had her arms crossed now. The phone woman had put her phone away. The ballroom held still.

"I think that's probably enough," Doug said.

Someone in the second row started clapping. The rest joined in, one after another, until the whole ballroom was doing what manners required. By the time Doug reached the stage steps, the applause was already breaking apart into chair scrape, glass clink, and the first relieved rise of table talk.

Doug said, "Thank you," and walked offstage.

Backstage, Denise was waiting with a water bottle. "Great job," she said too fast and too bright, the verdict already written before the evidence was in.

"Thanks," Doug said.

From the ballroom, Brian's voice came back over the speakers, already thanking everyone for a record quarter and reminding them about the morning shuttle.

"Brian's gonna do a couple minutes of wrap-up and then we're done for the night," Denise said. "There's a shuttle to the hotel at nine-fifteen if you need it."

Doug did not need the shuttle. He needed to be in his car, alone, driving east on I-70 toward Columbus, but it was nearly nine and he had a room at the Holiday Inn Express on West Washington Street and a pizza he'd ordered on his phone during Brian's introduction, which meant the pizza would arrive before he did, which meant it would be sitting in a hallway getting cold. He gave that problem his full attention because the real one was still unmanageable.

---

The Holiday Inn Express on West Washington Street was four stories of beige siding with a parking lot that was three-quarters empty. Doug's room was on the third floor. The hallway smelled of cleaning solution, carpet adhesive, and the faint residual presence of everyone who had ever walked it with a keycard and a reason for not being home.

The pizza was outside his door, in a box on the carpet. He brought it in. It was from a place called Tony's Original that he'd found on his phone while Brian was talking about Q2 sales targets. The pizza was cold. The cheese had set into one sheet. The crust bent only at the tip. Doug ate two slices standing at the bolted desk. Adequate. Roy's word.

The room had a king bed, a desk built into the wall, a television mounted at an angle that suggested theft prevention, and a bedspread with overlapping brown diamonds and a green the manufacturer probably called sage. The pattern was inoffensive. Doug sat on the bed. The mattress gave exactly enough.

He sat there. The clock on the nightstand said 9:47. A lamp was bolted to the nightstand. Reasonable policy.

His phone had two texts from Layla. The first, from 8:41, said *How'd it go???* The second, from 9:18, said *Call me when you've decompressed. We'll adjust.* There was nothing from Simone. The absence of Simone's name felt more accurate than either text.

In Columbus, Roy would be getting ready for bed. Roy went to bed at 9:45 every night. He'd mentioned it once and left it there.

Doug took out his phone and called Roy. It rang four times.

"Hello."

"It's Doug."

"I know. Your name comes up."

"Right." Doug paused. "Did I wake you up?"

"No. I was reading. There's a book about the Dust Bowl that's better than you'd expect."

"Huh."

"How'd it go?"

He pressed his thumb into one of the brown diamonds on the bedspread. The pizza box sat on the desk. The lamp and television were bolted down, and the desk itself was part of the wall. Everything in the room had been fastened except him.

"Bad," he said.

"Bad how?"

"Bad like everyone was polite about it after."

Roy was quiet for a moment. Doug heard a page turn. Then the house went still again.

"Polite is worse than bad," Roy said.

"Yeah."

"You know where you are with bad."

Doug didn't say anything.

"I stopped at nineteen minutes," he said, after a while. "The set was twenty-five. I got to nineteen and told them that was probably enough."

"Was it?"

"Was it enough? I don't know. It was enough for me. I don't think they would've fought for six more minutes of what I was giving them."

"Then nineteen was the right number."

Doug lay back on the bed. The pillow had been flattened by years of other heads. He stared at the ceiling. It was stippled. A water stain sat in the far corner, brown at the edges. Old damage. Painted over. No active leak. Someone had fixed the sightline and left the structure alone. The most common mistake in residential repair.

"I got paid fifteen thousand dollars tonight," Doug said.

"For nineteen minutes."

"The contract was for thirty. The set was twenty-five. I did nineteen."

"That's still a lot of money for nineteen minutes."

"Seven hundred and eighty-nine dollars a minute. I did the math in the parking garage."

"Of course you did."

They were quiet for a few seconds. Doug could hear Roy breathing, steady and unhurried.

"Are you driving back tonight?"

"No. I've got a hotel room. Holiday Inn."

"How is it?"

The air conditioner rattled once and kept going. The lamp was bolted down. The television was bolted down. The desk was fixed to the wall. The pizza box lid had gone translucent with grease. The bedspread pattern looked like it had surrendered during its own design process.

"It's a Holiday Inn in Indianapolis," Doug said.

"Right."

They sat on the phone. At Mutual Fidelity they could work side by side for ten minutes without speaking and neither man would mistake it for strain. This felt familiar. It was adequate.

"Roy."

"Yeah."

"Simone and I built twenty-five minutes that make sense on paper. Three sections. Callbacks. A closer. I can do all of it in my kitchen. Then I put four hundred people in front of it and the whole thing comes back denied."

"What thing?"

"The thing from the video. The thing they came for. They came to see the guy from the toast and I can't — " Doug stopped. He picked up the cold pizza crust from the nightstand where he'd set it and looked at it and put it down again. "I can't do it on purpose. The video was — I was drunk. I was angry about Brendan's toast being so goddamn perfect and I said things I never meant to say out loud. You can't rehearse that. You can't put it in a set list."

"No."

"Simone knows that too. She knows the sections are in the right order and the room still doesn't want them.
