But she doesn't know what else to try, and I don't know what else to try, and tonight I stood up in front of four hundred people and did the material and it was like — " In the far corner, a water stain sat under a layer of paint that fixed the sightline and nothing else. "It was like filing a claim you know is going to be denied. All the documentation is right, and the forms are filled out, and the loss is real, and you submit it and you know it's coming back rejected because the underlying thing isn't covered. The structure is sound and the answer is still no."

"You should get some sleep," Roy said.

"Yeah."

"Drive back in the morning. Don't drive tonight."

"I won't."

A pause. Then Roy said, "Doug."

"Yeah."

"Nineteen minutes is nineteen more than most people manage."

Doug wasn't sure if this was comfort or observation or just Roy's way of placing a fact in its correct location before closing the file. With Roy, the distinction was often academic.

"Good night, Roy."

"Night."

Doug hung up and set his phone on the nightstand next to the bolted lamp. He took off his shoes and dress shirt and lay on the bedspread in his undershirt and pants. The remote was under his hip. He left it there.

He thought about the woman checking her phone. The cough. Brian saying *the man, the legend*. He thought about the still image from the wedding on the projection screens, frozen into certainty he had never possessed sober. At the wedding, nobody had been braced for him, including him. Tonight everybody had.

Outside the window, traffic moved along West Washington Street. The air conditioner rattled and caught. The fifteen thousand dollars would arrive by direct deposit within fourteen business days.

After a minute he pulled back the covers and got under them.

Down the hall, the ice machine dumped a load into its bin. No one came for it.


# Chapter 10

Doug drove to Dayton on a Saturday because Saturdays were when Gloria expected him and because after Indianapolis he had no interest in finding out what excuse Gloria would reject. The drive was seventy miles on I-70, flat and straight, the fields on either side still brown but showing the first green suggestion of spring, as though Ohio were considering it but hadn't committed. Doug's Civic needed an oil change. The indicator light had been on for eleven days and he'd been meaning to deal with it but hadn't, and the orange glow on the dashboard was the kind of small failure he understood — quantifiable, fixable, ignorable for a while longer.

He was specifically and deliberately not thinking about the Holiday Inn bedspread or the nineteen minutes that should have been twenty-five or the polite applause that was worse than silence. He was thinking about oil changes and exit numbers and whether he should have brought Gloria flowers, which he hadn't, because the last time he'd brought Gloria flowers she had said they were the wrong kind and set them on the counter and never mentioned them again.

Maple Glen Senior Living looked like what it was — a one-story complex off Route 35 with a parking lot that was always half-empty and landscaping that someone maintained without enthusiasm. The sign out front had a maple leaf in the logo. There were no maple trees on the property. Doug had pointed this out to Gloria once. She had said, "There's a motel on Route 40 called the Paradise. There is no paradise on Route 40."

The woman at the front desk was named Angela. She wore a lanyard with a photo ID that showed a version of herself smiling more broadly than the one behind the desk. She gave him the standard "Here to see your mom?" and Doug said, "Yes, thank you," and signed the visitor log. The hallway smelled like institutional carpet and something that was supposed to be lavender but had given up halfway.

Gloria was in her room, sitting in the recliner that she'd brought from the house in Kettering when she moved in three years ago. The recliner was brown. The room was beige. Gloria was wearing a blue cardigan that Doug remembered from at least 2018 and a pair of white sneakers that were so clean they looked ceremonial. She was watching the local news with the closed captions on because the volume on her television had been set to a level that satisfied her and did not change regardless of programming.

"You're late," she said.

Doug looked at the clock on her nightstand. It was 11:07. He had said he'd be there at eleven.

"Seven minutes," he said.

"Your father was never late."

"I hit traffic on 35."

"Harold drove on 35 for thirty years and was never late."

Doug sat on the edge of her bed because the only other chair had a stack of crossword puzzle books on it that had achieved structural integrity and he did not want to be responsible for dismantling them. The bed was made tight enough to suggest military training that Gloria had never received.

"Your belt's on the last hole," she said. "What's in your refrigerator?"

"Food."

"List it."

"Eggs. Mustard. Half a gallon of milk."

"That's three items. That's not a refrigerator. Name one thing you've eaten off a plate this week."

"I had a sandwich yesterday."

Gloria considered this. "What kind?"

"Turkey."

"From where?"

"The place near work."

"The place with Roy."

"Yes."

"He's a strange man, Roy. Not bad. Strange."

Doug waited. He knew why she'd wanted him to come this weekend specifically, and he knew she'd get there, and he also knew that rushing Gloria was like trying to rush an insurance claim through underwriting — it did not speed the process and it annoyed everyone involved.

"I watched it," Gloria said.

She didn't say what. She didn't need to.

"How many times?" Doug asked.

"Four. The first time to see what the fuss was about. The second time because I missed part of it when Ruth called me in the middle of it. The third time because Patricia wanted to see it on my television because hers is too small. The fourth time because I wanted to make sure I was right about what I thought the first time."

"Were you?"

"I'm always right about what I think the first time. That's why I think it."

She picked up a notepad from the arm of the recliner. It was a lined notepad from the Dollar General, the kind with a spiral binding at the top, and Doug could see her handwriting on it — small, precise, tilted slightly to the right. Gloria had taken notes. She had watched her son's drunken wedding toast on the internet and she had taken written notes.

"First note," she said. "You spent the whole time folded over, tugging at that microphone cord and looking at the carpet. If you're going to stand in front of people, then stand. You looked like someone had called the wrong name and you were too polite to say so."

Doug said nothing.

"What you said about Brendan. About his toast being" — she glanced at the notepad — "'the kind of speech you give when you've been practicing in the mirror.' That was mean. He didn't deserve that." She paused. "But you weren't wrong. I watched his toast too. He cried at exactly the right moment. Nobody cries at the right moment. He's a surgeon. Surgeons rehearse. That's the whole job."

"He didn't rehearse his toast."

"He rehearsed. Trust me."

She turned the notepad over. There was more on the back.

"The part about the table," she said. Her voice slowed enough that Doug noticed each word separately. "About nobody sitting across from you again. About making your peace with it and not being at peace."

Doug's hands were on his knees. The bedspread under his palms was thin and pilled and he pressed into it.

"That part was yours," Gloria said. "Everything else — the whiskey, the laughing, the microphone trouble — that was racket. The last time you said anything that plain you were still in seventh grade. Then you got careful. Now you say the version people can stand. That line came out before you could fix it."

She put the notepad down on the arm of the recliner and squared it against the edge, because Gloria squared everything.

"Are they paying you?" she asked.

"For what?"

"For whatever it is you're doing now. The speeches. The television."

"Yes."

"How much?"

Doug considered not answering. He considered it for approximately one second, which was the maximum amount of time deception survived in Gloria's presence.

"Fifteen thousand for the one in Indianapolis."

The number sounded different in Gloria's room. It broke at once into rent, gas, groceries, whole months at Mutual Fidelity. Nineteen minutes for that amount still felt like a processing error somebody would catch and reverse.

Gloria's eyes moved slightly — the calculation was nearly visible, the arithmetic of a woman who had balanced household budgets for forty years on Harold's GM salary and could tell you what a gallon of milk cost in any year you named.

"For how long?"

"Thirty minutes. It was supposed to be thirty minutes."

"And how long was it actually?"

"About nineteen."

"So they paid you fifteen thousand dollars for nineteen minutes of talking."

"Yes."

"How much do you make at the insurance company in a month?"

Doug told her.

She nodded. The nod held a complete financial assessment. Her mouth settled into the expression Gloria wore when a number came out better than expected.

"That's decent money," she said. "For standing up there looking like you wanted to be somewhere else." She studied him. "Did it go well?"

"No."

"How bad?"

"People clapped because they felt sorry for me."

"That is bad."

"Yes."

"But they still paid you."

"They still paid me."

Gloria folded the notepad and set it on the arm of the recliner. "We should go to lunch," she said. "It's pork chop day. The pork chops are terrible but Ruth gets upset if we're late and she's saving seats."

---

The Maple Glen dining room had the acoustic quality of a room where sounds didn't land properly — forks on plates, the hum of a heating system that was still running in late April even though the weather outside had turned mild, conversations held at the volume of people who had lived long enough to stop worrying about being overheard. The carpet was green. The tables were round, six chairs each, and every table had a small vase with a plastic flower in it — the same plastic flowers that had been there every time Doug visited, slightly dusty, aggressively cheerful in a room that was neither.

Ruth was already at the table. She had saved four seats by placing her handbag on one chair and a folded newspaper on two others, and she waved at Gloria with the urgency of a person directing an aircraft onto a carrier deck. Ruth was eighty-one, wore clip-on earrings — gold circles that she adjusted every thirty seconds whether they needed it or not — and had been married three times, which gave her opinions about relationships the weight of field research.

Doug set his tray down and kept one hand on it longer than necessary. Two women at the next table gave him the quick, practiced look he'd been seeing for weeks now — recognition trying to pass itself off as manners.

The tray flexed under his hand. Recognition had its own sequence now: glance, second glance, the quick rearrangement of the face into ordinary politeness. He never knew which part he hated until it was already on him.

"He's here," Ruth said to Gloria, as though Doug were a delivery she'd been tracking.

"He's here," Gloria confirmed.

"I saw you on the computer," Ruth said to Doug. "My grandson Nathan showed me. He's in Cincinnati. He put it on his phone and then we watched it on the computer because the phone is too small and I can't see anything on those phones, I don't know how anyone does."

"Did you," Doug said. He sat down.

"You were funny. I didn't know you were funny. Gloria never said you were funny."

"Gloria doesn't think I'm funny."

"Gloria doesn't think anyone is funny. I thought you were funny. You should do one of those dancing shows. The one where they put regular people with the professionals. You have a nice presence."

Gloria made a short sound in her throat, the one that meant the subject had reached its limit.

Doug felt the back of his neck go hot. Being discussed by strangers in a ballroom had been one thing. Being discussed over pork chops by women who would still be sitting here after he drove home was another.

Patricia arrived with her lunch tray already loaded — pork chop, mashed potatoes, green beans, a roll, a cup of coffee that she held with both hands even when the tray was on the table and there was no practical reason for it. She held coffee in both hands as if it were a posture, her version of crossing her arms. She was seventy-nine, a retired English teacher from Dayton Public Schools, and she still corrected people's grammar reflexively — a habit she either didn't notice or had decided was a public service. She wore a red turtleneck and her reading glasses on a beaded chain around her neck, and she looked at Doug with the measuring expression of a woman who had spent thirty years grading.

"I watched the video," Patricia said. "Twice."

"Everyone watched it twice," Gloria said. "Or four times."

"It's a cry for help," Patricia said. She sat down and placed her napkin on her lap with the precision of a woman setting a table for the dean. "The entire performance is a cry for help. The drinking, the public confession, the humor used as a deflection mechanism for genuine emotional distress. It's textbook."

Doug cut into the pork chop and watched the breading drag loose from the meat. A cry for help implied intention, a flare gun, a plan. What had happened at the wedding and then in Indianapolis had felt less organized than that.

"It's not textbook," Gloria said. "There's no textbook."

"There are many textbooks. I read one last month about emotional avoidance in adult men. The patterns are quite clear—"

"Doug doesn't need a textbook. Doug needs to eat." Gloria looked at Doug's tray. "Is that all you're eating?"

Doug had taken a pork chop, potatoes, and a dinner roll. "This is plenty."

"Take more potatoes. They put butter in them. It's the only thing they do right here."

Deirdre was the last to sit down. She moved carefully but not uncertainly — a tall woman, thin, with white hair that she wore pinned back and the posture of someone who had spent her working life sitting up straight and had never stopped. She was eighty-three. She had been a bookkeeper for a car dealership in Springfield for thirty-one years and had moved to Maple Glen after her husband Ernie died and her house became, in her words, too many rooms for one person. She wore a green wool sweater with a small moth hole near the left cuff that she either hadn't noticed or had decided wasn't the sweater's fault. Her eyes were pale blue and they didn't miss things.

"Hello, Doug," she said.

"Hello, Deirdre."

She unfolded her napkin. The table settled into the rhythm of eating, which at Maple Glen meant the specific pattern of forks and talk and the pauses between talk that were comfortable rather than empty, the silences of people who had known each other long enough that silence wasn't a thing to be filled.

"Ruth wants to put you on one of those television dance programs," Gloria said to Doug.

"I heard."

"Ruth is in a fantasy. At your own wedding you kept your elbows glued to your sides and stared at Megan's shoulder."

"That's true."

"Harold was no better. Music came on and he looked cornered."

Doug could remember the exact feel of Megan's hand between his shoulder blades that night, steering him through a slow song in a rented hall while he counted under his breath so he would not step on her feet. He took a bite of potatoes so he wouldn't have to say any of that.

Ruth adjusted her earrings. "I'm just saying he has a nice presence. On the video. People like him."

"People liked him because he was drunk," Patricia said, taking a precise bite of her pork chop. "That's not the same as presence. That's disinhibition."

"I wasn't that drunk," Doug said. He was that drunk. Everyone at the table knew he was that drunk. Gloria didn't even bother to look at him.

"Eight whiskeys," Gloria said. "You said it in the video. Eight. Your father drank bourbon and he never had more than two. Three at Christmas."

"I don't usually drink that much."

"You don't usually do anything. That's your whole situation." Gloria cut her pork chop into precise squares. She ate pork chops the way she did everything — methodically, completely, without apology. "You do the same things every day in the same order and then one night you do something completely different in front of people with cameras."

"It was about a hundred and forty people."

"It was on the internet. It was eighty million."

Doug ate his potatoes. The mashed potatoes at Maple Glen were actually decent — Gloria was right about the butter — and he focused on them with the attention of a man who preferred potatoes to conversation. Ruth talked about her grandson Nathan's job in Cincinnati, which involved something with computers that she described as "he does the internet, I think he makes the websites." Patricia mentioned that her daughter in Chicago had called twice last month, which Patricia reported with a specific count and no commentary, though the count itself was the commentary — twice in a month was not enough and everyone at the table knew it and nobody said it. Deirdre ate quietly and nodded at things other people said, which was her version of participating and which carried more agreement than most people's whole sentences.

It was Ruth who brought the conversation back. "What did your daughter think?" she asked Doug. "About the video."

Doug put his fork down. "She wasn't thrilled."

"Of course she wasn't thrilled," Patricia said. "It was her wedding. Her father got inebriated and—"

"Patricia," Gloria said.

Patricia stopped. She picked up her coffee cup with both hands and held it like a verdict she was keeping to herself.

"Kelsey is fine," Gloria said. She said it to the table but she was looking at Doug. "She's her father's daughter. She'll be mad and she won't tell anybody. She'll wait. Then she'll say it in front of the wrong people. Bridges do that."

Doug didn't answer. He ate his roll. It was dry and needed butter but he wasn't going to get up to get some because leaving the table meant the conversation would continue without him and he didn't trust what Gloria would say in his absence. He could hear the dining room around them — a man two tables over laughing at something, a woman asking for more water, the particular ambient hum of a building where fifty people lived and ate and sat and waited.

"I thought it was sad," Deirdre said.

The table went quiet. The dining room kept up its low-grade murmur, but at their table every fork stopped for a beat. When Deirdre spoke, people listened, because Deirdre speaking was an event. She could go an entire meal saying nothing and her presence still registered more than Ruth's commentary or Patricia's diagnoses.

"I thought it was the saddest thing I've ever seen," Deirdre said. She was looking at her plate, not at Doug. Her fork was resting on the edge. "And also funny. Both at the same time. I didn't know those could be the same thing, but they were."

Doug looked at his plate. The breading had slid off one side of the pork chop. The plastic flower leaned in its vase. The green carpet showed between the chair legs. Deirdre had put the sentence on the table and left it there.

"Deirdre's right," Gloria said. And that was the end of it. She returned to her pork chop. Ruth started talking about a program she'd watched about the royal family. Patricia corrected Ruth's pronunciation of "Buckingham." The table moved on. What Deirdre had said stayed underneath the ordinary business of eating and talking and being alive on a Saturday in April.

---

After lunch, Doug walked Gloria back to her room. The hallway was wide enough for two people and a wheelchair, which was the architectural statement of Maple Glen — everything built for the body's eventual limitations, every doorway a little wider than necessary, every handrail bolted to the wall at exactly the right height. Someone had put Easter decorations on the bulletin board near the nurses' station — paper eggs in pastel colors and a construction-paper rabbit that looked like it had been made by someone who had a general idea of rabbits but hadn't committed to the details.

Gloria sat in her recliner. Doug sat on the bed again. The crossword books hadn't moved. Through the window he could see the parking lot and beyond it a strip of grass that ended at a chain-link fence, and beyond the fence the back of a Wendy's.

"Do you want coffee?" Gloria asked.

"I'm fine."

"I have instant. It's terrible but it's what I have. Harold used to make real coffee. After he died I switched to instant because there was no point in making a whole pot for one person, and I've been drinking terrible coffee for fifteen years and I don't see any reason to change now."

"You could get one of those single-cup machines."

"I don't want a machine. I want terrible coffee or no coffee. Those are my options and I've chosen."

Doug leaned back against the wall. The wall was cool through his shirt. Gloria's room was small — bed, recliner, dresser, a closet with a sliding door that stuck halfway. On the dresser were three framed photographs: Doug's parents' wedding photo, a picture of Kelsey at her high school graduation, and Harold in his backyard, standing next to a row of tomato plants, squinting into the sun with the expression of a man who was satisfied with his tomatoes and did not require the photograph to validate this.

"Your father," Gloria said.

She said it without transition or preamble, as though the previous conversation had ended and this new one had already started without needing anyone's permission.

"I want to tell you something about your father. Not because it's a secret. It was never a secret. It just never came up, and now it's coming up."

Doug waited.

"The Novaks had us over for dinner. This was — I don't know the exact year — 1989 or 1990. You were in high school. Frank and Jean Novak. They lived on Ackerman, the yellow house with the fence that was always leaning. Frank had just retired from GM. Early retirement. He'd been talking about it for two years — all the things he was going to do. Travel, learn to paint, fix the fence, plant a garden. He had a list. He brought up the list at every dinner party we went to for two years."

Gloria paused. Not for effect. To remember.

"So we're at dinner. Jean made a ham. It was always ham at the Novaks'. There was a salad and dinner rolls and Frank had bought wine, which was unusual because Frank didn't usually buy wine, but he was retired now and he was trying things. Frank starts talking about the list again. He was going to go to Spain. He and Jean were going to go to Spain. Everyone at the table is nodding and smiling and asking questions about Spain, and Harold is sitting there eating his ham and not saying anything. Which wasn't unusual. Harold didn't talk at dinner parties unless he had something to say, and he usually didn't."

She looked at the photograph on the dresser. Harold and his tomatoes.

"And then Harold says, right in the middle of the ham, 'Frank, you're not going to Spain.' Frank laughed a little. Harold said, 'No. You're not. You're going to stay home and keep talking about Spain. That's what you're going to do.'"

Gloria's hands were still in her lap. Her voice hadn't changed register. She told it as if she were recounting breakfast — flat, factual, without performance.

"Jean put her fork down. Donna Miller looked at the tablecloth. Jim Miller took a drink of water. Frank stopped chewing. Nobody said Harold was wrong."

Doug didn't move. The bedspread was thin under his hands. He could hear the television murmuring next door, someone else's afternoon, someone else's room.

"We left early. Jean walked us to the door. She said good night and held the knob so hard I noticed."

Gloria looked at her hands. Her fingers were thinner than Doug remembered them being, the knuckles more pronounced. She wore the wedding ring she'd worn for forty-three years. She had not taken it off after Harold died and Doug had never asked her about it.

"In the car, Harold didn't say a word. He drove the whole way home — eighteen minutes, I know because I watched the clock — with both hands at ten and two. He looked at the road. He didn't say he was sorry. He didn't say he was right."

She stopped again. Doug could hear his own breathing.

"I reached over and held his hand. On the steering wheel. He didn't look at me. I didn't say anything. I held his hand for the rest of the drive and we pulled into the driveway and he turned off the car and we sat there for a minute and then he went inside and went to bed. We never talked about it. Not that night. Not the next morning. Not ever."

She picked at the arm of the recliner where the fabric had worn down to a smoother surface underneath, the spot where her left hand rested every evening.

"Frank never went to Spain," she said. "He sat in that house for fourteen years and then he died."

She looked at the clock on her nightstand.

"It's almost two. You should go. The traffic on 70 gets bad if you wait."

Doug stood up. His legs had stiffened from sitting on the low bed with his back against the wall. Gloria stood too, slower, using the armrest, and for a moment he saw her body's negotiation with standing — how she braced, steadied, settled into her feet — and he looked away because she would not have wanted him to see it.

"Thanks for lunch," he said.

"The pork chops were terrible."

"They weren't bad."

"They were terrible. Don't patronize me about pork chops." She straightened her cardigan. "Bring me those butter cookies next time. The ones from the German bakery on Far Hills. Not the ones from the grocery store. The grocery store ones taste like dust."

"I'll bring the cookies."

"From the German bakery."

"Yes."

"And get your oil changed. I could hear your car from the window when you pulled in."

Doug didn't think that was possible. He also didn't think Gloria was wrong about much. He leaned down and kissed her on the cheek. Her skin was dry and cool and smelled like the soap she'd used his entire life, a specific pharmacy brand in a green wrapper that he could never remember the name of and that he would recognize anywhere.

"Bye, Mom."

"Drive safe. Not fast. Safe. There's a difference and your father never learned it."

He walked down the hallway past the Easter decorations and the nurses' station where Angela was eating a yogurt and reading something on her phone. He signed out in the visitor log. The entry above his was from two days ago — someone named Hernandez, visiting room 14. The parking lot was warmer than when he'd arrived, the afternoon sun on the asphalt, and his Civic ticked with heat when he opened the door.

He sat in the car for a while before starting it. The oil change light was on, orange and patient. Through the windshield he could see the front of Maple Glen — the sign with the maple leaf, the parking lot, the landscaping, the building where his mother lived and ate terrible pork chops and told the truth to anyone who sat still long enough.

He started the car. The engine caught on the first try, which was more than his father's Buick had ever managed, though Doug didn't know why he thought of that now. He pulled out of the lot and turned east toward 70.

The drive home was seventy miles. Exit 38, exit 44, exit 52. The fields were greener now, the dark ground showing spring in uneven patches. He did not turn on the radio. He thought about his father driving home from the Novaks' in silence, both hands at ten and two, and his mother reaching across the seat. Eighteen minutes. He thought about the toast, and the table, and the part Gloria had said was his, and four hundred pharmaceutical reps in Indianapolis clapping because they felt bad for him. He thought about the Novaks' ham cooling on the plates and Frank's list and Jean holding the front door at the edge of her anger.

He drove. The oil change light glowed. At exit 52 he noticed his hands were fixed at ten and two and left them there. The highway was flat and straight and exactly long enough.


# Chapter 11

By early May they'd been at it for three weeks, four days a week, at Doug's kitchen table. Simone had given up on jokes somewhere in the first week, and what they did now didn't have a name, or at least not one Doug recognized from any category of professional work he'd ever participated in. She asked him questions. He answered them. She decided which answers were something and which were nothing, and the criteria for that decision remained, to Doug, as opaque as the underwriting guidelines Mutual Fidelity revised every January without telling anyone what had changed.

The recorder sat between them — a small silver thing she'd carried from New York, scratched along one edge, a strip of masking tape on the back where she'd written "WORK" in black marker, though Doug didn't know what other kind of recording she might need to distinguish it from. She turned it on every morning when she arrived at nine and off again somewhere around one or two, and in between she listened in a way that made Doug hear his own voice in pieces — the pauses, the filler words he hadn't known he used, the places where a sentence died and he had to start a new one from scratch.

The table was the same one he'd bought from the IKEA in West Chester twelve years ago when he'd moved into the apartment. Birch veneer, four legs, no distinguishing features. It had a water ring from a mug he'd set down sometime in 2016 and never dealt with. Simone put her coffee on the same spot every morning, directly on the ring. Doug noticed and said nothing. Saying something would mean admitting he noticed where she put her coffee, and that felt too intimate for the arrangement.

"Tell me about the smell," Simone said. This was a Tuesday, the third week. She had her hair up, held with a pen she sometimes pulled out to write something and then stuck back, so that by noon her hair listed slightly to the left. She was wearing a flannel shirt with a button missing at the cuff, and she'd rolled the sleeves past her elbows. There was an ink stain on the inside of her left forearm that had been there since at least Thursday.

"What smell?"

"You said water damage has a smell. Yesterday, when you were talking about the house in Gahanna. You said you can tell how long the water's been standing by the smell."

"That's not — it's not really a smell thing," Doug said. "It's more of a timeline. You walk in and there's this —" He paused. He was looking at the table. "Okay. So within the first twenty-four hours, it smells like wet. Just wet. Like a basement, or a towel you left in the washer too long. And people are still in their houses at that point, still walking around, still thinking they can fix it with fans and a dehumidifier from Home Depot. The one that costs a hundred and forty dollars. Everyone buys that one."

"Okay."

"By forty-eight hours, if the water's been standing — in the walls, under the floorboards, in the insulation — it starts to turn. It goes from wet to something organic. Not rot yet. Pre-rot. It smells like earth, kind of. Like potting soil that's been sitting in a bag in the garage all winter."

"Pre-rot," Simone said.

"And then by day four or five, it's actual rot. You don't need to test for mold at that point. Your nose knows. The homeowner's been sleeping in that air for days and they've adjusted to it, the way you adjust to anything if you're in it long enough, but you walk in from outside and it hits you. It's sweet and musty and underneath there's something sharp, like wet cardboard that's been in the sun. And the homeowner is standing there asking if their floors can be saved, and you're breathing through your mouth trying to figure out the scope of the remediation, and the answer about the floors is almost always no, but you don't say that in the first five minutes."

Simone was writing something in her notebook. She didn't look up. "Why not?"

"Because they're standing on the floors. The floors they picked out. They're standing there in the house that smells wrong and the floors are soft under their feet and they already know. They just need you to not say it yet."

Simone stopped writing. She looked at Doug for a long moment. This was the face she made when deciding whether something belonged in the notebook or could be left in the room. Doug never understood the rules.

She looked up. "Go on."

"There's nowhere to go. That's what happens."

"Tell me about the dehumidifier. The hundred-and-forty-dollar one."

"It doesn't work. Not for real water damage. It's fine for a damp basement, but if you've got standing water in your walls, it's like trying to bail out a pool with a coffee cup. But people buy it because it's something to do. They need to be responding. They need to be the person who went to Home Depot and came back with equipment."

"Mm."

"I had a family in Reynoldsburg last year — March, pipe burst, five inches of standing water in the finished basement. They'd had it finished two years before. New carpet, new drywall, the whole thing. Twenty-two thousand dollars. I have the number because I looked at the original receipt. And they'd gone out and bought two of those dehumidifiers and a box fan and they had them all running in the basement and the husband was down there with a Shop-Vac and the wife was upstairs on the phone with her sister and the kids were at school and it was —" Doug stopped. He picked at the edge of the table's veneer where it had started to lift. "It was just a Tuesday. Just a regular day where everything they'd spent two years building was wrecked and they were trying to fix it with equipment that cost three hundred dollars total."

Simone sat back. She tapped her pen on the notebook twice. Doug had started keeping track of these things: the pen in her hair, the coffee on the water ring, the two taps when something mattered. He was an adjuster. He noticed patterns.

"What," Doug said.

"Nothing." She crossed something out in her notebook and wrote something else. "What else do you know about that costs a hundred and forty dollars and doesn't work?"

"It's a claim. I'm describing a claim."

"I know."

Doug thought about this. "My reading glasses."

Simone's mouth moved but she didn't laugh. She wrote it down.


By the third week Simone wasn't asking for jokes anymore.

On Wednesday she asked about cereal.

"What kind?"

"Raisin Bran. Store brand, not Kellogg's. Kellogg's is $4.89 and the store brand is $3.19 and they're the same cereal. Same raisins, same flakes, same sugar content. I've done the comparison."

"You've compared them."

"The Kellogg's has six more raisins per serving. I counted once."

Simone looked at him. "You counted the raisins."

"It was a Sunday."

There was a silence. Outside, the sound of a car door on the street, and then nothing.

"And this is dinner," Simone said.

"Most nights. It's fast, it doesn't generate dishes. One bowl, one spoon. You rinse them and you're done. A real dinner — a cooked dinner — is a plate and a pan and a cutting board and a spatula and by the time you've cleaned up, you've spent more time on the aftermath than on the meal. The ROI on cooking for one person is terrible."

"The return on investment of dinner."

"If you factored in your time at even minimum wage, every meal you cook alone costs about thirty dollars."

"You've done that math."

"Yes."

"Why is cereal better at night?" Simone asked. "That's the part I want. Not the economics. Why do you like it better as dinner than as breakfast?"

Doug looked at the water ring on the table. "Because in the morning it's what you're supposed to eat. You eat cereal for breakfast and it's just — it's breakfast. Nobody thinks about it. But at night, it's a decision. You're choosing not to cook. You're choosing not to order something. You're choosing —" He picked at the table's veneer. "I mean, I own a stove. I have pans. But the cereal is four minutes start to finish and I didn't have to — perform it. The meal. Nobody's watching you eat cereal at nine-fifteen."

Simone didn't say anything for a long time. Then she picked up her pen and wrote for a few seconds.

After a while she said, "Tell me about Roy's sandwich."

"Turkey on wheat with mustard. He's been ordering the same thing for nine years. One time they put tomato on it by accident and he peeled the slices off and set them on the edge of his tray and didn't mention it. He just ate the sandwich. The next time we went in, his order was correct. I don't know if he said something between visits or if the guy behind the counter noticed. It was never discussed."

"What does Roy think about all of this?"

"He didn't watch the video."

"I know. But what does he think about you doing this — the sessions, the Netflix thing, the whole trajectory?"

Doug considered this. "Roy thinks what he thinks. He doesn't — he's not going to have an opinion and then share the opinion with me so I feel something about his opinion. If I told Roy I was quitting to do comedy, he'd say 'Okay.' If I told him I was staying at Mutual Fidelity forever, he'd say 'Okay.' The okay would be the same both times."

"And that's comforting."

"I didn't say that."

"I'm saying it."

Doug looked at the water ring. "Yeah. It is."

By then Doug knew how it felt when she'd found something: the notebook came back open, the room went still, and whatever he'd just said stayed on the table between them longer than it should have.

When she asked about fame, he heard the change almost immediately. He gave her the public version right away: people coming up to him at Kroger, at gas stations, asking for pictures, asking for the toast. A man in the produce section had asked him to do it while Doug stood there holding bananas. Even as he was saying it, he could hear himself going generic.

Simone closed the notebook.

"You start talking about yourself and it goes dull," she said. "Not careful. Not private. Dull."

Doug looked at her.

"The insurance stuff, Roy, the cereal, that dehumidifier — you're inside that," she said. "This is you filling out a form about a man named Doug."

Doug felt the heat climb his neck. Bad material he could understand. Dull sounded permanent.

"That's a terrible note."

"It's the note."

The next day she asked about the toast. Kelsey's wedding, Brendan's toast, the microphone heavier than he'd expected: even inside the first sentence he could feel the whole thing flattening under his own hands while Simone sat still and waited for him to hear it. When he finally did, she asked if he wanted to break for lunch.

Outside, a car passed with its windows down and bass shaking the kitchen glass. Early May in Clintonville: the street trees were fully leafed out, and the parked cars along the curb had already started collecting a yellow film of pollen.

Simone went to the sandwich place around the corner — she'd been going there since her second week in Columbus and ordered a different sandwich every time, which Doug found inefficient. He ate a granola bar and drank water and stood in his kitchen looking at the IKEA bookshelf and the art print Megan had left behind, a watercolor of something that might have been a bridge or a pier. It occurred to him Megan had probably left it because she didn't want it. The thought arrived twelve years late and stung anyway.

Simone came back with a Cuban sandwich and a coffee. She set the coffee on the water ring.

"More insurance," she said.

Doug talked about depreciation. Thursday, or maybe still Wednesday; the sessions had started to bleed together. Same table, same recorder, same strip of light moving across the kitchen. He talked about what happens to the value of a couch over time — how a couch you bought for twelve hundred dollars is worth, after ten years, maybe two hundred, and the difference between what you paid and what it's worth now is depreciation. It was the most honest math he knew. It accounted for wear. It admitted use diminishes value.

"And that's how it works with everything," Doug said. "Not just couches. Appliances, roofing, carpeting, your water heater, your HVAC system. Everything has a schedule. Everything depreciates at a predictable rate. You can look it up. There are tables."

"And marriages?" Simone said. She said it casually, like it was the next item on a list, but her pen was still.

Doug looked at the recorder. "There's no table for that."

"But."

"But if there were." He paused. He was looking at the recorder. "If there were, you'd have your replacement cost and your actual cash value. Replacement cost is what it would take to get the same thing new — same terms, same coverage. Which doesn't mean anything for a marriage, but the formula. Actual cash value is what it's worth now, after everything. After the years and the arguments and the Tuesday nights and the way she loaded the dishwasher. Eighteen years of noticing how she loaded the dishwasher." He looked at the table. "The actual cash value is what's left when you subtract all of that."

"And nobody puts that in the paperwork."

"Nobody puts that in the paperwork."

Simone wrote something. She didn't look up.


By Friday they were still at the table at four. The light had gone amber and moved across the table from the recorder to Simone's notebook to the space between their hands.

They were both tired. Simone had stopped taking notes an hour earlier. The recorder kept running. After long enough they both forgot it.

She was asking about the apartment itself. She'd already spent days on the cereal, the bookshelf, the art print, the second bedroom with the boxes, and now she wanted the life around those things.

"How long have you been here?"

"Twelve years. Since the divorce."

"And you moved here right after."

"Within a month. Megan — we sold the house. Split it. I found this place online. I didn't see it in person before I signed the lease. The pictures were accurate."

"That's a very insurance-adjuster way to pick an apartment."

"The pictures were accurate."

Simone smiled. It wasn't the diagnostic smile. She was tired, and tired took the edge off her face. She'd told him once that Columbus was messing with her sleep because her body stayed on New York time. By late afternoon it showed. She sat deeper in the chair and stopped looking ready to spring out of it.

"How did you know it was over?" Simone said. "Your marriage."

Doug looked at her. Simone wasn't looking at her notebook. She was looking at him.

"The conversation, or —"

"Whatever it was. The moment that made it real."

Doug didn't answer right away. He could feel the pull toward telling it neatly, the way Megan would have told it. Simone sat very still.

"We'd been — Megan had been saying things for a while. About how she felt. About the marriage. She said it in that organized Megan voice. She'd say, 'I think we need to acknowledge that this isn't working,' and I'd say, 'What isn't working?' and she'd look at me and I genuinely didn't know what she meant. Or I did. I don't know." He paused. "There's a thing in insurance where you can know a claim exists without understanding your exposure. You got the notification. You know the number. You just haven't processed what it actually costs you yet. I think that's what I had. The notification."

Simone didn't react to the metaphor. She waited.

"So she left. One day I came home from work and she'd taken things. Not everything. Her clothes, her toiletries, some books. She took the KitchenAid mixer — four hundred and twenty dollars, I never used it — and she left the art print." He gestured at the wall without looking at it. "We had different stuff by then. Separate accounts. She'd already been moving into the new place incrementally, and the day she took the last of it was just — it was a Wednesday. I came home and I could see what was gone. I walked through the rooms and I could inventory it."

"But."

"But I was fine. I was processing. I knew it was coming. I'd known for months. The paperwork was in progress. I was managing it."

He heard the word after he said it and wished he'd chosen another.

"And then I took a shower. End of the day, I always shower when I get home, I still do. And I got in and her shampoo was gone from the ledge."

Simone did not move.

"I don't remember the brand. Something with green tea in the name. It came in a tall green bottle and she'd been using the same shampoo the entire marriage. Eighteen years. The bottle was always on the ledge. Every morning, every night. Even after she started talking about leaving, even after the conversations and the lawyers and the listing agreement — the bottle was there. It was the last thing of hers in the bathroom."

The apartment was quiet. Outside, late-afternoon sounds — a dog somewhere, a lawn mower whining once before it caught, the ordinary May noise of everybody trying to get ahead of the grass. The stripe of light on the table had reached Doug's hand.

"Everything else I could process. The mixer, the books, the clothes — those were items. I could inventory them, I could assess the depreciation, I could arrive at a number and the number would be accurate and I'd be fine. But the shampoo —" He looked at his hand in the light. "You can't depreciate shampoo. It's a consumable. It has no residual value. It's just gone, and the ledge where it sat is empty, and every time you take a shower you see the empty space on the ledge where it was. And after a while you don't see it anymore, because you adjust, because that's what you do, but for the first — I don't know. Six months. A year. Every shower."

He stopped. The recorder hummed between them, so quiet it was nearly the sound of the room itself.

Simone picked up her pen. She did not put it in her hair. She opened her notebook to a clean page and wrote. She wrote for a while, longer than she'd written down any single thing Doug had said in three weeks.

She didn't laugh. She didn't give him one of her work words. She just kept writing.

Doug watched her hand move across the page. She had water damage and cereal and Roy's sandwich and the art print and the boxes and now the empty ledge in his shower. It was all the same pile. If this ever went onstage, people wouldn't just be hearing jokes. They'd be seeing how he lived.

He was going to be known.

The pen stopped. Simone looked up. Her eyes were red at the edges. She closed the notebook and set the pen on top of it.

"We should stop for today," she said.

"Okay."

Neither of them moved. The light had shifted past Doug and was climbing the wall behind him, up over the watercolor Megan hadn't wanted.

"I've never told anyone that," Doug said. "The shampoo."

Simone reached forward and clicked the recorder off. The click barely disturbed the room.

"I know," she said.

They sat until the light thinned. Simone finished her cold coffee. Then she packed the notebook and recorder into her canvas bag.

"Same time Monday," she said.

"Yeah."

At the door she half-turned back to him.

"The shampoo isn't for the set."

Doug looked at her.

"I'm writing it down because you didn't dress it up," she said. "That's different."

He nodded. "Okay."

She left. He heard her on the stairs, then the outside door.

The apartment went quiet. Her coffee cup was in the sink. Her pen cap had rolled onto the table while she was packing up. He picked it up and set it on the counter.

He went to the bathroom. He didn't turn on the shower. He looked at the ledge. His own shampoo sat there, blue cap, half empty.

Back in the kitchen, he poured a bowl of store-brand Raisin Bran and ate it standing at the counter. The milk was two days from expiration and still fine. He rinsed the bowl and spoon. He washed Simone's coffee cup and set it in the rack.

The pen cap stayed on the counter.


# Chapter 12

Simone's pen cap was still on the counter on a Tuesday morning in May, next to a box of Grape-Nuts Doug hadn't put away, when Alex Chen unzipped a canvas messenger bag with a fraying strap and took out a digital recorder. He had come to Doug's apartment with a thesis. The thesis was that Doug Bridge represented something about contemporary masculinity, its failures, its accidental honesty, its unwillingness to perform. Alex had explained this over email in language that Doug read twice and understood about forty percent of. He'd agreed to the profile because Layla told him to and because saying no would've required a reason he didn't have.

Alex was thirty-one. He'd chosen journalism late enough to know the business was collapsing and still carried himself like the decision needed defending. He wore a denim jacket that was either vintage or just old, Doug couldn't tell, and he had a pen clipped to the breast pocket that he kept removing, chewing the cap, and replacing. The cap was destroyed, soft plastic cratered by months of thought. He also had allergies. He'd blown his nose three times before Doug got the coffee made, and he kept rubbing his eyes with the heel of his hand in a way that made him look like he'd been crying, which he hadn't. Columbus in May was a pollen event. Doug had processed a claim once involving mold remediation triggered by spring pollen accumulating in an HVAC system, a fringe case but not an unprecedented one.

"So," Alex said. He pressed record on the digital recorder and set it on the kitchen counter between them. "I want to start with something maybe a little abstract."

"Okay," Doug said.

"What drives you?"

Doug looked at the recorder. The red light was on. "The Honda, mostly," he said. "Gray Civic. If I leave after 7:20 Henderson backs up."

Alex smiled and waited, apparently trusting the silence to do some of the work. "No, I mean what motivates you? What gets you up in the morning?"

Doug thought about this. His alarm was set for 6:40. He got up because it went off. Before the alarm, he'd been getting up because his bladder woke him around 6:15, but he'd adjusted his water intake after 8 p.m. and that had mostly resolved. He didn't think this was what Alex meant.

"I have a job," Doug said. "The job starts at eight. So I get up in time for the job."

Alex bent over a Moleskine from a bookstore, its pages dense with small handwriting that tilted left. Doug noticed the tilt because he'd processed a claim once involving handwriting analysis, a forged receipt, a guy in Reynoldsburg, nine hundred dollars on a pressure washer that didn't exist. The analyst had said left-tilting handwriting indicated introversion. Doug didn't know if that was true for Alex or for the Reynoldsburg guy. Upside down, he could make out three fragments before Alex's hand covered them: "purpose = route," "job starts at 8," and, on a separate line, "won't generalize."

"Tell me about the toast," Alex said.

"Which part?"

"How it felt. That night. Standing up there."

"I was drunk," Doug said.

"Right, but"

"I'd had eight whiskeys. I don't normally have eight whiskeys. I don't normally have four. The bartender was pouring them tall because the venue was all-inclusive and he'd stopped measuring."

"But what made you stand up? What made you give the toast?"

"Brendan had just given one," Doug said. "My ex-wife's husband. His was very good. People cried."

"And that motivated you to"

"I don't know if motivated is the right word. I was already standing. Or I was sitting and then I was standing. I don't remember the transition."

Upside down, Doug saw Alex box the word "transition," then add "stood without deciding" underneath it. He had a feeling that second line was going to survive the editing process longer than the first one. What Doug meant was that he physically did not remember going from chair to upright.

They talked for another hour. Alex asked about Doug's childhood, his marriage, his divorce, his relationship with Kelsey, his job, his apartment, his daily routine, his feelings about fame, his feelings about comedy, his feelings about his feelings. Doug answered as if each question were a coverage inquiry, the specific information requested and nothing additional. Alex kept waiting for the additional part. Doug kept not providing it.

At one point Alex asked, "Do you think of yourself as funny?"

"No," Doug said.

"But eighty million people watched that video and laughed."

"Some of them were probably just watching. You don't know everybody laughed."

"The comments"

"I've read the comments. Some of them think I'm an asshole. One guy said I should be arrested for public intoxication. He got twelve hundred upvotes."

Alex paused. He rubbed his eye again. His allergies were getting worse. The morning light through Doug's kitchen window had a visible quality to it, a fine yellow-green dusting on the sill that Doug wiped down every other day and that returned every other day because that was what May in central Ohio did. Alex had stopped wiping his nose and had moved to a strategy of sustained sniffling that he seemed to think was less noticeable. It was not less noticeable.

"Can I use your bathroom?" Alex asked.

"Down the hall," Doug said. "Door on the left. Door on the right is the office."

"You have a home office?"

"It doesn't have a computer in it," Doug said. "Just boxes."

Alex hesitated, clearly wanting to ask about the boxes, and then went to the bathroom. Doug heard him blow his nose four times, a sequence that built in intensity like a man trying to clear a drain. Doug sat at the kitchen counter and looked at the recorder. The red light was still on. It was recording the silence, the refrigerator hum, the muffled honking from the bathroom. Somewhere in a digital file, this would exist: Alex Chen's sinuses and Doug Bridge's Frigidaire, captured for journalism.

When Alex came back, his eyes were redder and he stopped at the office door.

"Do you mind if I look?"

"It's boxes," Doug said.

"I know."

Doug showed him anyway. The room held seven taped moving boxes, an empty desk, a folding chair with two winter coats over the back, and a bread machine still in its original carton because somebody had once believed Doug and Megan were going to bake bread. One box had KITCHEN written on the side in black marker. One said BOOKS. One just had a thick strip of old masking tape where the label had peeled away. Alex stood in the doorway as if the room might start talking if he waited it out. Then the pen started going fast enough that Doug caught only fragments: "second bedroom unopened," "spare room held in reserve," "boxes still sealed."

Doug had not been holding anything in reserve. He had moved twelve years ago and then kept going.

---

Alex was supposed to shadow Doug for two days. Tuesday was the work day, office, lunch with Roy, then Simone in the late afternoon. Layla had called it "the full Doug experience," and Doug had not asked what that meant because he suspected the answer would involve the word "authentic" and he'd heard that word enough times from enough people who did not mean the same thing by it.

They took Doug's Civic to Mutual Fidelity. Alex sat in the passenger seat studying the cracked dashboard, the floor mat salt-stained from winter, the gas receipts folded in the center console.

"How long have you had this car?"

"Three years. Three years and five months, actually. I bought it in December."

"Do you like it?"

"It runs," Doug said. "The heat works. The AC works when it feels like it. The passenger window sticks in cold weather but we're past that now."

Alex thumbed a note into his phone. Doug drove.

The Mutual Fidelity parking lot was gray, flat, half-full at 7:52 a.m. Alex photographed the building. Doug had worked there for fifteen years and had never once thought it required a photograph.

Inside, Doug introduced Alex to Janet, who shook Alex's hand and said, "We're very proud of Doug." In fifteen years Janet had never expressed pride in anything outside the department's quarterly throughput numbers. Doug filed the sentence under information requiring a new category and kept walking.

Alex sat in a chair Doug borrowed from the break room and watched him work through a kitchen fire claim from Grove City: grease, not electrical, which mattered because the policy excluded appliance failure and the homeowner was trying to argue ignition malfunction. Doug reviewed the photographs, noted the pour pattern on the stovetop, wrote his assessment. Forty minutes passed. Alex kept watching, notebook on his knee, pen cap in his mouth, eyes going red again from whatever Columbus was doing to his face.

The next file was water damage in Gahanna. Doug called the homeowner because the contractor estimate had jumped by four thousand dollars overnight and people were usually more honest on the first call than the third. The woman answered with a baby crying in the background and said she was sorry, she was sorry, she had not slept. Doug told her which receipts he needed, which endorsement applied, and how to keep the ceiling from coming farther down before the mitigation company arrived. When she apologized again, he said, "It's water damage. It doesn't improve with guilt."

The woman laughed once, by accident. "Okay," she said. "Okay."

That got the pen cap out of Alex's mouth for the first time in forty minutes.

"Is this typical?" Alex asked when Doug closed the file.

"It's a Tuesday," Doug said.

"I mean, are your days usually like this?"

"Usually. Sometimes there's a site visit. Today there isn't."

A coworker named Diane Posner paused at Doug's desk with a yogurt cup in one hand and a plastic spoon in the other. "My daughter showed me a TikTok of you," she said. "She's fifteen. She said you're iconic."

Diane pronounced "iconic" as if it might require follow-up care.

"Tell her thanks," Doug said.

"She wants to know if you're on TikTok."

"I'm not."

"She says you should be."

"I don't know how it works."

Diane nodded, satisfied and disappointed at the same time, and moved on. Alex was already tapping his phone with both thumbs.

"Did you mean that literally?" Alex asked.

"About TikTok?"

"That you don't know how it works."

"Yes," Doug said. "Also I don't want another password."

Lunch was at the sandwich place. It had been the sandwich place for nine years, and it was called Sal's, though there was no evidence a person named Sal had ever been involved in its operation. The menu hadn't changed since Doug started going there. Roy hadn't changed either. Roy ordered turkey on wheat, mustard, no tomato. Doug ordered ham and Swiss. They sat.

"Roy, this is Alex. He's writing a profile."

Roy looked at Alex with the measured disinterest he brought to most new information. "About what?"

"About me," Doug said.

"Huh," Roy said. He unwrapped his sandwich.

Alex set the recorder on the table. "Roy, what was your reaction when you saw the toast video?"

"Didn't see it," Roy said.

"You never watched it?"

"No."

"Why not?"

Roy took a bite of his sandwich. He chewed. He swallowed. The pause went on long enough for Alex to shift in his seat, which was something Doug had learned not to do years ago. You waited for Roy. Roy did not speed up.

"I work next to him," Roy said.

"But your best friend went viral. Eighty million people"

"Doug's my coworker," Roy said. "He's also my friend. He was both before the video and he'll be both after. I don't need a phone to tell me what he's like. I've been eating lunch with him for nine years."

"That's actually a really interesting perspective. Can I quote you on that?"

Roy looked at Doug. Doug knew the look. It was the look Roy gave when someone treated a plain fact like a philosophical position.

"It's your article," Roy said.

Alex asked if he thought Doug's honesty was what people were responding to.

Roy opened a mustard packet. "I don't know what strangers respond to," he said. "He still eats the same sandwich."

Alex copied that one almost verbatim.

They ate. Alex asked more questions. Roy answered them in the fewest possible words. At one point Alex asked what Roy thought about Doug's fame and Roy said, "I don't think about it," and Alex looked up from the notebook as if Roy had handed him a theory instead of a plain description of his operating system.

Alex asked Doug if his friendship with Roy had changed since the video. Doug looked at Roy. Roy was eating his sandwich. Nothing about Roy had changed.

"No," Doug said.

"Not at all? Even"

"It hasn't changed," Doug said.

Roy folded his sandwich paper into a square. "More people call him," he said. "That's different from change."

Alex put a small star beside that one.

The session with Simone was at Doug's apartment at four o'clock. They'd been meeting four days a week for over a month now, and the apartment had accumulated evidence of the work: index cards on the kitchen table with phrases scribbled on them, a digital recorder that belonged to Simone, a legal pad full of notes in handwriting Doug couldn't read because it was worse than his, which he had not previously thought possible.

Simone was sitting at the table when they got back. She had a coffee from somewhere and was reading her phone with flat irritation, scrolling the way people did when they'd already had enough of the internet and were still making themselves take more. She looked up at Alex and then at Doug.

"He's the journalist," Simone said. It wasn't a question.

"Alex Chen," Alex said, extending his hand.

Simone shook it. She had rings on three fingers, not decorative rings, working rings that stayed on because taking them off was more effort than leaving them there. Her hair was up, held in place by a clip that was losing its structural battle. "Layla told me you were coming. What's the angle?"

"I'm interested in Doug as a kind of"

"A symbol?"

"I was going to say a case study in"

"He's not a case study," Simone said. "He's a guy. We're trying to write material." She looked at the recorder in Alex's hand. "You can stay if you stay quiet."

Doug sat. Alex's recorder was on the table next to Simone's recorder, two red lights blinking at slightly different intervals. He felt like a claim being documented from two angles.

Simone said, "Tell me the deductible story again. Start where it gets bad."

He told it. It was about a man in Hilliard whose roof leaked during a storm and who filed a claim not realizing that his deductible was five thousand dollars. The total damage was fifty-two hundred, so the insurance company's portion was two hundred. The man called Doug to ask if this was a mistake. Doug explained that it wasn't. The man said, "I've been paying premiums for twelve years." Doug said, "The premiums purchased the policy. The policy has a deductible." The man was quiet for a long time and then asked, "So what did I pay for?" Doug hadn't had a good answer because the man was technically right. He'd paid for the idea that something would be there when things went wrong, and something was there, but the gap between the idea and the two-hundred-dollar check was a gap Doug had spent his career standing inside, explaining to people why it was that size.

Simone listened without interrupting. Then she said, "After he asked that, what happened?"

"He breathed into the phone for a while," Doug said.

"Was he yelling?"

"No. He was trying not to."

Alex leaned forward. "Because the institution promises safety and then"

Simone held up a hand without looking at him. "Don't help."

Alex stopped.

Simone turned back to Doug. "Then what?"

"He asked if there was anybody else he could talk to."

"And?"

"I said no."

Simone nodded once. "Start there next time. Not with the roof. Not with the deductible. Start with him finding out you're the last stop and you still can't do anything."

Alex wrote fast enough that his pen scratched. Upside down, Doug could read: "two-hundred-dollar check," "no one else to call," and, on the line below that, "trained to swallow it." Doug had not said anything about swallowing it. He had said the man breathed into the phone and asked if there was somebody else.

They worked another forty minutes. Doug talked about claims and Simone kept stopping him at the moments where the mundane turned specific enough to be strange, or sad, or accidentally precise about something that wasn't insurance at all. At one point Simone looked at Alex and said, "You're going to make this sound like therapy, aren't you."

"I'm trying not to," Alex said.

"Try harder."

Alex smiled without much humor and kept his head down. After a while Doug saw three items underlined hard enough to dent the page: "counter dinners," "no one else," "name-brand cereal." It was like watching somebody sort his life into a version small enough to carry home.

After the session, they stopped at the Kroger on High Street because Doug needed groceries and Alex apparently needed groceries to happen on the record. Doug bought eggs, bread, and coffee. He explained the coffee sale cycle because Alex asked. Near the cereal aisle, a college kid in a Buckeyes hoodie recognized him, asked for a photo, looked at it, and said, "You look like you're getting arrested."

At checkout Alex touched the Grape-Nuts box in the cart. "Can I put this in?"

"It's cereal," Doug said.

"What kind matters."

"It matters that it doesn't get soggy."

Alex touched the box again, lightly, like it had just become evidence.

In the parking lot, while Doug loaded the trunk and put the eggs on top, Alex asked, "How's fame changed you?"

Doug shut the trunk. "I switched from store-brand Raisin Bran to Grape-Nuts," he said. "Dollar forty more. Sometimes a dollar sixty, depending on the week."

"That's it?"

Doug leaned against the Civic. The money was real. The attention was real. The contract with Layla, the sessions with Simone, the Netflix deal, the pharmaceutical conference in Indianapolis where four hundred people had watched him fail and then clapped, the kid in the hoodie, the photo that would end up on somebody else's feed, all of it was real. But the cereal was the only part he could line-item.

"That's the main change," Doug said.

---

The profile published nine days later. Layla texted Doug the link at 6:47 a.m. with three exclamation marks and a fire emoji. Doug was brushing his teeth. He read it standing at the bathroom sink, his mouth full of toothpaste, because he didn't want to wait the thirty seconds it would take to spit and rinse.

The headline was: **"America's Most Reluctant Comedian."**

The article was long and very good. Alex could write. It opened in Doug's kitchen with the Grape-Nuts on the counter and the pollen on the sill, which Doug had to admit were both there. After that accuracy ended. In the second paragraph Doug was a "midwestern Everyman." In a subheading he was "the last honest man in America." The piece made it sound as if he'd stood up at the wedding on purpose.

Alex had also described the apartment as "spartan but deliberate" and turned the second bedroom into "a room kept in reserve, seven sealed boxes standing in for the years after the divorce." Doug had not sealed the boxes as a gesture. They were taped because that was how boxes worked. The tape had stayed on because opening them would mean deciding what to throw out, where to put what stayed, and whether he was going to buy shelving. He had done none of those things.

The cereal became "the one visible luxury he'll allow himself." Roy became "the coworker who refused to confuse virality with knowledge." Simone appeared for one paragraph as "the comedy writer who hears the wound inside the anecdote." Alex had also set Doug's line about not knowing how to be a public person under a paragraph about modern masculinity. Doug had said it because Diane asked whether he had TikTok. He meant he didn't know how to use the app. On the page it read like he'd been cornered into telling the truth.

Layla called at 7:30. "It's incredible," she said. "The engagement's insane. It's already been shared like forty thousand times."

"Forty thousand," Doug said.

"I've had three calls this morning. A podcast wants you on to talk about fatherhood. Some leadership retreat in Denver wants a lunch conversation about authenticity. The literary agent's back about the book deal. And, okay, don't freak out, the *Times* wants to do a follow-up."

"The Dispatch?"

"The New York Times, babe. The *Times* Times."

Doug said nothing. He was still standing in the bathroom. The toothpaste had dried on the corner of his mouth. He looked at himself in the mirror: the same face, the thinning hair, the reading glasses on the back of the toilet where he'd left them two days earlier. He still looked like an insurance adjuster from Columbus who had once gotten very drunk at a wedding. The mirror didn't know about the article. The mirror showed what it showed.

"Doug? You there?"

"Yeah," he said. "I'm here."

At the office, four people mentioned the profile before Doug reached his desk. Janet stopped him in the hallway and said, "I read it. Very thoughtful," in a tone usually reserved for eulogies. Diane Posner leaned out of her cubicle and said, "My daughter liked the room-with-the-boxes part. She thought that was sad." A woman from accounting whose name Doug could not remember said, "My husband liked the cereal part." Doug thanked all of them, sat down, turned on his computer, and opened the claims queue.

Roy was already at his desk. His coffee was half-drunk.

"I read the headline," Roy said.

"Just the headline?"

"It was four words. That's three too many." Roy took a sip of his coffee. "They called you a comedian."

Doug waited.

"You gave one speech, Doug. You were drunk."

Roy turned back to his screen.

Doug looked at his own. Fourteen claims. A burst pipe in Westerville. A chimney fire in Dublin. A woman in Upper Arlington whose sump pump had failed during last week's rain. She'd photographed her basement from four angles. She'd kept the receipt for the pump. Her handwritten description got tighter and more frantic in the margins where she'd added what she'd remembered later.

Janet forwarded three phone messages from reception because people kept asking for him by full name. One was from the fatherhood podcast. One was from the Denver retreat. One was from a college paper whose reporter wanted to know whether the second bedroom was "still unresolved." The second bedroom was full of boxes. The article had been read more carefully than the apartment had.

The desk phone's red light blinked through the morning. Layla, probably, or a producer, or somebody who wanted the man who bought symbolic cereal and lived beside meaningful boxes. That version of Doug was already easier to book than the real one. He fit in a subject line. He fit in a panel theme. He fit in the clean white space next to a professional headshot.

Doug opened the Upper Arlington file. Coverage applied. No exclusions. He wrote the assessment, entered the determination code, and moved to the next screen.

The phone kept blinking. Doug picked it up. It was Layla. He put her on hold and dialed the woman in Upper Arlington. She picked up on the second ring and immediately started apologizing for the water in her basement.


# Chapter 13

Doug parked twenty minutes early on the side street south of campus and sat with the engine off, looking at the Thai place. He had been coming here for fourteen years, long enough to watch the awning fade from red to something closer to the color of salmon left out overnight, long enough to register that the ceramic cat in the window had migrated from the left side of the sill to the right at some point in 2019 and never understand it. The storefront was still wedged between the dry cleaner and the vacant lot that had been vacant for as long as he'd been coming. On Saturday, just past noon, he pushed the door open and the bell rang.

Kelsey wasn't there yet. Doug was twenty minutes early in the same way he was early for everything that might go badly: the diner in Worthington three months ago, every parent-teacher conference and dentist appointment and claims assessment in the decades before that. He showed up early automatically, reflexively, because the alternative was arriving to conditions you hadn't assessed.

The woman at the front — Doug had been seeing her for fourteen years and did not know her name, a failure he'd compounded annually until it became irreversible — pointed him toward the booth in the back without being asked. She was wearing the jade bracelet she always wore, and reading glasses that were new since the last time he'd been here, thin gold frames that she looked over rather than through as she walked him to the table. The booth's vinyl had cracked along the right side where it met the wall, a split that had started small and widened steadily because nobody repaired it. Doug sat on the left side. He always sat on the left side. Kelsey sat facing the door, which she'd done since she was a teenager, and he'd never asked why.

He opened the menu out of habit, not need. He had been ordering the same thing here for fourteen years: the massaman curry, number thirty-seven, which came with a small red sticker on the menu indicating spice, though the menu's rating system stopped at three stickers and the massaman only had one, which Doug interpreted as manageable. It was not manageable. Every time he ordered it, the first bite was fine and the second was fine and by the fourth his sinuses opened and his eyes watered and a warmth moved through his chest that he attributed to the peppers and not to anything else, and he ate the entire bowl every time and drank three glasses of water and never switched to anything milder, because switching would mean acknowledging that he'd been ordering the wrong thing for fourteen years, and Doug did not acknowledge errors of that duration. He carried them forward.

The restaurant smelled of fish sauce and hot oil and the lemongrass from the tom kha that someone at a nearby table was eating. The kitchen was visible through a narrow window in the back wall, and he could see hands moving, a wok flashing under the hood vent, steam rising. The music was the same instrumental loop it had always been — vaguely Southeast Asian, recorded on what sounded like a Casio keyboard, and it repeated every twelve minutes. He'd timed it once. Kelsey had been sixteen.

She came in at fourteen past noon. Not late — Kelsey wasn't late — but not early either, which for someone who'd driven two hours from Cleveland meant she'd sat in her car in the lot for a few minutes before coming inside. Doug knew this because he did it too. The Bridge approach to difficult encounters: arrive early, sit in the car, rehearse nothing because you don't know what to rehearse, then go in.

She was wearing a denim jacket over a white T-shirt, and her hair was down, which was unusual — at the diner in Worthington it had been pulled back tight, the work configuration, the she-was-there-to-do-something configuration. Down meant something different. Down meant Saturday. Down meant she'd thought about what to wear and decided on something that wasn't armor.

She slid into the booth across from him. She picked up the menu, looked at it for three seconds, and put it down. She had been ordering the pad thai, number fourteen, since she was fourteen, which she'd pointed out once as a coincidence and then corrected herself — she'd started ordering it at fourteen and now she was twenty-eight, so it was half her life, not a coincidence, just persistence.

"The drive was okay," she said, before he asked.

"Good."

"Seventy-one was fine. Construction past Medina but it wasn't bad."

"Medina. Yeah, they've been doing that stretch since —"

"I don't know. A while." She took a sip of the water the hostess had left. The glass had a hairline crack running from the rim to the midpoint, the kind of damage you'd document in a claim but that the restaurant had apparently decided was still within operational tolerance. "How's work?"

"Same. We're doing quarterly reviews next week."

"Exciting."

"It's not exciting. It's the same review I've had for eight years. 'Meets expectations.' Roy gets 'meets expectations' too. He considers it the correct rating."

Kelsey almost smiled. One corner of her mouth moved, then went flat again.

"How's the practice?" Doug asked.

"It's teeth, Dad."

"Right."

The woman with the jade bracelet came to take their order. She wrote nothing down, which Doug found both impressive and faintly unsettling — he could not have processed a single claim without documentation, and the idea that this woman carried the details of multiple orders in her head with no backup suggested a confidence in her own memory that Doug did not share.

Kelsey ordered the pad thai. Doug ordered the massaman curry. The woman nodded and left.

"Tyler says hi," Kelsey said.

"Tell him hi."

"He wanted to come down. I told him this was a dad thing."

"Okay."

She turned the chopstick wrapper over in her hands, creasing it along one edge, then folding it in half, then half again. He watched her hands. The nail on her left ring finger was chipped, a crescent of bare nail showing through the dark red polish, and the wedding ring sat below it — a simple band, white gold, the one Tyler had proposed with. Doug had met the ring at dinner three days after the engagement and had assessed it automatically: white gold, approximately one carat center stone, four-prong setting, retail value probably thirty-eight hundred to forty-five hundred based on the cut grade, which he couldn't determine without magnification. He'd shaken Tyler's hand and said something adequate. He couldn't remember what.

"I read the profile," Kelsey said.

"The thing Alex wrote."

"'America's Most Reluctant Comedian.'" She said it flatly, without inflection, as if it were a claims number. "Roy was right about the headline."

"Roy's usually right about things he reduces to one sentence."

"It was a nice piece. It was wrong, but it was nice."

Doug took a sip of his water. "I know."

"You know it was nice or you know it was wrong?"

"Both."

The pad thai arrived first, which was normal — pad thai was faster than curry. The plate was the same oval ceramic it had always been, with a chip at the edge that Doug had noticed approximately forty visits ago and that had not grown, which meant the chip was stable, not structural, a detail that mattered in claims work and not at all in restaurants but that Doug noticed anyway. A wedge of lime sat on the edge. Kelsey squeezed the lime over the noodles without looking, the muscle memory of twenty-eight orders, and stirred it with her chopsticks.

"I keep thinking about the toast," she said. Then she stopped. She set her chopsticks down. "Actually, that's not true. I keep thinking about something else."

Doug's curry arrived. The waitress set it down and the steam carried the smell of coconut and chili and something sweet underneath, tamarind maybe. He picked up his spoon. He did not eat.

"Something from a long time ago," Kelsey said. "From the divorce."

The word landed on the table between them — *divorce* — next to the soy sauce and the chopstick wrapper she'd folded into a small tight rectangle. Doug felt the air in the booth change. It had the stale, older quality of air released from a room that had been shut too long.

"Okay," he said.

Kelsey picked up the chopstick wrapper and unfolded it. Folded it again. She was looking at the table, not at him, and her jaw was set in the way it got when she was working something loose — the expression she wore, he imagined, when she was scaling below the gumline and the calculus wasn't giving.
