"I was sixteen. You and Mom had been separated for about two months. I was going back and forth. Tuesday and Thursday nights at your place, weekends alternating. The schedule."

"I remember the schedule."

"I know you remember the schedule, Dad." She looked up. Her eyes were direct and dark, and he could see something in them that he'd been seeing since the diner in Worthington — not anger, not the furious precision she'd brought that day. Something underneath the anger, older and quieter. "I'm not asking if you remember the schedule. I'm telling you something."

Doug set his spoon down. "Go ahead."

"One of those Tuesdays. I was at your apartment — the first one, the one on Indianola, before you moved to Clintonville. I was doing homework on your laptop because mine was at Mom's. You were at work, or maybe you'd gone to the store. I don't remember. You weren't there."

The Indianola apartment. Doug had lived there for eight months between moving out of the Briarwood house and finding the Clintonville place. It had been a one-bedroom with carpet that smelled faintly of the previous tenant's dog, and the bathroom had a window that didn't lock, and he'd slept on a mattress on the floor for the first three weeks because the bed frame he'd ordered online had arrived with a cracked side rail and the replacement took nineteen days. He remembered all of this. He documented his own life with the same thoroughness he applied to claims.

"I opened the laptop to do my homework," Kelsey said. "And there was a spreadsheet open. An Excel file."

Doug's hands went still on the table.

"Columns," Kelsey said. Her voice had gone quiet — the Kelsey quiet, the one that was precision rather than volume, the one he'd been hearing since she was old enough to figure out that being loud didn't work on him but being exact did. "Date. Event. Description. You had entries going back months. Before the separation. You were tracking things about Mom."

Doug did not move. The curry was sending steam past his face, and he could feel the heat of it on his cheek, and the Casio music had cycled back to its beginning and was starting the twelve-minute loop again, and the couple at the table across from them was laughing about something — a bright, uncomplicated laugh that belonged to people having a different kind of Saturday.

"March fourteenth, Kelsey pickup thirty-five minutes late from school. March nineteenth, forgot to send permission slip for field trip — had to be faxed from office. May second, schedule conflict with dentist appointment, Megan booked during my Tuesday." Kelsey recited these without looking at anything, from memory. She'd been carrying them for twelve years. "You had maybe forty entries. Each one with a date and a description. Some of them had a column that said 'documented' — yes or no."

The restaurant continued around them. A waiter passed with a tray of spring rolls. The kitchen window showed hands plating something green. The bell on the door rang and someone came in and the woman with the jade bracelet led them to a table, and Doug heard all of it as if through water — present but distorted, arriving through a medium that changed everything.

He remembered the spreadsheet.

He remembered it precisely: the cell formatting, the column widths, the lighter blue he'd used for the header row because Excel's default was too dark and he'd adjusted it to match the Mutual Fidelity formatting guide. None of that was a relevant consideration for a personal spreadsheet tracking his ex-wife's failures as a co-parent, but he'd done it anyway because formatting was formatting and he couldn't not.

"I didn't read all of it," Kelsey said. "I read enough." She pushed her pad thai to the side, the noodles cooling, the lime wedge darkening at the edges. "I was sixteen. I was doing homework. And I found a spreadsheet where my dad was tracking every time my mom screwed up, and every single entry was about me. About picking me up. About forgetting my permission slips. About scheduling around me."

Doug opened his mouth.

"I'm not done." Not loud. Steady. "I didn't tell you. I didn't tell Mom. I closed the laptop and I did my homework on paper and I never mentioned it. Twelve years."

The folded chopstick wrapper was in her hand, crushed now, a small dense rectangle of paper that her fingers kept working as she talked.

"And what I thought — what I thought at sixteen, sitting in that apartment on Indianola with the carpet that smelled like someone else's dog — was that my dad saw me as evidence."

The word arrived in Doug's chest. Not metaphorically — he felt it in his sternum, a pressure that had nothing to do with the curry or the booth or the cracked vinyl under his left thigh. *Evidence.* That was the word. His daughter had been sixteen years old and she'd found a file on his laptop where he'd turned her into data points, into a record of failures that belonged to someone else but were measured in the unit of her — her late pickups, her missed permission slips, her Tuesday nights.

"Not a person," Kelsey said. She was looking at him now, and her eyes were full but she wasn't crying — she was holding it the way she held everything, with the tight grip and the jaw set and the gaze that was its own form of pressure. "A case file. I was a case file."

---

Neither of them ate for a while.

The pad thai sat on Kelsey's side of the table and the massaman curry sat on Doug's, and the steam from the curry had thinned and the coconut smell had faded to something less insistent, and the Casio music played its loop and started over and the restaurant went on being a restaurant on a Saturday in late May, indifferent to what was happening in the booth by the window.

Doug had not spoken. He was aware of the seconds passing, of Kelsey across from him with the ruined chopstick wrapper and her untouched noodles, of the apartment on Indianola coming back with its dog-smell and the window that never locked. He put his fingers on the spoon, then took them off again. Nothing he had sounded usable.

"I remember making it," Doug said.

Kelsey was still. Her hands had stopped working the wrapper. She was watching him the way she'd watched him at the diner — level, silent, waiting.

"It was ugly," he said. "Not just that it existed. The way I made it."

He looked at the thinning steam over the curry and waited a second longer.

"I made columns for things I could use. Date. Incident. Description. Whether I had backup. If there was an email or a voicemail or something from the school, I'd mark documented yes. I liked having yes in the box. I liked how the row stayed put after I typed it."

He looked at his hands on the table. They were the same hands that had typed the spreadsheet, entered the dates, and changed the header-row blue because Excel's default was too dark.

"I could sit there on Indianola and turn a fight into paperwork. I could make your mother wrong in a way that didn't argue back. And you were in every line of it."

Kelsey let that sit. "Yeah," she said. "I know. That's what I saw. Not notes. A file you were ready to use."

"I was keeping score," Doug said. "I wanted a record. I wanted something I could take into a room if it came to that. Court, maybe. Some meeting. I wanted to win something."

The couple across the restaurant laughed again. A plate broke somewhere in the kitchen — a clean, undramatic sound — and someone said something in Thai and someone else responded, and the normal operations of the place continued without regard for what was being said in the booth by the window.

"I was also keeping score because I didn't know how to miss you any other way," he said.

He heard how much cleaner that sounded than the spreadsheet had looked. Kelsey set her glass down and looked at him.

Kelsey was quiet. She was looking at the chopstick wrapper in her hand — mangled, creased beyond use, a small ruin of paper that her fingers had been destroying for twenty minutes. She set it on the table next to her plate. She picked up her glass of water and drank. The hairline crack in the glass caught the overhead light.

"That's not an apology," she said.

"No."

"Okay." She put the glass down. "I'm not sure I wanted one."

"I'm not sure one would help."

"No. Probably not."

The music cycled. A family came in — two parents, two small kids, the smaller one in a booster seat that the hostess produced from behind the counter with the efficiency of someone who'd done this a thousand times. One kid wanted spring rolls. The other wanted Sprite.

Doug picked up his spoon. He took a bite. The curry was warm, not hot — it had been sitting too long — and the spice hit differently when the food wasn't steaming, sharper, more direct, without the cushion of heat. His eyes watered. He didn't wipe them.

"Eat your noodles," he said.

Kelsey looked at him. Then she picked up her chopsticks and ate a bite of pad thai, and the noodles were room temperature by now and the lime had gone flat and neither of them commented on it.

"I told Tyler," she said, after a while. "About the spreadsheet. Last year."

"What did he say?"

"He said that was messed up." She twirled her chopsticks through the noodles without lifting any. "And then he said, 'But at least he was paying attention.' Which is such a Tyler thing to say that I almost threw something at him."

"He's not wrong."

"He's not wrong in a way that's not helpful." She ate another bite. "That's kind of Tyler's whole deal. He's never wrong in ways that aren't helpful."

Doug ate his curry. The second bite was worse — the spice was building, accumulating. He drank water. The water didn't help. It never did.

"How is Tyler?" he asked, and he meant it differently than he usually meant it. He usually meant it as a logistical check — Tyler fine, Kelsey fine, Cleveland fine, everything processed and filed. He meant it now as a real question, and Kelsey heard the difference because she looked up from her noodles and studied him for a moment before she answered.

"He's good," she said. "He's really good. He —" She picked up a noodle with her fingers, not her chopsticks, and ate it. "He made dinner last night. Baked chicken, just salt, set the table, put out napkins. And it was fine, it was totally fine, and I'm sitting there with this plain chicken and I don't know what I'm — I don't know if it's him or if it's —" She stopped. "He sent me three house listings last week. Four-bedrooms. Asked if we should go see them next month. I told him maybe after summer."

She picked up her chopsticks and put them back down. "There's no after-summer plan. I just needed a date that wasn't now."

"Because of the house?" Doug asked.

"I don't know." She pressed her hands flat on the table, fingers spread, the chipped nail and the wedding ring and the slight tension in her wrists that meant she was working something out in real time, not reciting something she'd prepared. "Maybe the house. Maybe him. Maybe me getting weird every time something starts to look stable. He's easy to live with, and sometimes that feels good and sometimes it feels like I'm going numb."

Doug set his spoon down. "Did you tell him any of that?"

"No. I told him work's been bad and June's weird. Work hasn't been bad. June isn't weird. I'm just buying time."

Doug looked at the curry. "Then he thinks it's about houses."

Kelsey let out a breath through her nose. Not a laugh, but close. "Yeah. He thinks it's about houses."

She picked up her water. The hairline crack in the glass split the overhead light. "I need to call him tonight and say something that isn't scheduling. I needed to hear it out loud first."

Doug had no advice that felt usable. He picked up his spoon and ate another bite of curry. Without the steam it hit harder. His nose ran. His eyes watered. He wasn't going to start ordering something milder in year fourteen.

"Do you want dessert?" he said.

"Mango sticky rice," Kelsey said, without pause.

Doug flagged the waitress. One order, two spoons. The waitress wrote nothing down.

The mango sticky rice came on a plate that was too large for it, the mango sliced into a fan and the rice in a mound beside it, glistening with coconut cream and sesame seeds. Kelsey pulled it to the center of the table. They ate. The mango was cold. The rice was still warm in the middle.

Kelsey's spoon clinked against the plate as she reached for the last piece of mango. She got it. Doug let her. He had never fought her for the last piece of anything.

"I should probably head back before five," Kelsey said. "Tyler's making dinner."

"Tyler cooks?"

"He makes this chicken thing. It's fine."

"Fine like the plain chicken?"

Kelsey looked at him. For a second he thought he'd stepped on something tender and made it worse. Then she snorted — an ugly, genuine sound, not a laugh, closer to the noise someone makes when they've been hit with something unexpectedly accurate.

"Yeah," she said. "Exactly. The chicken thing isn't helping."

She put a twenty on the table. Doug reached for his wallet. "I've got it," she said.

"You drove two hours."

"And you listened. That's worth twenty bucks." She slid out of the booth. She stood and pulled on the denim jacket and zipped it halfway — always halfway, as if committing to the full zip would constitute an opinion about the weather she wasn't prepared to hold.

"Thanks for the curry," he said. This was not what he meant, but she would know what he meant, because she always knew what he meant, even when — especially when — he said something else.

"The curry you can't handle and won't stop ordering."

"It's fine."

"It's not fine. Your eyes were watering for twenty minutes."

"That was the curry."

Kelsey looked at him. She didn't argue. She didn't need to.

"I'll call you this week," she said.

"Okay."

"I mean it. I'll actually call."

"Okay."

She walked to the door. The bell rang. She was gone.

Doug sat in the booth long enough to look at the check twice. Thirty-eight dollars. He left Kelsey's twenty and added twenty-two of his own.

He walked to the lot. It was half-full, and Kelsey's Civic was already gone. Through the restaurant window the ceramic cat still had one paw up. The sun was warm on his arms. The trees at the edge of the lot were fully leafed, and the air had settled into late May.

He got in the car and set the key in his lap. What came back was not what she'd said but the file itself: blue header row, narrow columns, DOCUMENTED in caps. He started the car before the picture could settle.

He drove home to Clintonville on habit. Right on High, left on Arcadia, past the library and the coffee shop.

He parked. He went inside. He didn't eat cereal. He sat on the couch in the living room as the afternoon shifted into evening. Simone's pen cap was still on the counter where he'd left it weeks ago. The boxes were still in the second bedroom. The water stain on the ceiling was still shaped like Ohio. He stayed there until the room went dim enough that he had to lean forward to see any of it.


# Chapter 14

The rental car was a gray Hyundai Elantra that smelled like the previous renter's choices: a pine air freshener clipped to the vent, dried coffee in the cupholder, the stale salt-and-grease trace of a fast food meal that had happened somewhere between here and wherever that person had been going. Layla had booked the tour earlier that week in a single phone call while Doug was on his lunch break. Cleveland Thursday, Pittsburgh Friday, Detroit Saturday. "Warm-ups," she called them. "Before the big show." The Netflix taping was in two weeks.

She had emailed him a PDF itinerary with club addresses, hotel confirmations, and a note in all caps that said DO NOT OVERTHINK THIS. Doug had printed it because he did not trust phone batteries. The pages sat on the passenger seat beside a yellow legal pad with his set list divided into blocks: OPENING, INSURANCE, CEREAL, DIVORCE, CLOSER. Under OPENING he had written two possible first lines and circled the one he disliked less. Under CLOSER he had written LAND HERE, as if landing were a matter of instruction.

He pulled onto I-71 north toward Cleveland and did not feel warm.

He had driven this route to visit Kelsey when she first moved there, before the visits became less frequent, then occasional, then something they planned over text three weeks in advance. Delaware. Mansfield. Ashland. He knew where the highway widened and where it narrowed, where the rest stop coffee started tasting less like Columbus and more like trucks. Kelsey lived twenty minutes from the club. He had not told her he was coming. That felt less cruel than telling her and making her decide whether to see him bomb in person.

The Holiday Inn was off an exit lined with chain restaurants that had all decided on different versions of beige. The lobby smelled like lemon cleaner and the coffee by the elevator had been sitting long enough to acquire a skin. In his room the bedspread was maroon and the art over the desk showed a sailboat on a lake nobody in Ohio had ever seen. Doug set the legal pad on the desk, took off his glasses, put them back on, and said his opener to the mirror.

"Good to be here in Cleveland. My hotel is technically in Independence, which feels like a strong word for a place behind an Olive Garden."

In the mirror he saw a man delivering geography with hope.

He tried the second opener.

"I work in insurance, which means when your basement floods I'm the guy who comes over and says, yes, that's definitely water."

He had written PAUSE after that line. He held the pause for the hotel mirror. The mirror did what mirrors do.

He showered, changed into jeans and an untucked button-down, and drove to Wiley's with the legal pad on the passenger seat as if it were a person whose opinion mattered. The club sat in a strip mall between a Great Clips and a vape shop. The parking lot had fresh pothole patches the color of healing bruises. Inside, the room smelled like fryer oil, beer taps, and carpet that had absorbed a decade of apologies. A TV over the bar showed the Guardians game with the sound off. Doug stood near the back wall while the MC did ten minutes about dating apps and one guy in a Browns hoodie laughed so hard he slapped his own thigh like it had betrayed him.

When the MC brought him up, a small wave of recognition moved through the room before he even reached the microphone. Not recognition of him exactly. Recognition of the category. Viral man. Toast man. The person from the phone.

He smiled because he had practiced smiling. "Good to be here in Cleveland," he said. "My hotel is technically in Independence, which feels like a strong word for a place behind an Olive Garden."

Nothing happened for a second. Then the bartender smiled politely at the register, which was the kind of reaction a person had to hearing the address on a package read out loud.

Doug nodded as if that had counted. "I work in insurance, which means when your basement floods I'm the guy who comes over and says, yes, that's definitely water."

A woman in the second row took a sip of vodka soda. A man near the aisle looked toward the bar menu as if checking whether something better might be printed there.

"We use a phrase called actual cash value," Doug said. "That's when the company looks at your ruined couch and says, after depreciation, your sadness is worth about twenty-four dollars."

He waited. He had seen comedians wait after a line like that, smiling a little as if the room were catching up. The room did not look like it was catching up. It looked like it had received information.

"Anybody here own a home?" he said. "Rent? Anybody successfully avoiding both?"

No one answered. A couple at a high-top looked at each other, the way married people do when they are silently negotiating who has to participate in public. Doug had planned a follow-up if someone said homeowners. He had planned a different follow-up if someone said renters. He had planned a third if someone yelled neither. Planning turned out not to help.

"Tough room," he said, because he had heard comedians say that too. "That's okay. Denial is actually most of my job."

A short laugh came from somewhere near the kitchen, not enough to locate. Doug moved on too quickly, which made the quickness visible. He went into the cereal material he and Simone had been shaping and somehow made it sound like an onboarding seminar.

"I live alone, so dinner and breakfast have started sharing infrastructure," he said. "If a bowl's involved, I stop asking which meal it is."

A woman near the back nodded once in a way that could have been agreement, sympathy, or neck pain.

"Cheerios is an honest cereal. It isn't promising excitement. It's saying, here's a beige circle, let's all be adults."

That line had sounded passable in the hotel room. Onstage it sounded like an accountant trying to open for a dentist.

He tried crowd work with a man in a Cavaliers hat because the man had been making attentive eye contact and Doug mistook attention for an invitation.

"You eat cereal for dinner?" Doug asked.

The man blinked. "Sometimes."

"What kind?"

"Whatever's there."

Doug had no idea what to do with whatever's there. He said, "Right," into the microphone and felt the room watch him fail to convert a stranger's pantry into an event.

He moved to the divorce material early because that was what performers did when one section died. They pivoted. They adjusted. He had written a transition on the legal pad and now heard himself use it exactly as written.

"Speaking of long-term disappointment," he said, "I got divorced twelve years ago, which is enough time for a person to be fine and also still have opinions about towels."

This got less than nothing. A few people shifted in their seats in the specific way people shift when they suspect the topic itself might become work.

Doug could feel himself imitating several men at once, none of whom were helping him. He widened his stance because he had seen comics do that. He took a sip of water because he had seen comics do that. He tried a callback to the Olive Garden line because he had written CALLBACK? in the margin and had mistaken the note for destiny.

"At least the breadsticks there have replacement cost," he said.

A glass set down on a table in front. Somebody near the back said "Hmm" with the neutral tone of a person testing soup.

Eighteen minutes in, a woman near the back yelled, "Do the toast!"

Doug said, "I can't. I'm sober."

That got him the only real laugh of the night. It came fast and clean and spread farther than anything else had. He felt it hit the walls. He felt it leave. Then he made the mistake of trying to stand on it.

"Right," he said. "Anyway, actual cash value."

The room fell away from him again so quickly it almost had dignity. He did nine more minutes because ending early would have required a different kind of nerve. He thanked them. They applauded with the mercy reserved for public failure and little children performing instruments.

Outside, the air smelled like damp pavement and fryer grease pushed out the back door. Doug stood beside the Elantra and called Simone before he could decide not to.

"How bad?" she said.

"I asked if anybody had read a homeowners policy."

"Why."

"I thought that was audience interaction."

"Did anybody answer?"

"No."

"Why would they?"

Doug looked at the club's side door, where a cook in a black apron was smoking under a yellow security light. "I opened with a line about my hotel technically being in Independence."

Silence.

"Simone."

"I'm here."

"I tried to do it like a comedian."

"Don't," she said.

He laughed once through his nose, not because anything was funny. "That feels like advice I could've used earlier."

"You'll need it again. Get some sleep."

He drove back to the Holiday Inn with the radio off. In the room the maroon bedspread had the pattern hotels used when they expected it to survive everything. He sat on top of it in his jeans and dress shoes and opened his phone. If he texted Kelsey, she could answer or not answer, and either result would sit there in writing. He typed, In Cleveland for work. He deleted for work because it was not work. He typed, Twenty minutes away. He deleted that too because it sounded like a threat or an apology. Eventually he turned the phone face down on the nightstand beside the Gideon Bible and did not call her.

At six the next morning he went down to the breakfast room because staying in the room felt too much like agreeing with it. Two youth hockey families had taken over the waffle maker. A man in a polo from an HVAC convention was reading USA Today with his mouth slightly open. Doug poured cereal from one of those miniature boxes that held one serving if you were pretending not to be hungry and two if you were telling the truth. He carried it to a table by the window and watched the parking lot while he rewrote his opener in block letters on a fresh page.

GOOD EVENING PITTSBURGH.

He stared at it until the cereal went soft. Then he crossed out GOOD.

---

The drive to Pittsburgh was two and a half hours through northeastern Ohio and across the western edge of Pennsylvania, where the flat farmland wrinkled into hills and the highway started asking for more attention. Doug stopped for gas at a Sheetz outside Youngstown. The coffee cost a dollar eighty-nine and tasted how truck stops smelled: burnt, industrial, somehow fine if you did not examine it too closely. He stood by the condiment station stirring powdered creamer into it and listened to a woman in scrubs tell a man in a Steelers hoodie that if he bought one more breakfast sandwich she was leaving him there. The man bought the sandwich.

Back in the Elantra he put the lid on the coffee, took one sip, and said his new opener to the windshield.

"Good evening, Pittsburgh."

That was not an opener. That was a fact with manners.

He tried the insurance material again, trimming words as he drove.

"I work in insurance, and one thing about insurance is we use optimistic language for depressing situations."

He stopped there because his exit was coming and because the sentence sounded like the start of a keynote.

Pittsburgh was more vertical than Cleveland, which was more vertical than Columbus. His hotel was another Holiday Inn near the airport. It had the same floor plan as Cleveland, but with a blue bedspread and a water stain on the ceiling that looked like it had almost made up its mind. The room smelled faintly of pool chemicals from an indoor pool he could not see. Doug lay on the bed for fifteen minutes with his forearm over his eyes and listened to the air conditioner knock itself on and off. At four-thirty he sat up, pulled the legal pad into his lap, and wrote pool? in the margin beside INSURANCE.

The question mark irritated him. The pool story was not a maybe. It was a file from March, complete with photos, receipts, and one handwritten note from a woman who believed seventeen rosebushes represented both horticulture and moral worth. The problem was that it was not a joke. Simone laughed when he told claim stories because she laughed at reality's paperwork, but a club audience expected a person with a microphone to know where he was taking them.

He kept replaying the Cleveland laugh. I can't. I'm sober. Simone had said it came because he told the truth. Doug believed her and still had no idea how to use that without announcing in advance that now he was about to tell the truth.

The Comic Attic sat beneath a bar called O'Reilly's, down a narrow staircase that smelled like spilled Yuengling and industrial cleaner. Maybe seventy-five people fit down there. The ceiling was low enough that the laugh from the room before his seemed trapped in it. The MC, a young woman named Diane with a sleeve tattoo and a laugh that carried through walls, introduced him as "the man who broke the internet at his daughter's wedding, Doug Bridge."

The applause was genuine. Doug took the mic, looked at the room, opened his mouth, and nothing came. One second passed, then three, then five. The audience shifted. At seven seconds a man in the front row uncrossed his arms. At nine, a woman by the wall looked at the person beside her. At eleven, someone coughed.

"I wrote it down," Doug said. "I said it in the car. In the hotel. In a Holiday Inn bathroom. It was there an hour ago."

A few people laughed out of pity, but pity made sound too, and Doug took it. He started with the first line he could reach.

"I work in insurance," he said, "and one thing about insurance is we use optimistic words for depressing situations. Restoration. Betterment. Replacement cost. Nobody in my field has ever walked into a burned kitchen and thought, this could be a nice opportunity."

A man in the second row smiled with only the upper half of his face. It was not nothing, but it was not momentum either.

"Actual cash value," Doug said. "That's when the company looks at your destroyed couch and says, we need to remember the wear and tear. Which feels rude on the couch question and worse on the human level."

He had intended to move from there into divorce, then cereal, then back to insurance, but the sections had come loose from each other somewhere between Cleveland and the Fort Pitt Tunnel. He heard the gap before his next line and tried to cross it anyway.

"Anyway," he said, "I've been thinking about cereal."

This got a real laugh, but it was the laugh people gave when the person onstage had exposed himself by accident. Doug felt relief and shame arrive together and mistook both for encouragement.

"I eat a lot of Cheerios now," he said. "Not Honey Nut. Plain. Plain says you're participating in breakfast for structural reasons."

One woman laughed. Another took a sip of beer and looked down into the glass as if checking whether he might be in there.

"You can tell if a cereal is for adults by whether the box looks disappointed in you."

That line had killed at no stage other than his hotel chair. Down here it landed like a note left on a windshield.

Doug lost the next transition entirely. He started a sentence about the grocery store, abandoned it, said "Right" into the mic, and saw Diane by the back wall tilt her head the way people do when they want to help but have accepted that they cannot. Twelve minutes in, with the cards in his head no longer in any order he could trust, he told the pool story instead.

"I had a claim in Westerville," he said. "Above-ground pool. Side wall split at two in the morning. Nine thousand gallons of water went sideways through three yards and took out the neighbor's rose garden."

That got attention. Doug kept going before he could remember to be funny.

"Seventeen hybrid tea roses," he said. "The neighbor had receipts. Separate receipts. I know that because she put them in a Ziploc bag and handed it to me like evidence in a murder case."

The room laughed.

"Forty-two hundred dollars in roses. I remember the number because no one should ever have to say forty-two hundred dollars in roses more than once, but my job required repetition."

More of them now. Chairs stopped squeaking. Someone at the bar turned fully toward him.

"The policy covered the pool owner's fence, the patio furniture, and part of the landscaping, but not the roses at replacement value because there was a sublimit on ornamental plantings that made everybody furious for different reasons."

He said the word sublimit and the room laughed harder, which told him they were with him in a place he had not expected anyone to go.

"I had two adults standing in wet mulch at eight-thirty in the morning arguing over whether a rose counts as a shrub. I had to ask follow-up questions."

For about ninety seconds Doug felt the room come toward him. Not because he had built a joke. Because he had stopped building.

Then he felt the legal pad in his head again, the neat labeled tabs. He reached for the bit Simone had helped him shape, and the reaching was enough.

"So that's the thing about replacement cost," he said. "The form says we'll return you to pre-loss condition. Which sounds comforting until you've met adults."

Nothing. Not even courtesy.

He kept going because the line was on the page and therefore, in some humiliating administrative sense, active.

"There isn't really a pre-loss condition for a person," he said. "There's just the current version with documentation."

Glasses touched tabletops. A waitress squeezed behind the back row and the room's attention went with her for a moment and did not return in full.

Doug tried to pull them back with the divorce tag he had written in the hotel.

"My divorce paperwork had numbers all over it," he said. "None of them offered replacement cost."

A man near the wall took out his phone and looked at it without lowering the brightness. Whatever had moved toward Doug during the pool story went back where it had been. He finished to applause that was better than Cleveland and still wrong.

He called Simone from the hotel parking lot before he went upstairs. A plane went over low enough to roughen the air.

"I stood there for eleven seconds," he said.

"That's a long time."

"The pool story worked."

"How long?"

"Ninety seconds."

"And then?"

"Then I said, 'So that's the thing about replacement cost.'"

Simone was quiet for a beat. "Yeah."

"That 'yeah' sounds bad."

"It's not good."

"They liked the roses."

"People like a thing that happened."

Doug leaned against the car door. "What were you thinking about during the pool story?"

"I'm asking you," she said.

"Nothing. I wasn't, I don't know. I was just talking."

"Okay," she said. "Call me after Detroit."

He bought a Diet Coke from the vending machine because the machine did not require interpretation. In the room he took off one shoe, then the other, and sat on the blue bedspread listening to the freeway through the window unit. He thought about going over the set again. He thought about not. On the legal pad he drew a line through the replacement-cost paragraph so hard the next two pages dented under it.

In the morning the breakfast room had the same eggs as Cleveland in a different hotel pan. Doug ate one piece of toast and drank bad coffee from a paper cup with a plastic lid that did not quite fit. At the next table a family in matching Detroit Tigers shirts argued about whether they needed to leave early for the Henry Ford Museum. Doug looked at the shirts and thought about spending the evening inside a room full of people from Michigan who might want the man from the video and might get a man talking about ornamental plantings instead.

He folded the legal pad closed and went to the car.

---

The drive from Pittsburgh to Detroit was the longest leg, four and a half hours west on the turnpike and across northern Ohio to Toledo, where I-75 ran the last stretch north into Detroit. Doug drove it Saturday morning. The sun hit the windshield at an angle the visor could not fix. He stopped for gas outside Akron. The coffee tasted the same as the Youngstown coffee. He bought a gas station sandwich, unwrapped it, looked at it, and rewrapped it. He ate it twenty miles later because hunger was a simpler problem.

Outside Toledo he pulled into a rest area just to stand still for a minute. The building had vending machines, a rack of Ohio brochures, and a men's room that smelled like bleach and old paper towels. Doug stood at the sink washing hands that were not dirty and looked at himself in the mirror over the row of basins. Same button-down. Same face from Pittsburgh. Less available conviction. He said, "Good evening, Detroit," to the mirror and hated the civility of it.

Back on the highway Layla called at noon.

"So how's it going? I heard Cleveland was a little, you know. Finding your legs."

"It was bad, Layla."

"It wasn't bad. Gerry said you had good energy."

"Gerry lies to comedians after they bomb. That's his whole job."

"Okay, so Cleveland was a learning experience. What about Pittsburgh?"

"Ninety seconds worked."

"That's great. Ninety seconds is a real start. We can build on ninety seconds. We can literally market ninety seconds if we have to. We could clip ninety seconds."

Doug looked at the highway, then at a truck hauling drywall sheets strapped in blue nylon. "I need to focus on tonight."

"Totally. Detroit's gonna love you. The venue's great, babe. It's a real room. This is exactly what you need."

"I don't know what I need."

"You need reps," Layla said, moving so fast the phrase almost counted as certainty. "You need stage time and muscle memory and then the taping's gonna feel normal. Or not normal, but handleable. That's a word now. Handleable."

Doug said nothing.

"Hey," she said, quieter for one beat than she usually allowed herself to be. "Don't spiral in the car, okay? That's dead time. Use the drive."

He almost said, It is all drive. Instead he said, "Okay."

The Mark was a real comedy club, not a strip mall or a bar basement but a dedicated room with proper lighting, a real sound system, and tiered seating that climbed toward the back. It held about a hundred and fifty, and it was close to full. The lobby walls had signed headshots in black frames and a drink minimum printed in cheerful font. Doug checked into the attached hotel first. His room was on the fifth floor. The bedspread was green. Somebody had placed the TV remote in a paper sleeve that said SANITIZED FOR YOUR COMFORT, which Doug found less comforting than the alternative.

He put his garment bag on the luggage rack and tried the first three minutes of material one more time, sitting on the bed because standing had not helped in Cleveland. He did not rehearse the heckler scenario because that would have required admitting it was likely. He did not rehearse the toast line because it belonged to a version of himself he had not chosen to manufacture sober.

Downstairs, the showroom had the temperature of money spent correctly. A server in black walked by carrying six cocktails at once without looking at any of them. The MC, a local comic named Terrence, did five minutes on Detroit traffic that got the kind of laughs Doug had started to think of as physical evidence. Doug watched from behind the curtain and felt how separate Terrence's ease was from anything Doug knew how to do. Terrence looked like a person with a microphone. Doug looked like a man waiting to contest an appraisal.

When Terrence brought him up, the applause started large and stayed large long enough that Doug understood people were not cautiously curious anymore. They were arriving with an expectation. A recognizable person from a recognizable failure. A hundred and fifty people who wanted either the toast or a version of the toast refined into product.

Doug walked onstage. The lights were warm enough to turn the back half of the room into shapes. He took a breath and started with the material he and Simone had built.

"I work in property claims," he said. "We have actual cash value and replacement cost. Actual cash value is what the insurer thinks your couch is worth after six years, one dog, and whatever you did to it during football season."

A few people smiled. One man near the front laughed alone and then stopped when no one joined him.

"Replacement cost is the number they tell you before paperwork begins," Doug said. "It's the optimistic number. The brochure number."

That got a couple of courtesy sounds. The room was not dead. It was evaluating.

"I live alone, so dinner and breakfast have started sharing infrastructure," he said. "If a bowl's involved, I stop asking which meal it is."

More polite smiles. A woman in the third row took a sip of wine and looked at him with the encouraging expression people use on first-time presenters in conference rooms.

"Cheerios is an honest cereal," Doug said. "It isn't promising a good time. It's saying, here's a beige circle. Let's all conduct ourselves appropriately."

Three minutes in, a man in the second row lifted both beers and yelled, "Toast!"

He was maybe forty-five, heavy, wearing a Tigers cap and a polo shirt tucked into jeans. He was grinning already. Doug could see he was not angry. He was having the best time in the room, which was the problem.

"Toast! Toast! Toast!"

Doug looked at him and thought about every YouTube clip of a comedian destroying a heckler. "Hey, man," he said. "I appreciate the enthusiasm."

It was wrong. He heard it as soon as it left his mouth. It was supervisor language, claims-department language. The guy brightened, as if Doug had opened a door for him.

"Come on, man. Do the toast!"

"I don't do the toast," Doug said. "That's not, I've got different material tonight."

"We don't want different material!"

"Sir," Doug said, and then knew he had made it worse.

"Sir? He called me sir." The man turned to the woman beside him, who had already started looking embarrassed on his behalf and everyone else's. "DO THE TOAST, DOUG."

Half the crowd looked embarrassed for him. The other half wanted to see how bad it could get. Doug could feel the set list leave him. It was not the whole stage or his voice, just the labeled tabs he had planned to move through in order. He stood there with a hundred and forty people looking at him and a drunk man demanding a wedding toast and nothing in his head but the last two days of interstate.

"Okay," Doug said. "Here's what I actually have. I drove here from Pittsburgh this morning and the coffee at a gas station outside Akron tasted exactly like the coffee I had outside Youngstown yesterday. I think it's the same coffee. I don't know how that would work logistically, but it tasted the same."

A woman near the bar laughed into her beer. The man's date stopped tugging on his sleeve.

"I've been in three Holiday Inns in three nights. Cleveland was maroon. Pittsburgh was blue. Detroit is green. I think it's green. It might be teal, I don't — anyway, somebody in an office decided those are three different experiences."

That got more of them. The heckler lowered one of his beers.

"Every room has that chair by the window. The one nobody sits in. I sat in it in Pittsburgh and you can see the parking lot and part of a Denny's sign, so I don't know what the chair is for."

The laugh widened. Terrence, by the bar, uncrossed his arms.

"And rest stops. I stopped at — I think four rest stops in two days, which is a lot. Everybody in there is holding food they didn't want an hour earlier and looking at the highway like it owes them an apology."

Doug heard the room with him now and kept going because stopping would have meant thinking.

"I-80 through Pennsylvania is actually beautiful. I wasn't expecting that. It seems unfair, because you're just paying tolls to drive through these hills and trees and nobody's stopping."

Someone said, "Jesus," softly, and laughed right after.

"And every Holiday Inn smells the same. Cleaner and carpet and something. Other people's sleep, maybe. I don't know what that smells like, but it's in there. It's supposed to be comforting but really it just means you could be in any city and nothing about the room would tell you which one."

The heckler went quiet. His date put her phone down. What Doug got from them then was not laughter exactly. It was the attention people give something true.

"That chair's still by the window when you get back. The parking lot's still there. Cleveland or Pittsburgh or Detroit, after midnight it's the same room. Ice bucket. Gideon Bible. Key card that stops working at some point, and they never tell you when."

He talked that way for eight minutes, about toll roads and hotel carpets and how every fluorescent hallway made him feel temporarily misfiled, about waking up in a room that already had the air conditioner on as if someone had guessed the shape of his comfort and missed it by half an inch, about the breakfast bars where the cereal came out of wall dispensers with little chrome handles like a ration system for disappointment. He said he trusted road signs more than motivational language because a road sign would at least admit how many miles were left. He said all hotel art looked like it had lost a lawsuit. Each line came out before he had time to build a better version of it, and that was why the room stayed with him.

Then he felt himself reach for the cereal bit waiting on the set list, and the reaching killed it.

"Anyway," he said. "The other thing I've been thinking about this week is depreciation."

The temperature in the room changed. Not dramatically. Enough.

"Depreciation is a document's way of saying time happened to your recliner and now nobody wants to pay for it."

Ice touched glass. A server stopped beside a table to collect empties and nobody minded the interruption.

"My couch has an actual cash value of maybe forty dollars," Doug said. "Which feels rude, because I'm still using the couch."

A woman who had been leaning forward settled back. The heckler picked up one of his beers again, not to yell now, just to drink.

"Cereal's like that too," Doug said, trying to get back to the bit exactly as written. "The photo on the box is replacement cost. The bowl in your apartment is actual cash value."

Nothing. That was the problem. Not boos. Not offense. The little intimate electricity of the last eight minutes dissolved into a normal club room where a man was talking into a microphone and the audience was deciding whether to stay with him out of courtesy. The last four minutes were polite again. When he said, "Thank you, that's my time," the applause was generous and confused.

Backstage, Terrence held out a fist the way comics did. Doug looked at it for half a second too long before understanding and touching it with his own knuckles.

"Long drive?" Terrence said.

"Three cities."

"Shit." Terrence nodded as if that answered several questions at once. "Get home safe."

That was all. No diagnosis. No consolation. Doug appreciated him for it.

---

He left Detroit at ten-forty, still in his stage clothes, the parking lot damp from a rain that had come and gone while he was inside. The Elantra's GPS said three hours and twenty-two minutes to Columbus. By the time he reached the highway, the wipers were dry-clicking across glass that no longer needed them. He turned them off and drove through the outer ring of Detroit with neon reflected in leftover water on the pavement.

Thirty miles outside the city, on I-75 south, he called Simone.

"How bad?" she said.

"A guy spent half the set yelling for the toast."

"And?"

"I called him sir."

Simone made a sound through her nose that might have been a laugh. "Jesus."

"Then I talked about hotel coffee and parking lots and they listened."

"How long?"

Doug passed a sign for Findlay and watched his headlights catch the metal bolts on the guardrail. "Eight minutes. Maybe a little more. Then I went back to depreciation and lost them."

The line went quiet except for highway noise under it.

"What we built doesn't work," Doug said.

"I know."

"I can't hand Layla eight minutes about rest stops and hotel carpets and call it a set. I wrote versions of that in Cleveland, in Pittsburgh, in the car. It never sounded like that onstage."

Doug drove past a darkened billboard and an exit ramp lit by one orange lamp. "I can hear the point where it changes. I just can't stop before it changes."

Simone did not answer right away. When she spoke, her voice was flatter than comfort and kinder for that reason. "The taping's in two weeks."

He waited for the part where she would tell him what to do next. She did not offer it. The silence asked more of him than advice would have.

"I don't know how to do that on purpose," he said.

"I know."

"That's not helpful."

"Helpful isn't available right now."

He almost laughed. He almost got angry. What came instead was fatigue, complete and procedural. "Okay."

"Get home safe," she said. "Tuesday we'll look at what was alive."

He drove the rest of the way with the radio off. At a rest area south of Toledo he bought a bottle of water and a pack of peanut butter crackers from a vending machine because the machine asked only for money. In the men's room he looked at himself under fluorescent light and saw stage makeup he had not applied, just the shine from a room that wanted more from him than he had. Back in the car he tried saying some of the road material out loud to see whether it had shape away from people.

"Every room has that chair by the window."

In the empty car it sounded like a sentence from a man talking to himself at night, which it was. He tried the gas station coffee line. He tried the line about the breakfast dispensers. Without the room, he could hear the sadness more clearly than the comedy, and because he could hear it, he started arranging it again, tidying it, placing it back into labeled sections. By the time he reached Delaware it already sounded worse.

He got to Clintonville a little after two. The apartment was dark. He let himself in, set his keys beside Simone's pen cap on the kitchen counter, and stood still until the refrigerator motor kicked on. There was milk in the fridge, half a block of cheddar wrapped in wax paper, mustard, two eggs, and nothing that counted as a plan. He closed the door and looked at the yellow Cheerios box on top of the refrigerator without opening it.

Tuesday was on the calendar. In two weeks he would stand at the Lincoln Theatre in front of 580 people and cameras. Tonight had proved something that felt both useful and impossible. The only part of him audiences wanted was the part he could not prepare without killing. He stood in the kitchen with one hand on the counter and listened to the apartment not containing anybody else. Then he turned off the kitchen light and left the cereal where it was.


# Chapter 15

On the last Wednesday in May, Simone ordered a bourbon neat while Doug counted fourteen beers on the chalkboard behind the counter. He picked one of the fourteen that sounded like a beer and not a statement, and the bartender, who had dark red nails and a silver ring on her thumb, poured it from a tap with a wooden handle. The bar had high ceilings and the kind of exposed brick that meant it had been something else first.

Nine dollars. Doug did the math on what that would be annually if he came here twice a week, which was a thing he did with any purchase over six dollars. It was not a useful calculation. It was just what his brain did.

They sat at a high-top near the windows on tall stools that didn't have backs, which meant Doug had to decide what to do with his posture. He chose upright. Simone settled onto hers as if she'd already assessed the structural integrity and found it adequate.

She didn't have her notebook.

Doug had been watching Simone write in that notebook for almost three months. She wrote in pencil, quickly, in handwriting that tilted so far left the words looked as if they were bracing against the margin. She wrote things Doug said when he didn't know he was saying anything worth writing. She underlined things twice. She crossed things out with single precise lines. The notebook had been on his kitchen table at every session, and now it wasn't here, and the bare stretch of table between them made the dinner feel different before either of them had said a word.

The light through the tall windows was long and warm and the sidewalk outside was full of people who didn't seem to be going anywhere in particular, which Doug supposed was what people did in the Short North on a warm evening. He'd been to this neighborhood four times in his life, counting tonight. Two of those times were just driving through it to somewhere else.

"You look like someone who's never been to a bar," Simone said.

"I've been to bars."

"You look like you're appraising the property."

Doug looked at the windows, the tin ceiling, the long counter with its row of stools. "The ductwork's been painted but not insulated," he said. "You'd get condensation issues in winter."

Simone drank her bourbon. "That's exactly what I mean."

They ordered food. Simone got a salad with something on it Doug couldn't identify from the menu description — it said "shaved fennel" and he took this on faith. Doug got a burger. The waiter was a tall kid, early twenties, with the careful posture of someone who'd recently been told to stand up straighter, and he said "awesome" when Doug ordered, which was not a word Doug associated with a hamburger.

"How's your week been?" Simone said. It came out flat, more statement than question, what conversation sounded like after enough time together had worn the formal structures smooth.

"Fine," Doug said. "Processed a hail claim. A woman in Reynoldsburg — her roof's twenty-three years old and she wants full replacement cost. The policy caps it at actual cash value for roofs over twenty."

"How does that work?"

Doug explained. He explained the depreciation schedule, the inspection requirements, the specific way you calculated the remaining useful life of a twenty-three-year-old asphalt shingle roof. Simone listened with the expression she wore when she was listening professionally, except this wasn't professional, which made Doug aware that he was talking about roof depreciation on what was either a dinner between colleagues or a dinner between two people who no longer had a reason to be colleagues.

He stopped. "Sorry."

"Don't apologize. That's the first interesting thing anyone's said to me today."

"You need to meet more people."

"I've met plenty of people. Most of them are funnier than you on purpose and less interesting."

Their food arrived. The burger was fine. Simone's salad had the fennel on it, which looked like translucent strips of something that didn't want to be eaten. She ate it with the steady, unbothered pace of a person who ate salads at bars and didn't consider this remarkable.

For a while they didn't talk. The bar was filling in around them — a midweek crowd, which in the Short North meant young people in clothes that appeared to have been chosen with intention, and a few older couples who had the comfortable silence of people who'd been married long enough that dinner was a proximity arrangement, not an event. Doug could hear the music now, something with a guitar and a woman's voice that was probably famous and that he would never be able to identify.

"I keep thinking about Detroit," Doug said.

Simone looked up from her salad. "Don't turn it into a postmortem."

"I'm not." He watched the foam ring slide down inside his glass. "I'm saying eight accidental minutes isn't an hour."

"No," Simone said. "It isn't."

She swirled the bourbon in her glass and watched the liquid settle. Doug watched her. For almost three months he'd been noticing small things: how she held a pencil, how her whole body tipped slightly forward when she listened, how she used her ring finger to push her hair behind her ear. At some point the noticing had shifted from professional attention to something else. He didn't know when. It had happened one Tuesday at a time, until he was carrying around useless information about her body: which finger moved her hair, how long she held a glass near her mouth before she drank.

"Can I ask you something?" Doug said.

"Sure."

"Why'd you stop performing?"

Simone set her glass down. She didn't answer right away, which was unusual. Simone answered things quickly, a habit from two decades in writers' rooms where silence meant you agreed with whatever had just been said and your joke was going in the trash.

"I didn't know you knew I performed," she said.

"Layla mentioned it. When she was telling me about you. She said you'd done the circuit."

"Layla said that?"

"Those were her words."

Simone picked up her bourbon again but didn't drink it. She held it near her chin, and Doug could smell the whiskey from across the table, sweet and sharp, and he thought about how his own relationship with alcohol had become a negotiation since the toast — one beer, always one, always counted, always the awareness of what happened the last time he'd stopped counting.

"I did stand-up for eleven years," Simone said. "Improv for three of those. Some sketch. Chicago first, then New York."

"Were you good?"

"Technically, yeah. I could write a set. I could work a room. I understood structure — where to put the turn, how to build a callback, how to use silence. All the craft stuff."

"But."

"I was miserable." She pushed a piece of fennel to the edge of her plate with her fork. "And I don't mean nervous. Nervous is fine. Nervous burns off. I mean there was a room over a sports bar in Chicago where the curtain always smelled like fryer oil, and the stage lights were so hot I'd feel sweat start under my bra before I got to the second joke, and I still knew exactly where the laugh was coming. There was a landlord bit that hit every Thursday. Same inhale. Same guy in front slapping the table on the tag. By the end I could feel the laugh arrive in my body before I opened my mouth, and that should've been useful information, but mostly it made me want to peel my skin off."

Doug said nothing. He sat motionless on his stool, the way he did when somebody stopped circling and said the thing they had meant from the start.

"The laughs were the worst part," Simone said. "Because they were real. I'd come offstage with my thumb sticky from the tape on the mic and go sit in the bathroom until enough people had left that I didn't have to hear good set on the way out. One night I got in my car and drove up Lake Shore for forty minutes because if I'd gone straight home I would've had to admit I was choosing that on purpose. After a while I started cancelling spots. Then I started taking writing jobs. Then I stopped calling people back. Writing for other people felt cleaner. I could hear what was funny about someone else and put it in their mouth, and if it worked it was theirs. Not mine. I didn't have to stand there and own it." She said this looking at her glass, not at Doug. There was a small crease between her eyebrows that he'd seen before — she got it when she was concentrating on a note she was writing — but he'd never seen it aimed at her own speech. She was concentrating on being honest, and the effort was visible.

"How long have you been writing?"

"Twenty years, give or take. Three late-night shows. A pilot that never aired. A bunch of corporate stuff I'd rather not get into. Two podcasts." She paused. "Your material."

"My material doesn't work."

"It doesn't, no." She set her fork down. "Your voice doesn't go onto a page. I haven't figured out why."

They sat with that for a moment. Outside, the light was finally softening, the sun dropping behind the buildings on the west side of High Street. The bar had gotten louder — someone had turned the music up or the people had gotten drunker or both. The bartender's silver ring tapped against a glass as she poured.

"I can build it for somebody else," Simone said. "You mutter one true thing and a room goes with you. I'm still a little sore about that."

Doug thought about this. He pictured Simone in her twenties in a club in Chicago, saying lines she knew would work while some part of her stood off to the side and waited for it to end. Then he pictured himself in Pittsburgh after the pool story, the microphone heavy in his hand, the room going flat in front of him. The two failures were different, but they felt bad in the same place.

"Probably the same amount of worse," he said.

Simone laughed. It was short, more breath than voice, and she covered her mouth with her hand and then took her hand away.

"What?" Doug said.

She shook her head. "Nothing."

---

The burger was gone and Simone was on her second bourbon and Doug was still on his one beer, now warm, which he drank anyway because leaving a beer unfinished was wasteful in a way he couldn't articulate but felt strongly about.

Simone was talking about a comedian she'd written for years ago, a woman with timing so exact Simone could feel the laugh coming before the line was finished. "Third joke, first tag, little pause, then the word," she said. "Every night. Same place. Same laugh." She tore a piece of bread from the basket that had come with Doug's burger and that neither of them had touched until now. "Then the lights would come up and everybody'd put on their coats and leave exactly the same as they arrived. Dead."

"Dead how?"

"Like the room knew its part," Simone said. She rolled the bread between her fingers. "Laugh there. Then there. Everybody safe."

"Safe from what?"

"From saying what the laugh was actually about." She looked at him over the rim of her glass. "A real laugh isn't approval. It's what comes out when your body knows something before your manners do."

"At the wedding nobody knew where it was going," Doug said.

"No."

"Brendan did his speech. Everybody cried in the right spots. Then I got up there and started talking about the centerpieces and Megan's mother and that table and whatever else came out." He could feel the sequence as he said it, the heat in his face from that night, the looseness in his mouth. "I wasn't steering it. I was already saying it before I'd decided to say it."

"Right," Simone said. "You got there before the room could start behaving again."

"So they laughed because I was rude."

"Partly. Mostly they laughed because they knew. About the mother. About the table. About marriage, probably. You said the thing they were all busy not saying."

Doug rubbed the wet ring his beer had left on the table with his thumb. "When I do a set, they can hear me coming," he said. "Even if the joke's decent. They know I'm about to ask for something."

Simone nodded once.

"Yeah. The toast didn't ask. It showed up drunk and said it anyway."

Doug looked down at the table. The wet ring from his glass had already started drying at the edges. "So performance is the warning label."

"Something like that."

He waited for the part where she'd tell him what to do instead. She picked at the bread.

"You don't have a fix," he said.

"No."

"That's what my mother would do," Doug said. "Just stare at me. Then ask if I was eating enough."

Simone laughed, low and from the chest, and covered her mouth with her hand before letting it drop.

"Your mother," Simone said, and shook her head.

"She said I'd been polite for forty years and that was the first time I sounded like myself. She was eating a dinner roll when she said it."

"That's a good detail. The dinner roll."

"It's not a detail. She was eating a dinner roll."

"I know. That's why it's good."

They sat there. The bar was loud now, the midweek crowd settled in, and Doug could hear someone at a table behind them talking about a television show with the passionate specificity that people reserved for things that didn't matter, and he could hear the bartender's ring against glass again, and he could feel the high-top's edge against his forearm and the stool's absence of a back and the warm air that came in every time someone opened the door to the sidewalk.

"The taping's a week from Friday," he said.

"I know."

"I don't know what to do with that."

Simone didn't say anything for a while. She looked at the bread she'd torn and not eaten. Then she said, "I don't either. Not exactly." She finished her bourbon. "I know you can't go out there and do the version of you that's already smiling before he says the line. I know that much."

"So what, I walk out there and hope I accidentally become myself?"

"Maybe. Maybe you stop trying to make the room feel handled." She rubbed her thumb against the side of the glass. "Or maybe that's too clean. I don't know. I don't know how to write the part where you quit asking permission."

"That sounds useful."

"It does. That's how you know it's dangerous."

"Detroit wasn't a method," Simone said. "You were tired, you were pissed off, and there was nothing left to protect. That's all."

"That's not reproducible."

"No," she said. "But it's more precise than 'be truthful,' which is what idiots say when they don't have a note."

"In Cleveland I was wearing a costume."

"You were. You were doing Mulaney-meets-insurance-adjuster. It was terrible."

"Thanks."

"You're welcome."

Doug signaled for the check. It came to forty-seven dollars. He put his card down. Simone reached for her wallet and he said, "I've got it," and she said, "You sure?" and he said, "You came from New York to teach me to be funny and I'm not funny. The least I can do is buy you dinner."

"You're doing it again," she said.

"Doing what?"

"The thing you do. Where you say something true and it comes out funny and you've got no idea."

"I'm not being funny. I'm telling you the truth."

"I know."

They stepped outside. The air was warm enough that his shoulders dropped without permission. High Street was lit up with storefronts and streetlights and the headlights of cars moving slowly through foot traffic, people lining up outside bars and arguing over where to eat.

They walked south. Simone had parked two blocks down, on a side street off High. Doug had parked on a meter around the corner from that. The walk was short and mostly silent. Their shoulders almost touched once at a curb and didn't.

Doug could smell something blooming. He didn't know what it was. He wasn't a plant person. But it was there, sweet and sudden, one of those late-spring Ohio smells that only showed up after the temperature finally committed.

At Simone's car — a gray Subaru with New York plates and a coffee cup visible on the passenger seat — they stopped. Simone got her keys out. She held them in her hand without unlocking the door.

"Thank you for dinner," she said.

"Yeah."

"And for the roof depreciation lecture. That was genuinely interesting."

"It's a twenty-three-year-old roof. There's a conversation to be had."

Simone stood there. Doug stood there. The distance between them was about the width of the car's side mirror. He heard a car door slam up the block and felt his right hand come off his thigh as if it had somewhere to go. Simone still hadn't unlocked the door. Her keys were pressed into her palm, and her eyes dropped to his mouth once, quick, then came back up.

If he stepped forward, he would have to do it on purpose. He didn't know whether he was supposed to touch her arm first or just go in. He didn't know where his hands were supposed to go if she turned away.

Neither of them moved. The moment held a beat longer, enough to make restraint and refusal look identical, and then Simone said, "Goodnight, Doug."

"Goodnight," Doug said.

She unlocked the car. The lights flashed once, orange in the dim of the side street. She got in. She moved the coffee cup from the passenger seat to somewhere he couldn't see. She started the engine, and Doug stepped back onto the curb, and she pulled out and turned left on High and the Subaru's taillights joined the others heading north and then were gone.

Doug walked the half block to his Civic. He sat in the driver's seat and didn't start the car. The meter had expired — he'd fed it two hours and dinner had been closer to two and a half — but when he checked, there was no ticket. The windshield was clean. Small mercies, or Columbus parking enforcement, which was inconsistent on weeknights.

He sat with his hands on the steering wheel until the dome light went out. The taping was a week from Friday. It was not just an hour. It was walking onto a stage on purpose and trying not to look like a man arriving with his hands already up.

He started the car. The engine turned over with the reliable, unbeautiful sound it always made. He drove home to Clintonville, eleven minutes, the route he could do without thinking. He parked in his usual spot. He went inside. He stood in his kitchen.

The kitchen table where he and Simone had worked for three months — her notebook, his voice, the recorder she set between them like a referee — was bare. He'd wiped it down after their last session. It was just a table now, a flat surface in a quiet apartment in a building where nobody knew about the taping or the tour or the two feet of sidewalk outside a gray Subaru that neither of them had crossed.

He took the dinner receipt out of his pocket and flattened it on the table. The total was forty-seven dollars, plus tip. On the blank back he wrote, Good evening, Columbus, because apparently every bad idea started with a greeting. He looked at it for a full ten seconds. The words sat there like advance notice. He crossed them out.

Under that he wrote, The drive to Detroit was five hours and change if traffic held through Toledo. That died too. Once it was written down it became material, and material was the thing the room could hear coming.

He left the receipt on the table beside the pen. The ink shone wet under the kitchen light, two dead openings on cheap white paper. Then he opened a beer and drank it standing at the counter. He thought about Simone's keys, the quick drop of her eyes to his mouth, how she'd said his name, and the way she'd looked at him when she said permission like it was a real object, something a person could set down and still somehow be holding. Then he rinsed the glass, put it on the rack, and went to bed. A week from Friday, he would walk onto the Lincoln Theatre stage and try not to warn the room.


# Chapter 16

On Wednesday morning Doug parked the Civic on East Long Street. The Lincoln Theatre was in a part of Columbus he'd driven past for twenty-six years without ever registering as a place where people went on purpose. He'd processed a claim once for a storefront two blocks east — water intrusion, failed flashing, four inches of standing water in a basement that had been a jazz bar in the 1940s — but the theater itself had never entered his consciousness until Layla sent him a one-page fact sheet listing its seating capacity (582 on her sheet; 580 in the Netflix contract, so her sheet was wrong and Doug had not raised it), its Art Deco heritage, and three bullet points under the heading "Why This Venue Works For Your Brand."

He had never been inside it. He was inside it now.

The lobby smelled like old carpet and fresh paint. Black cables ran from the front doors to the theater entrance under strips of gaffer tape. Doug had nearly tripped on the first strip and learned the name from a crew member. Gray adhesive residue clung to the edge of his shoe. He noticed things carefully and for no useful purpose.

The stage was bigger than he'd imagined. He'd pictured something the size of the comedy clubs — Cleveland, Pittsburgh, Detroit — where the stages were platforms, barely elevated, close enough to the front row that you could see the drinks sweating. This was different. The stage at the Lincoln Theatre was wide and deep, with a proscenium arch above it and wings on either side where things disappeared into darkness, and when Doug walked out onto it for the first time, at 10:15 a.m. on a Wednesday in early June, his footsteps echoed in a way that sounded like someone else walking behind him.

The seats were empty. Five hundred and eighty of them, balcony included. Dark red upholstery. The work lights were on. Doug could see Simone already sitting in the front row of the balcony, legs crossed, notebook on her knee, watching the stage the way she watched everything — like a doctor reading a chart she'd already diagnosed.

The crew was setting up. Nineteen people, Layla had told him, as if the number were a good sign. Doug counted them himself because counting gave him something to do while people moved around him carrying equipment he didn't understand. Four cameras on wheeled platforms. Lights hung from metal rigs above the stage and stood along the sides, aimed at the stool. At the back of the orchestra section a woman worked the sound board with headphones around her neck and a faded pink travel mug that said KAUAI. A rubber band snapped against her left wrist between tasks. Cables crossed the stage floor. A stool sat at center stage. Next to it, a small table with a glass of water and a monitor angled down toward him. Nobody had explained the monitor. Doug hadn't asked.

Charlie Falk was in the third row, center section, wearing sneakers brighter than any of the stage lights. He had a notebook, his phone, and a disposable coffee cup with a palm tree on it. Columbus apparently had a coffee place with a palm tree logo and Doug had missed it for twenty-six years. Charlie was talking to a woman in her thirties with a headset, a clipboard, and a pen behind her ear. She kept touching the pen as if checking it was still there.

"Okay!" Charlie called up to the stage. "Whenever you're ready, Doug. Just run it through. We're not rolling, this is just a feel thing. Getting the energy of the room."

The room's energy, as far as Doug could assess, was the energy of a room that was waiting for something to happen and was professionally prepared to wait as long as necessary. The crew moved efficiently and quietly. Someone adjusted a light. Someone else checked a cable connection at the base of a camera. The sound board woman sipped from her Kauai mug. The room was cool — the air conditioning had been running since before Doug arrived, and the vents were audible, a low hum that Doug tried not to think of as the sound the theater was making while it waited for him.

He looked at the stool. He looked at the glass of water. He looked at the monitor, now clearly a countdown timer set to sixty minutes and not yet started. He looked at the five hundred and eighty empty seats and the nineteen crew members and the four cameras and Charlie Falk's palm-tree coffee and Layla, who was in the back row of the orchestra section, sitting in the aisle seat, doing something with her phone that could have been texting or scrolling or praying.

He picked up the microphone from the stool. It was heavier than the ones in the clubs. Or it was the same weight and his hand was different. He couldn't tell.

"Good morning," he said. The microphone turned his voice into something that filled the room and bounced off the back wall and came back to him a half-second later, so that he heard himself greeting an empty theater twice, which felt about right for how the last six months had gone.

He started the material.
The first ten minutes held together only on the level of sequence. The opening about insurance and risk. The cereal bit. Roy's sandwich order. The water-damage section Simone kept calling the core. The words came out in the right order, at roughly the right pace, with the right pauses, and still died seven rows out. Charlie typed into his phone. The clipboard woman stopped pretending to nod. A camera operator near the left wing adjusted a lens that did not need adjusting. Doug heard the set flatten as he said it, the performed version turning into a photograph of a fire: all image, no heat. He stopped in the middle of a sentence and set the microphone back on the stool.

"Can I stop there?" he said.

"Sure," Charlie said. "Take five."

From the balcony, Simone gave one small, unsurprised shake of the head, the look Doug had seen on homeowners still trying to save floors that had already buckled. Her notebook stayed closed on her knee. Doug knew her silences by stage. This one was day four.

---

Layla took Doug to dinner at a restaurant three blocks from the theater. It was Italian, or the menu was in Italian and the bread came with olive oil, and the tables had white paper over the tablecloths that you could draw on with crayons from a cup, which suggested the restaurant had not decided what it was. Layla ordered a glass of red wine and then a second glass before the entrees arrived, which Doug had not seen her do before. She also ordered a bruschetta she didn't eat — it sat between them on a small plate, accumulating olive oil around its edges, and Doug tracked its gradual saturation with more attention than the conversation warranted because the conversation was the thing he did not want to be tracking.

Charlie was there too. He'd changed his sneakers since the rehearsal. Doug noticed this because the sneakers were now white instead of orange, which meant Charlie had brought two pairs of sneakers to Columbus for a two-day trip, which meant Charlie had a system for footwear that Doug could not access and did not want to.

"Okay," Layla said. "Here's what I've got." She was leaning forward, elbows on the white paper, and she'd drawn a small circle with one of the crayons without seeming to realize it. Red crayon. Tight circle.

"What if we restructured the format," she said. "Not the content — the content is strong, the content's really strong — but what if the delivery was more conversational? Like, what if Charlie moderated? What if it was more of a sit-down, interview-style — "

"A conversation," Charlie said. He was already nodding, already building. "Yes. Like, Doug, you sit, I sit, we just — it's like a hangout. Like a late-night vibe but without the bits. Just two guys talking and the audience is in on it."

"I'm not a talk show host," Doug said.

"No, and that's why it works. You're not hosting, you're just there. You're being Doug. I'm the conduit. I'm the thing that lets the Doug happen."

Doug picked up a piece of bread and looked at it. The bread was good. It was the best part of the evening so far, and the evening was twenty minutes old.

"Or," Layla said, pivoting without taking a breath. "Okay, other idea. What if we showed the original toast? Like, on a screen behind you, and you watch it and react."

"React to my own video."

"Yes! And you can comment on it, you can fill in the blanks, you can tell the audience what you were actually thinking at each — because that's what people want to know. They've seen the toast. They want the inside."

Doug chewed the bread. He swallowed. He set the remaining piece on his plate.

"That's not comedy. That's a DVD commentary."

The table went quiet. The first two ideas died there. Layla's crayon circle had been joined by a second circle, tighter than the first, drawn inside it. Charlie was looking at his phone but not reading it. His eyes were parked a few inches above the screen.

"What about a Q and A," Charlie said after a while. "From the audience. Totally unscripted, totally — the audience drives it, Doug responds, and we just capture what happens."

"A Q and A about what."

"About everything. About you, about the toast, about your life, about — "

"That's what the profile was. That's what the whole internet is. People asking me questions and me not having the answers they want."

"But that's the bit," Charlie said. "That IS the bit. You not having the answers — that's what people — "

"That's not a bit. That's just what happens when you ask me questions."

Layla reached for her wine glass, stopped, and drew a third circle inside the second one. The crayon made a faint sound against the paper, a dry scratching that was the loudest thing at the table. She put the crayon down. She picked it up again. She put it down.

Then Layla did something Doug had never witnessed: she stopped talking. She sat there with both hands flat on the paper tablecloth.

"What if we..." Charlie said. He lifted a finger as if the rest of the sentence were on its way, then let his hand fall and looked past the bruschetta at nothing Doug could use.

Doug looked at them both. Layla with her circles and her untouched bruschetta and her two glasses of wine. Charlie with his white sneakers and his phone by the plate, screen dark now. Nobody had another idea.

"Can I get the check?" Doug said to the waiter. Nobody argued.

---

He went back to the theater at a quarter to eleven.
