The front doors were locked but a side entrance was propped open with a rubber wedge — a man in a black T-shirt carrying a coil of cable over his shoulder nodded at Doug as he passed without asking what he was doing there. People had been nodding at Doug without questioning his presence for six months. It was one of the consistent features of being briefly famous — the assumption that you belonged wherever you showed up, even when you had no idea why you were there yourself.

The theater was almost dark. The production lights were off, the work lights were off, but a single bare bulb on a tall stand glowed at center stage — a ghost light, one of the crew had called it that morning during setup, explaining to a colleague that you always leave one burning. Tradition. Also safety, the orchestra pit being three feet down and uncovered in places. Also, the same crew member had added without smiling, ghosts. Doug hadn't been able to tell if he meant it. He'd had a tattoo of a compass on his forearm and the habit of testing cable connections by tugging them twice, always twice, with the precision of someone who had learned the cost of a loose connection.

The ghost light threw a circle of yellow on the stage floor, maybe eight feet across, and beyond it the theater was darkness and shape — the curve of the balcony, the rows of seats visible only as repeating shadows, the exit signs glowing red at the ends of the aisles like the indicator lights on smoke detectors. The HVAC was still running, quieter now than it had been during the day, a vibration Doug felt through the stage floor more than he heard through the air.

He sat on the edge of the stage, legs hanging over the front, feet dangling above the orchestra pit. The pit was shallow — three feet, maybe, with a plywood cover over most of it and a gap near the left wing where cables had been run through. Doug looked at the gap and estimated the fall onto plywood from stage height at roughly the same as stepping off a kitchen counter: survivable, stupid, enough to break a wrist if you landed wrong. He chose not to think about what it meant that his first action in the dark empty theater was to assess the fall risk. He was an adjuster. He assessed things. It was the only response to the world he'd ever had that worked reliably.

The seats stretched out in front of him, five hundred and eighty of them, visible only as shapes in the red exit-sign glow. In two days they would hold people. Kelsey would be in the fifth row. Tyler would be next to her, saying something agreeable about the venue, because Tyler said agreeable things about everything and meant them all equally and none of them specifically. Megan and Brendan would be in the back section, which Doug knew because Layla had mentioned the seat assignments and Doug had absorbed them the way he absorbed claim file details — completely, involuntarily, and with the understanding that knowing a fact and knowing what to do with it were entirely different competencies.

Gloria would be there. Fourth row center. She'd called two days ago to confirm she was coming and to tell him that Ruth had wanted to come too and Gloria had said no because "Ruth claps at the wrong times and I won't have it." Doug had not argued. Gloria's management of Ruth was not his jurisdiction.

Roy would not be there. That was Roy.

Doug sat in the quiet and pictured Gloria at Maple Glen, pushing retirement-community lasagna around the plate and telling him the real part was the table. Not the toast. The kitchen table with the water ring on it. The room after Megan left. Him sitting there with cereal and paperwork before anybody called any of it material. Gloria had recognized before he did that the only parts of him that worked were the parts he had not arranged.

He thought about Kelsey at the Thai place and the spreadsheet she'd found at sixteen. Rows, columns, timestamps. Every late pickup. Every missed permission slip. The thing he had built to survive the divorce had looked, to his daughter, like a ledger proving she belonged in the file. He kept turning people into documentation because documentation was what he knew how to make. Then he carried the same habit onto a stage and wondered why the room stayed flat.

He sat on the stage. The ghost light hummed above him. The theater breathed.

Tomorrow was a day off. Layla had scheduled it as a "mental health day," which Doug understood to mean a day where nobody had to watch him not work and everybody could pretend the problem might fix itself by Thursday night. The taping was Friday. Five hundred and eighty people would be in these seats, whether they had paid for tickets or been given them — Doug didn't know which, and the not-knowing felt like one more small thing in a pile of small things he should probably understand but didn't.

He pulled out his phone. The screen lit up his face and the first four rows of seats in a blue-white wash. It was 11:22 p.m.

He called Roy.

Roy answered on the second ring. He was still awake, unusual because Roy went to bed at 9:45. Roy had mentioned that once, years ago, not as a confession or a philosophy but as a fact. Doug had filed it with the other fixed points: the dented Camry, the paper files, the exact lunch routine. Roy said a thing once and it stayed true.

"It's late," Roy said without judgment.

"I know. Sorry."

"I was reading." A pause. "The library had a book about bridges. Civil engineering. It's not as interesting as it sounds."

"I'm at the theater," Doug said.

"The one for the show."

"Yeah."

"At eleven o'clock at night."

"I'm sitting on the stage."

Roy didn't say anything for a moment. Doug could hear, through the phone, what he was fairly certain was the sound of Roy turning a page. Roy was still reading while talking on the phone because Roy did not subordinate one activity to another when both could be conducted at the same time. In Roy's system efficiency covered most of what other people called politeness.

"I don't think I can do this," Doug said.

There was a silence, only a few seconds. Roy's silences were measured that way, not in the theatrical pauses other people used to signal they were composing something important. Roy's silences meant he was constructing a sentence, and the sentence, when it arrived, would contain exactly the information he intended and nothing more.

"Then cancel it," Roy said.

Doug sat with this. The ghost light flickered once — a power fluctuation, or a loose connection, or ghosts.

"I signed a contract," Doug said.

"Then it becomes clerical," Roy said.

"What does that mean?"

"It means people email each other."

Doug laughed. It hit his chest first, then came out. Short, surprised, real. He bent forward and put a hand over his mouth too late. For a second he recognized the same loss of control as the toast, only smaller this time and cleaner. Something true had arrived and his body got to it before the rest of him did.

He laughed and the theater caught the sound and sent it back to him from the balcony and the back wall and the five hundred and eighty empty seats. For a moment the Lincoln Theatre was full of Doug Bridge laughing at Roy turning disaster into office work. It was the first real thing that had happened on this stage all day.

"Yeah," Doug said. "People email each other."

"Go home," Roy said. "It's Wednesday."

"I know what day it is."

"Go home. Sleep. Eat something. Not cereal."

"I'll probably eat cereal."

"Then eat cereal." A pause. The sound of a page. "Friday's going to come whether you're ready or not. That's how days work."

"That's very helpful, Roy."

"It wasn't meant to be helpful. It was meant to be accurate."

Doug sat on the stage for another ten minutes after he hung up. The theater was quiet except for the HVAC and the ghost light's low electrical buzz and, faintly, from the alley, the sound of someone closing a truck door. He looked at the empty seats. He looked at the ghost light. He looked at the monitor by the stool. The countdown still showed sixty minutes.

He thought about the contract on his laptop. Eleven pages. Percentages, clauses, mutual indemnification, the tail. He had been treating it like weather, something already moving toward him whether he did anything or not. Roy had turned it back into a document. If Doug canceled, Layla would call, Charlie would call, somebody in Los Angeles would send an email, and the building would still be standing on East Long Street. Those were administrative outcomes, not acts of God.

Doug stood up. His knees cracked — a sound the theater faithfully amplified and returned to him, because that was what this building did: it took whatever you gave it and made it bigger, whether you wanted it to or not.

He walked off the stage into the wing, past the ghost light, through the side door and into the alley. The air outside was warm — early June, the season when Columbus stopped apologizing for its weather and committed, briefly, to being a place where you could stand outside at midnight without a coat. Doug's car was parked on East Long Street, under a streetlight that buzzed at a frequency similar to the ghost light's, as if the city and the theater were running on the same current.

He got in the car and sat for a moment with his hands on the wheel and the engine off. The streetlight buzzed above the windshield. Roy would already be back in his bridge book. For the first time since he signed with Layla, Friday did not feel inevitable. It felt optional. That was not relief exactly. It was smaller than relief and more useful. If he went back to the Lincoln Theatre on Friday, it would be because he chose to walk in.

He started the Civic and drove home.


# Chapter 17

The green room at the Lincoln Theatre was not green. It was the color of a claim form — the off-white of documents designed to be functional rather than noticed. A folding table, two chairs, a mirror ringed with bulbs that gave off a dry heat Doug could feel from across the room. Someone had placed a bottle of water on the table next to two pens and a note card that said GOOD LUCK DOUG in handwriting he didn't recognize. The script was there too — seventeen stapled pages, the corner curling where he'd folded it back during rehearsal, the first line visible through the top sheet. He'd read that line so many times it had stopped being language and become a shape, the way a policy number stops being a number when you've typed it enough and becomes just a motion your fingers make.

A production assistant came in — early twenties, headset, clipboard. She had a small silver ring on her thumb that she kept rotating with her index finger, a gesture so constant it seemed involuntary, something she did the way other people blinked.

"Fifteen minutes, Doug."

"Okay."

She hesitated. "Can I get you anything? Water, tea —"

"I'm good. Thank you."

She left. The door clicked shut and the room settled around him — the hum of the bulbs, the faint vibration of the building's old heating system running even in June because buildings built in 1928 did what they wanted.

Through the wall, Doug could hear the audience. Not words — a collective hum, the sound of 580 people settling into seats and producing the noise that humans make when they're together and waiting. He'd heard this sound four times before: Indianapolis, Cleveland, Pittsburgh, Detroit. Each time it had meant the same thing — that the event was real, that people had driven here and parked and found their seats and were now sitting in them expecting something from a man who adjusted insurance claims in Columbus, Ohio. They were expecting the toast guy. Doug was not sure which man would walk out there.

He stood and looked in the mirror. He was wearing a blue button-down — not the suit, the suit was retired, the suit had done enough — and khakis that were clean and pressed in a way that felt like preparation for a job interview rather than a performance. His hair was the same. His face was the same face he'd seen in the bathroom mirror six months ago, the morning after the wedding, except the bags under his eyes were less dramatic and the hangover had been replaced by something more vertical, a standing dread that had no peak and no floor. The mirror gave him exactly what he was: a fifty-two-year-old insurance adjuster from Clintonville who had been asked to be somewhere he didn't belong and had run out of ways to say no.

He picked up the script. The paper was warm from the bulbs. He put it back down.

Through a gap in the stage door he could see part of the house — the first rows, the aisle, the ornate ceiling rising above the balcony. The Lincoln Theatre had been built for vaudeville, and the plasterwork and gilded columns still had the confident excess of a building that assumed it would matter. Doug had never been inside it before Wednesday's rehearsal, and the scale of it — the carved proscenium, the painted ceiling, the fact that the ceiling was painted at all — still felt like someone else's venue. His venues were the Mutual Fidelity conference room and the break room and, recently, comedy clubs with low ceilings and tables so close to the stage you could smell the drinks. This was a theater. People performed here on purpose.

In that slice of the house he could pick out faces.

Fourth row center: Gloria. She was sitting with her purse on her lap and her coat folded over the armrest, looking straight ahead at the empty stage with the expression she brought to everything — attentive, unsoftened, prepared to have an opinion. She'd driven from Dayton. An hour on I-70. Doug had gotten a call that afternoon from Ruth at Maple Glen, who'd called to wish him luck and to inform him that Gloria had described the event as going to watch her son do whatever it was he was doing. The seat beside Gloria was empty. She hadn't brought anyone. Gloria went places alone.

Fifth row, slightly right of center: Kelsey. She was leaning toward Tyler, saying something, and Tyler was nodding the way Tyler nodded at everything — automatically, warmly, without it necessarily indicating that the content had been processed. Kelsey was wearing a sweater Doug hadn't seen before, dark green, and her hair was down, and she looked the way she looked in every context where something was about to happen to her father — alert and slightly braced, the posture of someone who has learned to prepare for the unpredictable from a man who was constitutionally incapable of predicting himself.

Back section, house left: Megan and Brendan. Doug couldn't see them clearly from this angle, but Layla had handled the tickets, which meant she'd handled the seating, which meant Megan was far enough from the cameras that whatever Doug said about their marriage wouldn't be broadcast alongside her reaction. Layla thought about these things. Doug thought about the fact that his ex-wife was in the same building and that whatever he said in the next hour would be said in front of her, and in front of the man she'd left him for, and that the man she'd left him for had once told Doug that the original toast was brave, and that Doug had wanted to evaporate when he heard the word.

Out of habit he searched for Roy, the same way he glanced at the passenger seat of his Civic on mornings when he'd driven to work alone for twelve years. Roy was at home. Roy had not been invited because Roy did not want to be invited, and Doug had not insisted because the conversation would have been brief and final. Roy's absence was not a commentary. Roy's absence was Roy.

Simone was in the balcony. She'd told him she'd be there, same spot as rehearsal. "I'll be up there," she'd said that afternoon, on the phone, her voice flat and professional. "I won't be doing anything. Just watching." He'd asked her what she'd be watching for and she'd said, "I'll know when I see it," which was either a kindness or a diagnosis, and with Simone the difference was narrow.

Layla was somewhere below, managing proximity to the event with the practiced instinct of someone who understood that being near things when they happened was half the job. Charlie Falk was in the third row — Doug had seen him at the tech check, wearing sneakers that cost more than the folding table in this room, carrying the same unshakable enthusiasm he'd brought to every meeting since LA.

The production assistant returned. "Five minutes."

Doug picked up the script again. Seventeen pages. Simone's structure, his words, callbacks and transitions and a section about insurance terminology that built to a line about coverage gaps, and a section about the toast that was supposed to be self-deprecating without being self-pitying, and a section about Roy that was supposed to be the warm part, and a section about Kelsey that was supposed to be the ending. It had all been organized. It had all been rehearsed. It had been dead for days and everybody knew it and nobody had said the thing that would have made saying it unnecessary.

He walked to the stage entrance and waited.

---

The applause when Doug walked out was warm and immediate, and it was exactly what he'd been afraid of.

Warmth meant expectation. 580 people who had watched a seven-minute video of a drunk man saying true things at a wedding and who had driven to a theater on a Friday evening in June and paid for parking and bought tickets that were, based on what Layla had told him, not cheap — these people wanted the man from the video. They wanted the voice that had broken when it talked about the kitchen table. They wanted the flat insurance-adjuster delivery that the internet had decided was a comic style. They wanted the toast guy, and the toast guy had been produced by eight whiskeys and twenty years of silence, and Doug did not have either of those things tonight. He had a script and stage lights and a microphone and the accumulated evidence of four previous performances that the thing they wanted was beyond him.

The lights were bright enough that the first four or five rows were visible and everything beyond them dissolved into shapes — the general fact of bodies, the occasional glint of a phone screen. He could see Gloria. She was sitting with her hands on her purse, watching him with the composure of someone who had spent seventy-eight years watching things and had not yet been surprised. He could see Kelsey, leaning forward in the fifth row, Tyler beside her with his pleasant posture.

Doug picked up the microphone from the stand. It had the weight of the ones at the clubs but different — a theater mic, heavier in the base, the kind that expected to be held by someone who knew what to do with it.

"Good evening," he said. The words came out in his work voice — the one he used on the phone with claimants, steady and a bit too formal for a room full of people who'd come to laugh. A few people laughed anyway. He didn't know if they were laughing at the formality or because they were ready to laugh and any sound from him would have done it. Both were problems.

He started the script.

The opening section was about the video — a self-deprecating preamble that Simone had built to acknowledge the toast, contain it, and pivot. "So I gave a toast at my daughter's wedding," he began, and paused for the recognition, which came, warm and easy. "Eighty million views. I checked this morning. Eighty million people have watched me do the worst thing I've ever done, and about twelve of them left comments calling me brave, which — I've never associated bravery with not remembering what I said. That's usually covered under a different part of the policy." It landed. Mild, warm, the audience easing into what they understood: a comedy special with the guy from the video. Doug moved to the second section — the aftermath, the phone calls, the internet turning his face into a meme — and the laughs continued, spaced and polite, the sound of a room that was with him without being surprised by him. It was fine. Fine was what it had been in rehearsal. Fine was what it had been in every club, at every venue where Doug had stood with scripted words and delivered them in order and on time and produced a response that was technically laughter but felt, to him, like the acoustic version of a courtesy handshake.

Four minutes in, he could feel it. The room was kind. The room was with him. And the room was not alive.

He knew the difference because he'd felt the other thing — in flashes, in accidents, in the eight minutes in Detroit where he'd talked about gas station coffee and highway driving and the room had leaned in, and in the ninety seconds in Pittsburgh where the pool story had worked, and in the seven minutes of the original toast where the whiskey had dissolved every barrier between what he thought and what he said and the room had gone silent in the way that means someone has reached something true. This was not that. This was 580 people being generous about the fact that the man they'd come to see was reading from a script, and generosity was worse than hostility because hostility at least meant the room was engaged.

He reached the end of the third section — a bit about the Netflix meeting, about a development executive who'd called his life "dad-core" to his face — and the laugh it produced was the laugh of recognition, the audience locating the reference they'd expected. Doug stood in the sound of it with the script in his left hand, the pages soft from the moisture of his grip, and he looked at the stool beside him where a bottle of water sat, where the script was supposed to go during the transition to section four. The Roy section. The warm part. The part that worked best in rehearsal because talking about Roy let Doug be something resembling funny without trying, except that doing it from a script was trying, and the gap between reading a version of Roy and just talking about Roy was the same gap that had been killing him for six months, and the gap was the whole problem, and the gap was every problem.

His eyes dropped to the script, then lifted to the room.

He set the script on the stool the way he set down a claims file when he'd read it through and needed to think about what he'd read. The pages sat there, and the room, which didn't know what the script contained or that setting it down was anything, simply waited.

Silence. Three seconds. Five.

"There's a script on that stool," Doug said. His voice had changed. The work-voice formality was gone, and what came out was flatter, plainer, the voice he used at lunch with Roy or on the phone with Kelsey. "I've been carrying versions of it from one room to another for weeks and it keeps turning into the wrong thing. Indianapolis. Cleveland. Pittsburgh. Detroit. Two nights ago I stood here with the cameras on and the crew in their seats and the comedy writer I've been working with up there in the balcony." He gestured vaguely toward the dark above the lights. "I got to the end and she had that look people get when the report came back the way they knew it would."

A quiet laugh moved through part of the room and died.

"So I don't know what this is," Doug said. "I'm not doing the script. The script is over there. It's fine. There's a good bit about insurance in there, there's a section about my friend Roy that probably would've gotten a real laugh around minute twelve. I'm sorry you won't hear it the way it was supposed to go. Roy is —" He paused. "He's also not here tonight. Roy doesn't go to things. He goes to work, he goes to the sandwich place, he goes home. I didn't ask him to come because I knew what he'd say, and what he'd say would be either 'no' or nothing, and both of those mean the same thing."

The room settled after that. Programs stopped rustling. Someone in the left section leaned forward. Doug could feel most of them staying with him. He could also feel the ones who hadn't decided yet — people in the middle rows who'd bought tickets for the toast guy and were getting a man standing under lights holding a microphone and no visible plan.

Once he'd said that much, more came.

Layla came first. The woman who had flown to Columbus and walked into his apartment in Clintonville and looked at his Ikea bookshelf and his one art print and his kitchen where he ate cereal standing at the counter, and he'd watched her do a sweep of the room the way he swept a room after a flood — quick, comprehensive, the eyes moving from surface to surface and calculating something. "I'm an insurance adjuster," he said. "I assess properties. I evaluate damage for a living. And this woman walked into my apartment and I could see her doing it — my job. She was doing my job on my apartment. She was calculating the value." He took a breath through his nose. "She told me I was a brand. I said I adjust insurance claims. She said, 'That's the brand.' And I don't — I still don't really know what that means, but she seemed confident, and she talks fast enough that by the time you've decided you don't agree with something she's already three ideas ahead and the original thing doesn't matter anymore." He shifted his weight. "She calls me 'babe.' Nobody's ever called me that. Not my ex-wife, not — nobody. The first time she said it I turned around to see who she was talking to."

A real laugh came from the left section and parts of the middle, individual laughs rather than a collective sound. Doug noticed this and then let it go.

From there he moved to Los Angeles, to a glass-walled conference room with succulents on the table and a man in sneakers that probably had their own publicist. He described the words — "authentic," "vulnerable," "dad-core," "the masculinity conversation" — and the way they arrived with enormous conviction and no referent, like a weather report for a planet that didn't exist. Then he got to the hotel in Silver Lake with no closet doors and a book on the nightstand called *The Surrender Experiment*. "The shower was all glass," he said. "Every wall. I could see the whole bathroom and half the bedroom. I stood in there with the water running and I thought, this is the opposite of a wall, and I'm supposed to be surrendering, and I don't know what I'm surrendering to but apparently it requires being visible from every angle at all times." He let that picture sit for a second. "I took a very fast shower."

A bigger laugh. Doug didn't pause for it the way a comedian would have. He moved forward, because moving forward was what he did when he didn't know what else to do, and because the laugh felt like something he'd stumbled into rather than earned, and stopping to appreciate it would have been the same as claiming it.

Then he got into insurance. Actual cash value and replacement cost. The depreciation schedule for residential appliances. What a seven-year-old water heater was worth on paper versus what it cost to have a new one installed. Doug went into it with genuine engagement, the way he went into it at work, leaning into the distinctions that mattered to him and that were, in their own way, more interesting than anything he'd been asked to do on a stage. The room held for about ninety seconds. Then a cough broke loose past the tenth row, followed by a rustle in the middle section and the faint blue click of a phone screen turning on. He heard it and kept going for another thirty seconds because he was in the middle of explaining something and stopping there went against an instinct that had been running since his first year at Mutual Fidelity. He had never left a policyholder in the middle of a coverage question and he was not going to do it to a theater full of strangers.

He stopped. "Short version," he said. "Your stuff is worth what it's worth now, not what you paid for it. That's — a furnace you bought eight years ago, on paper it's not the same furnace. It's depreciated. And that's true about — I don't actually know if that's a metaphor or just a fact about furnaces." He stood there for a second. "I do better with actual numbers. I'm better when there's a form."

A pocket of laughter came from the left side and part of the back. The middle rows stayed quiet.

The pool in Westerville came next. The above-ground pool whose wall gave out at two in the morning and sent several thousand gallons of water across the property line into the neighbor's rose garden — seventeen hybrid tea roses, documented with photographs and receipts, assessed value just over four thousand dollars. The neighbor filed a claim. The pool owner's policy didn't cover damage to adjacent properties from structural failure of recreational equipment. The neighbor's homeowner's policy covered weather events and named perils, but a neighbor's pool wall collapsing overnight was not a named peril.

"Nobody was covered," Doug said. "There was a gap in the coverage. The roses were dead and the water was gone and two insurance companies both agreed that neither of them was responsible, which — if you've ever tried to get two insurance companies to agree on anything, you'd know that this is an achievement. They agreed on nothing. Unified in their refusal." He let the sentence sit. The room was quieter now, a different quiet from the insurance-terminology quiet. This one had attention in it. "These were roses somebody had been growing for eleven years. She could tell you the name of each one. Named varieties. And they were sitting in chlorinated water and there was nothing in either policy that addressed the thing that had happened, because the thing that had happened wasn't imagined by anyone who wrote the coverage."

He shifted his weight and tried another angle. "A woman I work with — the comedy writer, the one in the balcony — she was the first person I told that story to. She laughed. I told her the part that interested me was that both policies were clear and the roses were still dead. She wrote it down. She writes everything down. I've been talking to her for four months and she's been writing it all down and I still don't entirely know what she's going to do with any of it."

His gaze went up toward the balcony, toward the dark space above the lights where Simone was sitting. He couldn't see her. He kept his face turned that way anyway.

Indianapolis followed: the pharmaceutical conference, four hundred people who had been drinking since the afternoon session ended, the room deciding in the irreversible way rooms decide that he was not what they'd come for. "I did nineteen minutes," he said. "I was supposed to do twenty-five. I stopped and I said I thought that was probably enough. And they clapped." He waited through the memory of it. "Polite applause. That — I didn't know this before Indianapolis, but that's worse than silence. If nobody claps, at least I know where I am. That's a number I can work with. But polite applause is —" His fingers tightened around the mic. "I process claims. Sometimes I deny a claim and the policyholder says thank you. They thank you for telling them no. That's what polite applause sounds like. People being very polite about the fact that you failed."

This got a laugh — a real one, surprised, from more of the room than he'd had in a while. Doug didn't slow down for it. He was looking at a point on the stage floor about six feet in front of him, the way he looked at claim files when he was thinking through a coverage question.

From there he got to cereal. The experience of eating it at the kitchen counter at seven-thirty in the evening, standing up, because sitting at the table alone felt like a decision he wasn't ready to make — like sitting down would make the aloneness official, would convert it from a temporary condition into a filed claim. Then the price of cereal — he quoted the price per box at the Kroger on High Street and the price it had been two years ago and the percentage increase, because he had these numbers and not having them would have been the same as not knowing. "After the video took off, I quit buying store brand," he said. "Not because I got richer. I didn't. Same paycheck. Same apartment. But I was standing there in the cereal aisle thinking eighty million people have heard me say the kitchen-table part, so maybe I can pay for the box with the strip across the top that closes right." His shoulders lifted once. "I don't know if that's funny either. It's just what I did."

A laugh came a second late, from the back first and then the middle, and Doug was already looking for the next thing by the time it reached him.

Doug lost the thread. It happened between the cereal and whatever should have come next — there was no next, there was no structure, there was only him and the microphone and the lights and his own life, which had been producing material for fifty-two years without asking his permission and which had now, at this moment, stopped supplying anything. He stood there. The silence extended into a space that felt like thirty seconds but was probably five or six. A woman in the second row uncrossed and recrossed her legs. Someone in the balcony cleared their throat. The stage lights hummed at a frequency he could feel more than hear, a vibration in the floor that came up through his shoes.

"I drove to Dayton to visit my mother," Doug said, finding something. "After the Indianapolis thing. My mother is seventy-eight years old. She lives in a retirement community called Maple Glen, which — the name suggests something. Maples and a glen. The building does not deliver on either of those. It's a fine building. She's fine in it. She's fine everywhere. My mother doesn't experience her surroundings. She experiences her opinions about her surroundings, and the opinions don't change based on the building."

A laugh. Gloria, in the fourth row, did not move.

"She watched the video four times. She had notes." He pressed his lips together. "My mother has notes on everything. She has notes on my hair and my apartment and my car. She had notes on my marriage while I was still in it, and —" He stopped, let a half-thought go. "She told me the part about the table was the first honest sentence she'd heard out of me in years. She told me my father would have been embarrassed." He shifted the microphone higher in his hand. "Not by the toast. By the slouching."

He didn't look at Gloria. Looking at her while talking about her would have required something — not permission, because Gloria didn't grant permission, but an acknowledgment of the circuit between them that would have changed the current in it. He talked past her, to the dark middle rows, and he could feel her watching him from seven meters away with the same composed attention she'd been bringing to his entire life.

When Roy came back into it, it was the real Roy, the man one desk over at Mutual Fidelity who had achieved something Doug didn't have a name for, something that existed in the space between wanting more and accepting less, a place where the word "adequate" meant what it was supposed to mean and nothing else. He got into Roy's sandwich order, which had been the same for nine years, and then the call from a Holiday Inn in Indianapolis at eleven-thirty at night after the worst performance of his life, Roy saying the bad part was everybody being decent to him afterward, and how that sentence had been more useful than anything anyone had told him in six months — any manager, any comedy writer, any development executive with vocabulary. "Roy never watched the video," Doug said. "Eighty million people did. Roy didn't. He said if something is already on a screen in front of him, fine, but he was not going home to spend an evening looking at me on purpose." Doug stood there for a second. "Everybody else had a reaction. My mother had notes. My ex-wife — everybody had something to say about it. And Roy just didn't look." He checked the next words the way he'd check a number on a form before committing it to the record. "I keep trying to find the right word for what that is. Generous, I think. He just let me have it. He let me have the thing without telling me what he thought about it, and I —" He took another breath. "I think that might be what generous actually means. I'm not sure. But I think so."

The laugh that followed was smaller and quieter than the earlier ones, and it came from fewer places.

There was another dead stretch. Doug drifted toward the profile — the journalist who'd spent two days following him, the article that came out with a headline calling him a comedian — but the story went sideways. He couldn't find the piece of it that mattered in front of these people, in this room, under these lights. He started a sentence, abandoned it, started another. The room felt him searching and some of them waited and some of them didn't, and the ones who didn't were looking at the people next to them or shifting in their seats, and they were not wrong to, because the story genuinely didn't have a destination. He let it go. "I don't remember where I was going with that," he said, and a few people laughed, and a few people checked their phones.

After the profile story died, he fell back on the apartment: the Ikea bookshelf that had a lean to it, the art print from a Columbus gallery that his ex-wife had left behind when she moved out because it didn't match her new house, the counter where he ate cereal.

And then he said something he hadn't planned to say, which was the only condition under which he'd ever said anything worth hearing.

"I have a second bedroom," he said. "I've been in this apartment for twelve years. Since the divorce. And the second bedroom is an office. That's what I call it. Except there's no computer in there. No desk." He swallowed once. "There are seven boxes. I packed them when I moved out of the house. The house where my daughter grew up. The house with the kitchen table. I packed them and I labeled them — BOOKS, KITCHEN MISC, TAX DOCS 2010 through 2013, PICTURES, one marked KELSEY, and two that just say STUFF because I ran out of categories before I ran out of things." He drew in air through his nose, and the room was quiet, the full-room quiet that has weight and direction. "They've been in that room for twelve years. I close the door. I pay rent on a two-bedroom apartment and one of the bedrooms has seven boxes in it that I packed twelve years ago and haven't opened. I won't unpack them and I won't throw them away. Unpacking would mean I live here now. Throwing them away would mean I don't live there anymore. And I haven't been able to —" He stopped. The sentence didn't end so much as arrive at a wall. "The boxes just sit there. In the dark. Behind a closed door. For twelve years. And I pay rent on the room they sit in."

The room went quiet hard enough that he could hear somebody in the middle section take a breath and not let it out right away. Doug stood there with his hand around the microphone and let the silence stay where it landed.

"Yeah," he said, after a while. He didn't add anything. He didn't need to.

He kept going after that because if he stopped there, the boxes would be the last thing in the room, and he was not ready to leave them with strangers.

"After Indianapolis I went back to the Holiday Inn and sat on a bedspread with a pattern that looked designed by people who did not want any individual event to stand out," he said. "Burgundy, navy, little gold lines. The kind of blanket where if something happened on it, the blanket had an argument ready." A laugh broke loose near the back and spread thinly across the room.

Doug kept going. "Hotels do that. Insurance companies do it too, sometimes. If the surface is busy enough, the damage gets harder to classify. Was it staining, saturation, repeated seepage, maintenance failure —"

The laugh died before he reached the end of the list. Somebody near the aisle coughed. A phone screen came on in the balcony and stayed on. Doug heard himself naming categories and knew he had gone back to work in the middle of the set.

He stopped. "This is why the script was seventeen pages," he said. A few people laughed, mostly out of relief. "Every time I get nervous I start adjusting a claim nobody filed."

And then he said Kelsey's name.

"My daughter —" he said, and stopped.

He hadn't planned to talk about her. He hadn't planned not to. She was in the fifth row and he couldn't see her face clearly under the lights, but he could see the dark green of the sweater and the way she was sitting — forward, her elbows on her knees, the posture he'd watched her take at the Thai place and in his apartment and at the kitchen table in the house on Briarwood where she used to pound the keys on her TI-84 calculator while he filled out proof-of-loss forms on the other end. He had opened his mouth and her name had been in it, and then the thing behind the name — the table and the homework and the calculator, the spreadsheet he'd kept during the divorce, the lunch where she'd told him she'd found it, the pad thai and the curry and the mango sticky rice — all of it pressed forward with a weight that his voice could not carry. He'd been carrying it for months. He'd been carrying it for years. He'd been carrying it the way he carried the boxes, behind a closed door, paying rent on the room where it sat and never opening it.

He stood there with the microphone lowered to his side and his mouth slightly open and nothing coming out. The mechanism had quit. He was sober enough to feel every edge of the silence, the sharp specific edges of standing in front of 580 people and his daughter and his mother and his ex-wife and not being able to finish a sentence that started with her name.

Five seconds. Eight. The room held. No coughing, no phones. The silence just sat there, ordinary and huge, the way it would in an empty room except this one held 580 people.

Doug took a breath. "I was going to —" he started, and stopped again, and then found a narrower version of the sentence that could fit through. "She's here. She's in the fifth row. And I want to say things to her that I can't say to — I've never had — I had the right words once, and I was drunk, and I didn't choose them. They just happened. And I've been trying to find them again for six months and they're not —" He stopped for the third time. "I'm going to move on. I'm sorry, Kels. I'll call you tomorrow."

He moved on. The room let him. The silence where the Kelsey moment had been stayed in the air like weather that had passed through but left the pressure changed, and when he spoke again he reached for something smaller.

"My car starts every morning," he said. "Gray Civic. Dent by the rear wheel well. The fan works on two settings: too little and full aviation. But every morning I turn the key and it starts. That's the whole interaction. I respect that in a machine." The laugh came quick this time, broad enough that even Doug could not mistake it for kindness.

He nodded once and kept going. "A woman called me last month because her sump pump failed during a storm. She'd called three times before anybody called her back. By the time I got her on the phone she was crying hard enough I couldn't get the street number out of her, so I said, okay, don't give me the address yet. Tell me what room the water is in. She said basement, utility room, half the rec room. I said good. Three rooms. We can start with three rooms. Take pictures before you move anything. Put wet paper in one pile and dry paper in another. Don't throw anything away yet. We can make a list." Nobody laughed at the list part. Nobody checked a phone either.

She calmed down because there was an order to it. There was a form. There was a sequence. Doug could hear his own voice settling into the work cadence, the one part of him that had not failed all night.

"That's what I do," he said. "That's what I actually do. Not this." He took in the stage, the lights, the camera in the back. "I don't know why you're here. I'm not sure why I'm here. But I think —"

He stopped. The sentence had reached the place where it should end and he let it.

"I think that's everything."

---

He said it quietly, looking at a spot just beyond the footlights.

The room held for a moment. Two seconds. Three. Doug stood with the microphone in his hand and the lights on his face.

Then the applause started.

It started in pieces — the left section, someone in the middle, a pocket near the back. It never turned into a roar. It stayed ragged and separate, some people clapping hard and fast, some slower. A few people stood. Then more. Plenty stayed seated. Some were on their feet because they meant it. Some got there a second later because the person beside them had already risen.

In the fifth row Kelsey was standing. Her face was doing something he couldn't read from this distance — not crying, not smiling, but the expression of someone watching a person she's known her whole life do a thing she hadn't expected and hadn't finished categorizing. Tyler was beside her, standing, clapping the way Tyler did everything — steadily, pleasantly, the reliable rhythm of a man who was easy to be next to and difficult to describe. Kelsey's hands were at her sides. She wasn't clapping. She was standing there, looking at him.

Toward the back Megan's face was gone in the glare. He could make out Brendan's height, the shape of them together, but the lights were too bright and the distance too far and whatever Megan's expression held was hers and not his to know.

Fourth row center: Gloria.

Gloria was sitting. Her purse was in her lap. Her coat was folded on the armrest. Her hands were still on the purse, and she was not clapping. She was looking at Doug directly.

And she nodded.

Once. Small enough that somebody three seats over might have missed it.

Doug saw it, and for a second the applause around her sounded far away.

He set the microphone back in the stand. He didn't wave. He didn't bow. He walked off stage the way he walked out of flooded basements and smoke-damaged kitchens and the ruined rooms of other people's lives — with the steady gait of a man who had seen what there was to see, and documented it, and was going home.

Backstage, Layla was standing in the hallway with her phone and an expression that Doug recognized as calculation — not the numbers but the narrative, what this had been, what it could become, how you categorize a performance that wasn't a comedy special and wasn't the toast and wasn't anything she had existing vocabulary for. Her mouth was open and she was about to say something when Doug came through.

"Doug, that was —"

"Yeah," he said. He didn't wait for the word. He walked past her, down the narrow corridor that smelled like old paint and warm wiring and the layered dust of a building that had been hosting people's performances for nearly a hundred years. He found the green room. The beige room. He sat in the folding chair and put his hands on his knees.

The script was still on the stool, out on the stage. Seventeen pages under the lights. He thought about it sitting there — the staple, the curled corner, the first line still visible through the top sheet. Someone from the crew would pick it up. Or they wouldn't. Either way, it would be there for a while, in the light, in the empty theater, the document of a plan that had been replaced by whatever the last sixty-three minutes had been.

He picked up the water bottle from the table and drank all of it. The water was room temperature and tasted like nothing, which was fine. He sat in the folding chair in the room that was not green and he listened to the sound of the building settling around him — the audience leaving, the crew beginning to break down, the theater returning to itself. He sat there and he breathed, and the breathing was easy, and the room was quiet, and the quiet was the same quiet his apartment had, and it was enough.


# Chapter 18

Six months later, the special went live on Netflix at nine o'clock Eastern on Friday, a scheduled premiere Layla had spent the week calling an event. Doug watched it alone in his apartment, sitting on the couch with no lights on except the television. He had considered not watching. He had considered driving to Dayton, or going to a movie, or processing the backed-up claims on his kitchen table. But when the play button appeared where the countdown had been, he pressed it and watched the whole thing.

It was strange, seeing himself on a screen that size. The Lincoln Theatre stage looked bigger than he remembered, and the version of him standing on it looked smaller — a man in khakis and a button-down, holding a microphone like someone had handed him a fish and told him to speak into it. He watched himself start the scripted material. He watched himself stop. He watched the seven seconds of silence after he set the notes on the stool — he had counted five in the moment, but it was seven — and he watched himself start talking without a plan, which from the outside looked either very brave or very stupid or both.

Some of it was hard to watch. The insurance material went on too long around the twelve-minute mark, when he'd gotten deep into depreciation schedules and the room had settled into the patient silence of people waiting for him to move on. He could see himself lose them. He could see the second when he had no idea how to get them back, and then somehow get them back two minutes later with the thing about cereal, a line that had vanished from his memory entirely and still got a laugh that surprised him. There was a stretch around minute thirty where he was talking about driving between comedy clubs and eating gas station food and the room was with him in a way he could feel even through the screen. Then he said something about the pharmaceutical conference in Indianapolis and a woman in the fourth row laughed so hard she put her head down. Doug watched himself absorb it without turning his head and keep talking.

The sixty-three minutes had felt like either three hours or nine minutes when he was on stage. Watching them back, they felt like sixty-three minutes.

He turned off the television when it ended. He did not watch the credits. He went to bed at 10:47, which was early for him, and he slept without trouble, which surprised him.

Saturday and Sunday he checked his phone the way he checked the weather, once in the morning and once at night, and late Sunday Layla sent Netflix's internal forty-eight-hour number: 1.4 million households had started the special, followed by two more texts explaining why that was solid.
A *Vulture* review spent six paragraphs trying to decide what the hour was and ended without being much help; Doug read it at the kitchen counter, set the phone face down, and drank a glass of water.
A Reddit thread split almost cleanly between people calling the special boring and people calling it devastating, with a smaller faction insisting the whole unscripted hour had to be planned, and after a few minutes Doug slid the phone into the kitchen drawer and shut it hard enough for the silverware to knock together.
He left it there until dark, finished two backed-up claims, ate leftover Thai out of the carton, and watched a baseball game with the sound low.

---

Monday morning, Doug drove to Mutual Fidelity. The route was the same route it had been for fifteen years — Clintonville to the office, past the gas station where he sometimes filled up and the Wendy's he never went to and the elementary school where crossing guards appeared and disappeared with the academic calendar. It was June now. The trees along High Street were fully leaved, a heavy, saturated green that had nothing to do with the gray-white winter light of six months ago when his phone had started ringing and hadn't stopped. He parked in the lot, third row, the same general area he always parked, and sat in the Civic for a moment with the engine off. The lot smelled like warm asphalt. A cardinal was doing something aggressive in a bush near the entrance.

He went inside. He took the elevator to the second floor and walked the hallway to the office, where the carpet was that gray-brown of institutional neutrality — a color that existed only in office buildings and airport terminals and that Doug had been walking on for so long he'd stopped seeing it and now, for some reason, noticed again. The floor hummed with thirty people processing other people's disasters — keyboards, phone murmurs, the copier cycling in the corner. The fluorescent lights gave everything the same flat, even quality, which was their job.

Roy was already at his desk. His coffee was half gone, which meant he'd been there since at least 8:15. His desk was clean. It was always clean — a monitor, a keyboard, a phone, a pen cup with two pens, and whatever file he was currently working, squared to the desk's edge. Roy's desk looked like a display model for the concept of having a desk.

"Doug," Roy said.

"Morning," Doug said.

He sat down. He put his keys in the top left drawer, where they always went. He turned on his monitor. There were thirty-seven new emails, most of them the usual — automated claim updates, a department-wide reminder about the July 4th office schedule, something from HR about a summer wellness challenge that involved counting steps. Janet walked by and said, "Morning, Doug," and did not mention the special. Doug did not know if this was because she hadn't seen it or because Janet understood that some things did not require comment in a professional environment. He was grateful either way.

Roy said nothing about the special. Doug's entire six-month excursion into public life sat outside Roy's area of interest. Roy cared about claim disputes, sandwich orders, and whether the office thermostat was set too low, which it always was. The special was an hour of television about Doug, and Roy already saw Doug five days a week. He'd skipped the premiere and planned to leave it there. If Doug had asked Roy directly whether he planned to watch it, Roy would have said he'd rather spend the hour some other way and meant it the way other people meant "cilantro tastes like soap" — a fact about himself that required no further discussion.

They ate lunch at the sandwich place at noon. They sat in the same booth they always sat in. Roy ordered what he always ordered. Doug ordered what he always ordered. They talked about a claim Roy was working — a family in Reynoldsburg whose detached garage had taken a tree limb during last week's storm. The limb had come from the neighbor's silver maple, which raised the question of whether the neighbor's homeowner's policy was liable if the tree had shown signs of disease prior to failure.

"I got two arborist opinions," Roy said. "First one said it was obviously dead wood. Second one said you can't determine structural integrity from external observation."

"What did you think?"

"I'm not an arborist."

"But the bark. Could you tell?"

Roy chewed his sandwich. He considered. "The bark was peeling."

"That's dead wood."

"Probably. But I'm not going to put that in the report. I adjust insurance claims. I don't diagnose trees."

They finished. Doug paid because it was his turn in a rotation they'd never formalized but had maintained without error for nine years. They walked back across the parking lot in the noon sun, which was full June warmth now, the kind that sat on the asphalt and radiated upward through the soles of your shoes.

The afternoon had a new claim in Doug's queue. A woman in Hilliard whose basement had flooded when her sump pump failed during the Thursday storm. Six inches of standing water. Ruined carpet. A waterlogged furnace. Possible mold risk depending on how quickly the water had been extracted. Her name was Linda Ostrowski. She had called three times since Friday. The notes from the intake representative said she was "concerned about timeline" and "first-time claimant."

Doug picked up the phone and dialed. She answered before the first ring finished, which meant she'd been sitting near the phone, which was something Doug recognized. It was the anxiety of someone whose house had done something wrong and who did not understand the system that was supposed to help.

"Mrs. Ostrowski? This is Doug Bridge. I'm the adjuster assigned to your claim."

"Oh. Oh, thank God. I've been calling — I know I've been calling a lot, and I'm sorry about that, I just — nobody told me what happens next and I've got all this water and the carpet is — I think there might be mold already, is that possible? It's only been five days. Can mold grow in five days?"

Her voice was thin and fast. She was maybe sixty, maybe older — Doug could hear the age in the slight quaver underneath the speed, and the way she apologized for taking up space in someone's day.

"Mold can start in forty-eight to seventy-two hours depending on temperature and humidity," Doug said. "But we'll get someone out to assess. I want to walk you through what's going to happen so you know what to expect. Is that all right?"

"Yes. Please. Yes. I've got my husband's folder out — he used to handle all of this, the policies, the bills, all of it. He passed in October. I found the policy number but I don't know if it covers what happened."

"Your policy covers this," Doug said. He had the file open. Standard homeowner's with an equipment breakdown endorsement. Deductible of a thousand dollars. Preliminary damage estimate of $11,400. "You have an endorsement that covers mechanical failure of equipment like a sump pump. You're covered, Mrs. Ostrowski."

"I don't even know what that means, an endorsement. He would have known."

"It means this is the kind of thing your policy was designed for. Here's what happens next." He walked her through it. An adjuster would come out within three business days. He'd document the damage — photographs, measurements, moisture readings. He'd generate a repair estimate. She'd get a copy. She'd choose a contractor from the approved list or her own. They'd handle the carpet, the furnace assessment, the mold inspection if needed. Her out-of-pocket was the deductible. Everything else was covered.

"And the furnace," she said. "The furnace is making a sound it didn't make before. Like a clicking. Like something's loose in there. Is that — should I be worried?"

"Don't run it until the adjuster's been out. I'm going to flag it for priority assessment. If the furnace is damaged, it's part of the claim."

"Thank you. I'm — thank you. I'm sorry I called so many times. I just didn't know who to talk to."

"You didn't call too many times," Doug said. "You called the right number of times."

She thanked him again and hung up. Doug typed his notes: furnace priority flag, mold-risk timeline, first-time claimant, husband deceased in October, anxious about process questions. Then he sent the file to scheduling and watched the priority flag appear beside Linda Ostrowski's name before the next claim replaced her on his screen.

He worked the rest of the afternoon. A hail damage assessment in Westerville. A follow-up on a kitchen fire in Gahanna. An inquiry about a detached garage sinking on one side due to soil settlement, which was not covered under the homeowner's policy and required a careful explanation of why. Janet walked by around three and mentioned the July 4th schedule again. Roy's phone rang and Roy answered it with "Utley" and listened and wrote something on a pad and hung up. The copier jammed and someone in the next row swore at it and someone else came over and unjammed it.

---

A text from Kelsey arrived at 3:40.

*Tyler and I watched it last night. He laughed at the pool part. I cried at the boxes part. We're coming down next weekend.*

Doug read it twice. Tyler had laughed at the pool part — the bit about the above-ground pool bursting and the neighbor's roses drowning and the coverage gap nobody could solve. Tyler had listened. Tyler had laughed at that part. This was more information about Tyler than Doug had accumulated in two years of knowing him.

Kelsey had cried at the boxes part. That was the thing Doug had said on stage about the second bedroom — how it had been an office for twelve years except there was no computer in it, just boxes from the move that he'd never unpacked, and how the boxes had sat there long enough that leaving them packed had become a decision he'd made every day without knowing he was making it. He didn't remember the exact words. He remembered the silence in the room when he said it, and then the laugh that came after, with something caught in it.

Doug texted back: *I'll clean the office.*

Kelsey: *Don't you dare.*

He put the phone on his desk and sat there for a moment. Then he turned back to the Gahanna fire follow-up.

Gloria called at 4:15. She called his desk phone, not his cell, which meant she'd dialed the Mutual Fidelity main number and asked the receptionist to transfer her, because Gloria did not believe in cell phones for anything short of a genuine emergency. She'd once defined emergency as "the house is on fire or someone is dead," and she'd said it to a Verizon salesman who had not asked.

"Douglas."

"Hi, Mom."

"I watched it three times."

"You didn't have to—"

"I watched it three times because the first time I was forming my opinion and the second time I was testing my opinion and the third time I was confirming it. My opinion is as follows."

Doug leaned back in his chair. Roy, at the next desk, glanced over. Doug made a face that Roy had learned to read over fifteen years as *my mother is delivering a verdict.* Roy turned back to his screen.

"The insurance material went on too long. You lost the room around that depreciation part. Nobody in a theater knows what depreciation means."

"That's actually not—"

"The shampoo was manipulative."

Doug sat with that. "Manipulative how?"

"You picked the one thing that would make a room full of people feel sorry for you and you put it right out there. Your father did that." She was quiet for a second. "Harold used to do that. He'd come home from somewhere and find the one detail that made him look the worst off and put it right where you had to look at it. I'm not saying the shampoo wasn't true. I'm saying you picked it on purpose."

Doug did not have an answer for this that wouldn't prove her point.

"The part about your father," Gloria said, and stopped. Gloria Bridge did not stop mid-thought. "He'd have hated it. He'd have left the room." Then: "That means you got it right."

"Thanks, Mom."

"I'm not complimenting you. Did you make money?"

He told her the number.

There was a silence on the line. Doug could hear her calculating — not just the dollar amount but the ratio of effort to compensation, the value proposition, the dignity-to-dollars conversion that Gloria's generation computed automatically and without apology. She had opinions about money that were hard and unyielding, rooted in a lifetime of knowing exactly what things cost.

"That's decent money," she said. "For talking."

She hung up without saying goodbye. She had been ending phone calls this way for as long as Doug had been alive and probably before that. He set the receiver back in the cradle and reopened the Gahanna fire follow-up. The cursor was still waiting in the same box.

He drove home at 5:20. The traffic was the usual Monday-in-June crawl, every light stacking six or seven cars at a time. He stopped at the Kroger on High Street. He went in for milk, which he actually needed, and came out with milk, a dozen eggs, a loaf of bread, a package of butter, and a bag of spinach that he put in the cart without knowing why. He had never in his adult life voluntarily purchased spinach. He let the impulse stand. He put the bags in the back seat of the Civic and drove the rest of the way home and carried them up the stairs.

The apartment was the same — the Ikea bookshelf, the art print from the Columbus gallery that Megan had left behind because it didn't match the new house, the kitchen counter where he'd eaten standing up approximately four thousand times since the divorce. The glass shower. The bedroom with the bed he made every morning because making the bed was the kind of small order that kept the day from starting loose.

He put the groceries away. The eggs and milk in the refrigerator, the bread on the counter, the spinach in the crisper drawer where it would sit, he suspected, until it liquefied into a small tragedy of its own — wilted, then brown, then a thing in a bag that no policy covered. He would throw it out in a week, probably. But he'd bought it.

He stood in the hallway outside the second bedroom. The door was closed, the way it had been for months. The room had been shut long enough that his memory stopped somewhere before the wedding, before the toast, before the video, before any of it. He turned the handle and pushed the door open. He stayed in the hallway.

The boxes were there. Seven of them, stacked in two uneven columns against the far wall, the cardboard softened and faded at the edges from twelve years of sitting in a room with no purpose. He had labeled them when he moved in — KITCHEN MISC., BOOKS (2 OF 3), PHOTOS/ALBUMS, OFFICE STUFF, WINTER CLOTHES, MISC., and one that just said KELSEY, which held things from her childhood bedroom in the old house. Things she'd left behind when she went to college. Doug had packed that box himself, wrapping each item in newspaper because the items were hers and they mattered even if she didn't know she wanted them yet.

The room smelled like cardboard and dust and the particular stillness of a space that nobody uses. The carpet was the same beige as the hallway. There was an outlet on the far wall with nothing plugged into it. The window blinds were closed but June light came through anyway, angled and warm, landing across the boxes in a stripe that lit up the handwriting on the labels — his handwriting, from twelve years ago, in the black Sharpie he'd used to mark everything during the move. The same Sharpie was probably in a kitchen drawer somewhere. He still had it. He still had everything.

He stood there long enough to read each label twice. KITCHEN MISC. BOOKS (2 OF 3). PHOTOS/ALBUMS. KELSEY. They were things from another address, moved once and not moved since. They had waited twelve years without asking anything from him.

Kelsey was coming next weekend. He could show her the box with her name on it. She might take what was inside. She might not.

He pulled the door mostly shut, then left it open a few inches.

He went to the kitchen. He took out two eggs and the bread and the butter. He put a pan on the stove and turned on the burner and cracked the eggs into the pan. One of them broke wrong — the yolk catching the edge of the shell and splitting, running thin and pale before the heat could set it. He put two slices of bread in the toaster. The toaster had one setting, which was too dark. He'd owned it for nine years. He had never adjusted it and was not certain it could be adjusted. The bread went in white and came out the color of something that had stayed in the heat longer than intended.

He put the eggs on a plate. He put the toast next to the eggs. He ate at the counter, where he always ate.

The eggs were fine. A little overcooked at the edges. The toast was too dark, past the point where anyone would have chosen to stop it. He spread butter on the toast and the butter melted into the charred surface. It tasted like dark bread and salt and the bitter edge of something left in the heat a minute too long. He ate it standing there. He ate all of it.

He rinsed the plate and put it in the dish rack. He wiped down the counter with the towel that hung from the oven handle — the same towel that had hung there since he moved in, washed so many times the pattern had faded to three pale stripes.

The apartment was quiet. The evening light came through the kitchen window and landed on the counter where his hands rested. Outside, a car passed on the street. Someone's dog barked once, then stopped. Down the hall, the second bedroom door was still open.
